Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
6 min
Distinguishing the Original Ruling from Obligations in Hanafi Contract Theory
Explaining the two types of contract effects: original ruling and obligations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the original ruling (al-hukm al-asli) of a contract and the obligations (al-iltizamat) it creates, and apply this distinction to classify contract effects according to Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
The original ruling is realized immediately upon contract formation; obligations require subsequent performance.

This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Aamm introduces a foundational distinction in Hanafi contract theory: the original ruling vs. obligations. Understanding this distinction is crucial for properly categorizing the effects of any contract and for determining when rights and duties arise. The original ruling is the essential legal purpose that is realized immediately, while obligations are charges that need to be fulfilled later. Classical Hanafi texts consistently maintain this separation, as shown by the citations from al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Types of Contract Effects
From the passage, identify that every valid contract produces two distinct types of effects: an original ruling (al-hukm al-asli) and obligations (al-iltizamat). The original ruling is the essential legal purpose; obligations are specific duties that arise.
The original ruling is sometimes called the 'essential effect'.
2
Define the Original Ruling with Examples
Use the definitions and examples given: sale (transfer of ownership), gift (ownership without consideration), lease (temporary ownership of benefits), marriage (permissibility of marital enjoyment), settlement (elimination of dispute). Recognize that the original ruling is the 'subject matter' of the contract.
The original ruling is constant across contracts of the same type.
3
Define Obligations with Examples
Obligations are charges to do or refrain from an act, such as delivery of the sold item, payment of the price, warranty against defects, non‑use of a deposit. They are called 'subsidiary rulings' (ahkam tabi'a) in classical texts.
Obligations are specific to the parties and require action.
4
Identify the Key Difference: Timing of Effect
The passage states that the original ruling is realized 'immediately upon valid formation' without needing any further act. In contrast, obligations do not take effect at formation; they require implementation after the contract. This is the hallmark distinction.
The original ruling is self-executing; obligations require execution.
5
Apply the Distinction to a Sale Contract
Using a concrete sale scenario: upon valid offer and acceptance, ownership of the sold item transfers instantly to the buyer (original ruling). But the obligation to deliver the item and pay the price arise later and require performance. If the item is destroyed before delivery, who bears the risk? The original ruling (ownership) determines risk, not the obligation.
This distinction has practical implications for liability.

الفصل (۳۹) آثار العقد - الإلزام
( أ ) فأما الحكم الأصلي فهو الأثر النوعي للعقد، أو هو الغاية الحقوقية الأساسية التي شُرع ذلك العقد ليكون طريقاً مشروعاً للوصول إليها .
وذلك كانتقال الملكية بعوض في عقد البيع وبلا عوض في الهبة. وكتمليك المنافع موقتاً بعوض في الإجارة وبلا عِوَض في الإعارة. وكحل المتعة الزوجية في عقد الزواج، وسقوط أسباب النزاع القائم بين المتنازعين في عقد الصلح، وهلم جراً . . .
يتضح بذلك أن الحكم الأصلي للعقد هو الذي يعتبر موضوعاً لذلك العقد حين التعاقد، بالمعنى الذي سبق شرحه للفظ «الموضوع» في الفصل الأول (ر: ف ٤/٢٩ - ٥).
(ب) وأما الالتزامات التي يُنشئها العقد فهي كل تكليف بفعل، أو بامتناع عن فعل يجب بمقتضى العقد على أحد العاقدين لمصلحة العاقد الآخر. كتسليم المبيع، وضمان العيب وأداء الثمن، وعدم استعمال الوديعة، ونحو ذلك مما تقدمت أمثلته (١) .
(۱) (ر الدر المختار ورد المحتار، أوائل كتاب البيوع ٦/٤). وإن هذا التمييز الذي ميزناه بين الحكم الأصلي للعقد وبين الالتزامات التي ينشئها هو المستفاد من مجموع نصوص فقهائنا في مختلف العقود. فإن فقهاءنا في مطلع البحث عن كل عقد بعد تعريفه وبيان شرائطه يبينون في الغالب حكمه الأصلي، وكثيراً ما يميزون هذا الحكم الأصلي عن أحكام أخرى تنشأ بالعقد نفسه أيضاً يسمونها أحكاماً تابعة أو تبعية فقد قال صاحب «الدر المختار» شرح «تنوير الأبصار» في أوائل كتاب البيوع ما نصه: وحكمه أي حكم البيع - ثبوت الملك.
أي أن حكم عقد البيع هو ثبوت ملكية المبيع للمشتري انتقالاً عن البائع. قال ابن عابدين في حاشيته «رد المحتار» تفسيراً وتعليقاً على قول الشارح: وحكمه ثبوت الملك ما نصه: أي ثبوت الملك في البدلين لكل منهما (أي المتبايعين في بدل). وهذا حكمه الأصلي والتابع (أي حكمه التبعي) وجوب تسليم المبيع والثمن، وثبوت الشفعة لو كان المبيع عقاراً .... إلخ ...
فأفاد، كما ترى أن للبيع حكماً أصلياً واحداً هو نقل الملكية بعوض، وأحكاماً تبعية أخرى تابعة لذلك الحكم الأصلي وهذه الأحكام التبعية هي ما نسميه اليوم الالتزامات الناشئة بالعقد؛ وهي غير الحكم الأصلي. وسنرى قريباً (ف ۷/۳۹) أن هذه الالتزامات ما كان منها من ترتيب الشارع بحيث يثبت دون اشتراط من أحد العاقدين يسمى مقتضى العقد كوجوب تسليم المبيع والثمن بعد البيع.
الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
٦/٣٩ - نتائج هذا التمييز :
لهذا التمييز بين الحكم الأصلي للعقد وبين الالتزامات التي ينشئها العقد نتیجتان مهمتان:
النتيجة الأولى : أنَّ الحكم الأصلي يتحقق وينفذ بمجرد انعقاد العقد صحيحاً، فلا يحتاج إلى تنفيذ فبمجرد انعقاد البيع صحيحاً تنتقل الملكية إلى المشتري. وكذا بمجرد الانعقاد الصحيح في الإجارة والنكاح والصلح يملك المستأجر منفعة المأجور، وتحل العشرة الزوجية بين الزوجين : وتمتنع الخصومة القضائية في الحقوق التي كانت هي محل النزاع بين المتصالحين، وهلم جراً . . .
ذلك لأنَّ كلاً من هذه الأحكام هو الغاية الأساسية التي شرع العقد طريقاً موصلاً إليها، فمبجرد انعقاده صحيحاً يوصل إلى هذه الغاية.
أما الالتزام فلأنه تكليف على شخص لمصلحة آخر، وكل تكليف يحتاج إلى تنفيذ لذلك لا يتحقق مقتضاه بمجرد انعقاد العقد صحيحاً، بل لا بد لتحقق مقتضاه أن من يقوم بتنفيذه بعد العقد ذلك العاقد الملتزم به. ومن هذه النتيجة تُنصب علامة فارقة لتمييز الحكم الأصلي عن الالتزام بين مجموع آثار العقد، وهي:
أي ثبوت الملك في البدلين لكل منهما (أي المتبايعين في بدل. وهذا حكمه الأصلي والتابع (أي حكمه التبعي) وجوب تسليم المبيع والثمن، وثبوت الشفعة لو المبيع عقاراً .... إلخ ...
فأفاد، كما ترى أن للبيع حكماً أصلياً واحداً هو نقل الملكية بعوض، وأحكاماً تبعية أخرى تابعة لذلك الحكم الأصلي وهذه الأحكام التبعية هي ما نسميه اليوم الالتزامات الناشئة بالعقد؛ وهي غير الحكم الأصلي. وسنرى قريباً (ف ۷/۳۹ أن هذه الالتزامات ما كان منها من ترتيب الشارع بحيث يثبت دون اشتراط من أحد العاقدين يسمى مقتضى العقد كوجوب تسليم المبيع والثمن بعد البيع.
2 - And the various obligations that the contract creates.
Chapter (39) Effects of the Contract - Obligation
(a) As for the original ruling, it is the specific effect of the contract, or it is the basic legal purpose for which that contract was legislated to be a legitimate means of reaching it.
Such as the transfer of ownership for consideration in a sale contract and without consideration in a gift. And the temporary ownership of benefits for consideration in a lease and without consideration in a loan. And the permissibility of marital enjoyment in a marriage contract, and the elimination of the causes of existing dispute between disputants in a settlement contract, and so on...
It becomes clear from this that the original ruling of a contract is what is considered the subject matter of that contract at the time of contracting, in the sense previously explained for the word 'subject' in the first chapter (see section 4/29-5).
(b) As for the obligations that the contract creates, they are every charge to do an act or to refrain from an act that is required by the contract upon one of the contracting parties for the benefit of the other contracting party. Such as delivery of the sold item, warranty of defect, payment of the price, non-use of the deposit, and the like, examples of which have preceded (1).
(1) (See al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar, beginning of the Book of Sales 6/4). And this distinction we have made between the original ruling of the contract and the obligations it creates is derived from the entirety of our jurists' texts on various contracts. For our jurists, at the beginning of the discussion of each contract after defining it and stating its conditions, they usually explain its original ruling, and often they distinguish this original ruling from other rulings also arising from the contract itself, which they call subsidiary or dependent rulings. Thus the author of al-Durr al-Mukhtar, commentary on Tanwir al-Absar, said at the beginning of the Book of Sales: 'And its ruling, i.e., the ruling of sale, is the establishment of ownership.'
That is, the ruling of the sale contract is the establishment of ownership of the sold item for the buyer by transfer from the seller. Ibn Abidin said in his gloss Radd al-Muhtar, interpreting and commenting on the commentator's statement: 'And its ruling is the establishment of ownership' as follows: 'i.e., the establishment of ownership in the two counter-values for each of them (i.e., the two parties to the sale in the exchange). This is its original and subsidiary ruling (i.e., its dependent ruling) is the obligation to deliver the sold item and the price, and the establishment of pre-emption if the sold item is real estate... etc.'
So it conveys, as you see, that the sale has one original ruling, which is the transfer of ownership for consideration, and other subsidiary rulings that are dependent on that original ruling, and these subsidiary rulings are what we today call the obligations arising from the contract; and they are distinct from the original ruling. We will see shortly (section 7/39) that among these obligations, what is from the ordinance of the Lawgiver such that it is established without condition from either contracting party is called the requirement of the contract, such as the obligation to deliver the sold item and the price after the sale.
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh
6/39 - Results of this distinction:
This distinction between the original ruling of the contract and the obligations the contract creates has two important results:
The first result: That the original ruling is realized and executed immediately upon valid formation of the contract, so it does not require implementation. For upon valid formation of the sale, ownership transfers to the buyer. Likewise upon valid formation in lease, marriage, and settlement, the lessee owns the benefit of the leased property, marital companionship becomes permissible between the spouses, and judicial litigation is prevented in the rights that were the subject of dispute between the parties to the settlement, and so on...
This is because each of these rulings is the basic purpose for which the contract was legislated as a means to reach it, so upon its valid formation it reaches this purpose.
As for the obligation, because it is a charge upon a person for the benefit of another, and every charge requires implementation, therefore its requirement is not realized immediately upon valid formation of the contract, but rather for its requirement to be realized, the obligated contracting party must carry it out after the contract. And from this result a distinctive criterion is established to distinguish the original ruling from obligation among the totality of contract effects, namely.
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Aamm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa explains the distinction between two types of effects of a contract: the original ruling (al-hukm al-asli) and the obligations (al-iltizamat). The original ruling is the essential legal purpose for which the contract was legislated, such as transfer of ownership in a sale, or permissibility of marital enjoyment in marriage. This original effect takes effect immediately upon valid formation of the contract without any further action. For example, when a sale is validly concluded, ownership of the sold item transfers instantly to the buyer, even before delivery.

In contrast, obligations are charges (taklif) requiring one party to do or refrain from doing something for the benefit of the other, such as delivering the sold item, paying the price, or not using a deposited item. These do not take effect immediately; they require implementation by the obligated party after the contract's formation. The passage emphasizes that this distinction is consistently found in classical Hanafi fiqh texts, citing al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its commentary by Ibn Abidin. Ibn Abidin notes that the sale has one original ruling (transfer of ownership) and subsidiary rulings (obligations like delivery and payment). The passage also outlines the first result of this distinction: original rulings are self-executing upon contract formation, while obligations require subsequent performance. This is a foundational concept in Hanafi contract theory, helping to classify the various effects of a contract correctly.

Key Terms
الحكم الأصلي (al-hukm al-asli): الأثر النوعي الأساسي الذي شرع العقد لتحقيقه، كالملكية في البيع، ويتحقق فور الانعقاد الصحيح
الالتزام (al-iltizam): تكليف بفعل أو امتناع عن فعل يجب بمقتضى العقد على أحد العاقدين لمصلحة الآخر
أحكام تابعة/تبعية (ahkam tabi'a/taba'iyya): الالتزامات الناشئة عن العقد وغير الحكم الأصلي، كوجوب تسليم المبيع والثمن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in the passage, what is the original ruling of a valid sale contract?
A) The obligation to deliver the sold item.
B) The transfer of ownership for consideration.
C) The payment of the price.
D) The warranty against defects.

Key Takeaway
The original ruling of a contract is its essential legal purpose that takes effect immediately upon valid formation; obligations are subsequent duties that require performance by the parties.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
10 min
Distinguishing Original Ruling from Contractual Obligations
Distinguishing immediate effects from duties in contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the original ruling (al-hukm al-asli) of a contract and its obligations (iltizamat), and apply the two results of this distinction to sale and other contracts.

Core Idea
The original ruling of a contract is realized immediately; obligations require execution.

This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm introduces a fundamental conceptual tool for the mufti: distinguishing between what a contract automatically achieves and what it only demands. Without this, one might confuse immediate effects with deferred duties, leading to errors in fatwa regarding contract completion and burden of proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Results
Carefully read the two results (nati'jaan) listed. The first result discusses the automatic realization of the original ruling; the second concerns burden of proof when obligations are disputed.
Pay attention to the examples given for each result.
2
Examine the First Result
Note that for sale, ownership transfers automatically; for lease, the lessee automatically owns the usufruct; for marriage, marital companionship becomes permissible; for compromise, litigation is prevented. Understand that these are immediate effects of the valid contract.
The author emphasizes that the original ruling is the fundamental purpose of the contract.
3
Examine the Second Result
If the parties disagree about performance of an obligation, the obligation is considered unperformed until the obligated party proves fulfillment. For example, if the buyer denies receiving goods, the seller must prove delivery.
This shifts the burden of proof to the person alleging performance.
4
Understand the Marker
The marker to distinguish original ruling from obligation: what a person automatically understands upon hearing of the contract is the original ruling; what requires inquiry is an obligation.
Example: hearing a car sale intuitively tells you ownership transferred, but not that the price was paid.
5
Preview Inherent Obligations
The passage begins to classify obligations into two types. The first type are obligations that the contract entails without stipulation, like delivery, payment, and guarantee of defects. These are called muqtada al-'aqd.
This sets the stage for the next section on how contracts create obligations.

النتيجة الأولى : أنَّ الحكم الأصلي يتحقق وينفذ بمجرد انعقاد العقد صحيحاً، فلا يحتاج إلى تنفيذ فبمجرد انعقاد البيع صحيحاً تنتقل الملكية إلى المشتري. وكذا بمجرد الانعقاد الصحيح في الإجارة والنكاح والصلح يملك المستأجر منفعة المأجور، وتحل العشرة الزوجية بين الزوجين : وتمتنع الخصومة القضائية في الحقوق التي كانت هي محل النزاع بين المتصالحين، وهلم جراً . . .

أي ثبوت الملك في البدلين لكل منهما (أي المتبايعين في بدل. وهذا حكمه الأصلي والتابع (أي حكمه التبعي) وجوب تسليم المبيع والثمن، وثبوت الشفعة لو المبيع عقاراً .... إلخ ...

الفصل ((۳۹) آثار العقد - الإلزام
ان ما يفهم الإنسان حصوله بمجرد سماعه بوقوع العقد الصحيح هو الحكم الأصلي لذلك العقد. وما لا يفهم حصوله بالعقد، بل يحتاج العلم بحصوله إلى استفهام، فهو التزام.
فمن سمع أنَّ فلاناً قد باع سيارته يفهم أنَّ ملكيتها قد انتقلت إلى المشتري فأصبح هو مالكها، ولكنه لا يستطيع أن يفهم من وقوع البيع الصحيح أن ثمنها قد دفعه المشتري، وأن البائع قد سلّمها إليه، لأن وجوب دفع الثمن وتسليم المبيع هما التزامان يحتاجان إلى تنفيذ من قبل الملتزم، ولا يتحقق مقتضاهما بمجرد العقد كما يتحقق به انتقال ملكية المبيع.
أنَّ وكذا من سمع بزواج امرأة لا يفهم من مجرد وقوع العقد أن الزوج بأن فلاناً أودع متاعاً عند آخر لا يفهم قد دفع المهر. ومن سمع الوديع كان مدة الإيداع قائماً بالتزامه في الامتناع عن استعمال الوديعة.
النتيجة الثانية : أنه عند اختلاف المتعاقدين في تنفيذ بعض الالتزامات العقدية تُعتبر تلك الالتزامات غير منفذة حتى يثبت الملتزم المكلف بها أنه قد نفذها .
فلو أنكر المشتري قبض المبيع كان على البائع إثبات التسليم.
ولو أنكر البائع قبض الثمن كان على المشتري إثبات الدفع وهكذا يقال في كل التزام.
٧/٣٩ - كيفية إنشاء العقد للالتزامات ونظرية مقتضى العقد :
إنَّ الالتزامات التي ينشئها العقد بين الطرفين نوعان:
أ - فمنها التزامات يستلزمها العقد فيُعتبر العاقد مكلفاً بها دون حاجة إلى اشتراطها عليه صراحة من قبل العاقد الآخر. وذلك كالتزام البائع بتسليم المبيع وبضمان العيب والتزام المشتري بدفع الثمن في عقد البيع ؛ وكالتزام المؤجر بتسليم المأجور والتزام المستأجر بدفع الأجرة وبعدم التعدي على المأجور في عقد الإجارة.
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and What Follows in Jurisprudence
6/39 - Results of this Distinction:
This distinction between the original ruling of the contract and the obligations that the contract creates has two important results:
First Result: That the original ruling is realized and executed immediately upon the valid conclusion of the contract, and does not require execution. For example, immediately upon the valid conclusion of a sale, ownership transfers to the buyer. Similarly, upon valid conclusion in lease, marriage, and compromise, the lessee owns the usufruct of the leased property, marital companionship becomes permissible between the spouses, and judicial litigation is prevented regarding the rights that were the subject of dispute between the parties entering into compromise, and so forth...
This is because each of these rulings is the fundamental purpose for which the contract was legislated as a means to reach it, so upon its valid conclusion, it reaches this purpose.
As for the obligation, because it is a duty upon a person for the benefit of another, and every duty requires execution, therefore its requirement is not realized merely upon the valid conclusion of the contract. Rather, to realize its requirement, there must be someone who executes it after the contract, that is, the obligated party. From this result, a distinctive marker is established to distinguish the original ruling from the obligation among the total effects of the contract, which is:
=
That is, the establishment of ownership in the two countervalues for each of them (i.e., the two exchangers in the exchange. This is its original and subsidiary ruling (i.e., its secondary ruling): the obligation to deliver the sold item and the price, and the establishment of preemption if the sold item is real estate, etc....
So it benefits, as you see, that sale has one original ruling, which is transfer of ownership for consideration, and other secondary rulings subsidiary to that original ruling, and these secondary rulings are what we today call obligations arising from the contract; and they are other than the original ruling. We will shortly see (in 7/39) that among these obligations, what is from the arrangement of the Lawgiver such that it is established without stipulation by any of the contracting parties is called the requirement of the contract (muqtada al-'aqd), like the obligation to deliver the sold item and the price after the sale.

Section (39): Effects of the Contract - Obligation
What a person understands to have occurred merely upon hearing of the valid conclusion of the contract is the original ruling of that contract. And what is not understood to have occurred by the contract, but rather knowledge of its occurrence requires inquiry, is an obligation.
For example, if one hears that someone has sold his car, he understands that its ownership has transferred to the buyer, so he becomes its owner, but he cannot understand from the valid sale that the buyer has paid its price, and that the seller has delivered it to him, because the obligation to pay the price and deliver the sold item are two obligations that need execution by the obligated party, and their requirement is not realized merely by the contract as the transfer of ownership of the sold item is realized.
Similarly, if one hears of a woman's marriage, he does not understand from the mere occurrence of the contract that the husband has paid the dowry. And if one hears that someone deposited goods with another, he does not understand that the depositary during the deposit period was fulfilling his obligation to refrain from using the deposit.
Second Result: That when the contracting parties disagree regarding the execution of some contractual obligations, those obligations are considered unexecuted until the obligated party charged with them proves that he has executed them.
Thus, if the buyer denies receipt of the sold item, it is upon the seller to prove delivery.
And if the seller denies receipt of the price, it is upon the buyer to prove payment, and so on for every obligation.
7/39 - How the Contract Creates Obligations and the Theory of the Requirement of the Contract:
The obligations that the contract creates between the two parties are of two types:
A - Some are obligations that the contract entails, so the contracting party is considered charged with them without the need for explicit stipulation by the other contracting party. That is like the seller's obligation to deliver the sold item and to guarantee defects, and the buyer's obligation to pay the price in a sale contract; and like the lessor's obligation to deliver the leased item and the lessee's obligation to pay rent and not to misuse the leased item in a lease contract.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm discusses the distinction between the original ruling (al-hukm al-asli) of a contract and the obligations (iltizamat) arising from it, and provides two important results of this distinction. The author begins by stating the first result: the original ruling of a contract is realized immediately upon its valid conclusion, without needing further execution. For example, in a sale, ownership transfers immediately. This is because the original ruling is the very purpose for which the contract was legislated. In contrast, obligations (like delivery of the sold item or payment of price) are duties that require actual performance; they are not automatically realized by the contract alone. The passage then explains that this distinction provides a clear marker to differentiate between original rulings and obligations among the total effects of a contract. The author illustrates this with the example of a sale: the original ruling is transfer of ownership for consideration, while secondary rulings (which are obligations) include the duty to deliver, the duty to pay, and preemption rights for real estate. The passage also mentions that these obligations, when they arise from the Lawgiver's arrangement without stipulation, are called muqtada al-'aqd (the requirement of the contract).

The second result pertains to disputes: if the parties disagree about whether an obligation has been fulfilled, the obligation is considered unfulfilled until the obligated party proves its fulfillment. For instance, if the buyer denies receiving the sold item, the seller must prove delivery. This places the burden of proof on the person claiming to have performed.

Finally, the passage begins to discuss how contracts create obligations, stating that there are two types. Only the first type is described here: obligations that are inherently required by the contract, such as the seller's duty to deliver and guarantee against defects, and the buyer's duty to pay, as well as similar duties in lease contracts. The discussion is incomplete in this window.

Key Terms
الحكم الأصلي (al-hukm al-asli): The primary legal effect of a contract that is realized immediately upon its valid conclusion without need for further execution, such as transfer of ownership in a sale.
الالتزام (al-iltizam): A duty created by the contract that requires performance by the obligated party, such as delivery of the sold item or payment of price.
مقتضى العقد (muqtada al-'aqd): Obligations that arise from the Lawgiver's arrangement without needing explicit stipulation, such as the duty to deliver and pay after a sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the original ruling (al-hukm al-asli) of a sale contract?
A) Transfer of ownership for consideration
B) Obligation to deliver the sold item
C) Obligation to pay the price
D) Guarantee against defects

Key Takeaway
The original ruling of a contract is realized immediately upon valid conclusion; obligations require separate proof of performance.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

8 min
Primary Effect vs. Obligation in Contracts
Distinguishing primary effects from obligations in contracts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the primary effect (ḥukm aṣlī) of a contract and obligations that require performance, and apply the Hanafi rule that the burden of proof lies on the party claiming performance.

Core Idea
Hearing of a contract conveys ownership automatically, but not payment or delivery—those are obligations needing proof.

When we hear that a sale has taken place, we immediately assume ownership has transferred, but we do not assume the price has been paid or the goods delivered. This distinction between what the contract automatically achieves and what requires separate effort is central to Islamic contract theory. In this lesson, you will learn to identify these two categories and understand the procedural consequence: the party who claims they have performed an obligation must prove it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary effect
Consider the concrete examples given: sale, marriage, deposit. For each, determine what a reasonable person understands automatically happens upon the contract's conclusion. This is the 'original ruling' (ḥukm aṣlī).
For sale, ownership transfer is automatic; for marriage, the marriage bond itself is automatic, but not dower payment.
2
Contrast with obligations requiring inquiry
List actions that are not automatically understood, such as payment, delivery, or refraining from use. These are 'obligations' (iltizāmāt) that need explicit inquiry to confirm if they have been performed.
The author uses the word 'istifhām' (inquiry) to highlight that these are not self-evident.
3
Understand the burden of proof rule
Apply the second result: when parties dispute performance, the one claiming to have performed must prove it. Analyze the examples: if buyer denies receipt, seller must prove delivery; if seller denies payment, buyer must prove payment.
This is consistent with the general legal principle that the claimant bears the burden of proof (al-bayyina ʿalā al-muddaʿī).

Source Text

الفصل ((۳۹) آثار العقد - الإلزام

ان ما يفهم الإنسان حصوله بمجرد سماعه بوقوع العقد الصحيح هو الحكم الأصلي لذلك العقد. وما لا يفهم حصوله بالعقد، بل يحتاج العلم بحصوله إلى استفهام، فهو التزام.
فمن سمع أنَّ فلاناً قد باع سيارته يفهم أنَّ ملكيتها قد انتقلت إلى المشتري فأصبح هو مالكها، ولكنه لا يستطيع أن يفهم من وقوع البيع الصحيح أن ثمنها قد دفعه المشتري، وأن البائع قد سلّمها إليه، لأن وجوب دفع الثمن وتسليم المبيع هما التزامان يحتاجان إلى تنفيذ من قبل الملتزم، ولا يتحقق مقتضاهما بمجرد العقد كما يتحقق به انتقال ملكية المبيع.
وكذا من سمع بزواج امرأة لا يفهم من مجرد وقوع العقد أن الزوج قد دفع المهر. ومن سمع أن فلاناً أودع متاعاً عند آخر لا يفهم أن الوديع كان مدة الإيداع قائماً بالتزامه في الامتناع عن استعمال الوديعة.

النتيجة الثانية : أنه عند اختلاف المتعاقدين في تنفيذ بعض الالتزامات العقدية تُعتبر تلك الالتزامات غير منفذة حتى يثبت الملتزم المكلف بها أنه قد نفذها .
فلو أنكر المشتري قبض المبيع كان على البائع إثبات التسليم.
ولو أنكر البائع قبض الثمن كان على المشتري إثبات الدفع وهكذا يقال في كل التزام.

٧/٣٩ - كيفية إنشاء العقد للالتزامات ونظرية مقتضى العقد :
إنَّ الالتزامات التي ينشئها العقد بين الطرفين نوعان:
أ - فمنها التزامات يستلزمها العقد فيُعتبر العاقد مكلفاً بها دون حاجة إلى اشتراطها عليه صراحة من قبل العاقد الآخر. وذلك كالتزام البائع بتسليم المبيع وبضمان العيب والتزام المشتري بدفع الثمن في عقد البيع ؛ وكالتزام المؤجر بتسليم المأجور والتزام المستأجر بدفع الأجرة وبعدم التعدي على المأجور في عقد الإجارة.
فهذه الالتزامات وأمثالها في كل عقد قد نظمها الشرع آثاراً للعقد الذي ينشئها بحسب الحاجة، تحقيقاً للتوازن الواجب الرعاية بين العاقدين في الحقوق والوجائب وبذلك يستغني العاقدان عن ذكر هذا النوع من الالتزامات في كل عقد، اعتماداً على ما هو مقرّر في النصوص الشرعية . ومجموعة هذا النوع من الالتزامات في كل عقد تسمى في اصطلاح فقهاء الشريعة مقتضى العقد .
ومرجع معرفة هذا النوع وتحديد ما يدخل في مقتضى العقد هو نصوص الشريعة وآراء الفقهاء في الباب المخصوص لكل عقد من العقود المسمّاة (1) أي المعيّنة.
ب - وما سوى ذلك من الالتزامات العقدية لا يجب على أحد العاقدين إلا إذا اشترطه عليه العاقد الآخر في العقد. وذلك كما لو اشترط بائع الفرس أن يظل مستعملاً لها بعد البيع بقية الشهر، أو اشترط المشتري على البائع إيصال المبيع إلى بيته أو اشترط الواهب على الموهوب تعويضاً عن الهبة أو اشترط البائع عدم ضمانه ما يظهر في المبيع من عيوب خفية، أو اشترط المؤجر دفع الأجرة سلفاً عن مدة الإجارة كلها. فكل ذلك وأمثاله مما لم توجبه نصوص الشريعة ولا اجتهاد الفقهاء، لا يكون من مقتضى العقد ولا يترتب على العاقد إلا بالشرط .
هذا وسنرى في بحث سلطان الإرادة العقدية ما يجوز اشتراطه شرعاً من الالتزامات وما لا يجوز.
(1) العقد المسمى هو كل عقد له اسم يميزه عن سواه وقد نظم الشرع له أحكاماً تخصه، كالبيع والإجارة، إلخ. ر ف ١/٤٦، ف ٣/٤٧).
Chapter (39): Effects of the Contract - Obligation

What a person understands to occur simply by hearing of the conclusion of a valid contract is the original ruling of that contract. And what is not understood to occur by the contract, but rather knowledge of its occurrence requires inquiry, is an obligation.

Whoever hears that someone has sold his car understands that its ownership has transferred to the buyer, so he becomes its owner. But he cannot understand from the occurrence of the valid sale that its price has been paid by the buyer, and that the seller has delivered it to him, because the obligation to pay the price and deliver the sold item are two obligations that require execution by the obligated party, and their effect is not realized by the mere contract as the transfer of ownership of the sold item is realized.

Similarly, whoever hears of a woman's marriage does not understand from the mere occurrence of the contract that the husband has paid the dowry. And whoever hears that someone deposited goods with another does not understand that the depositary was, during the period of deposit, fulfilling his obligation to refrain from using the deposit.

Second result: That when the contracting parties differ regarding the performance of some contractual obligations, those obligations are considered unperformed until the obligated party charged with them proves that he has performed them.

So if the buyer denies receiving the sold item, then the seller must prove delivery.
And if the seller denies receiving the price, then the buyer must prove payment. And so on for every obligation.

7/39 - How the contract creates obligations and the theory of Mقتضى العقد:

The obligations that the contract creates between the two parties are of two types:
A) Obligations required by the contract, so the contracting party is considered obligated to them without needing to stipulate them explicitly by the other party. Such as the seller's obligation to deliver the sold item and to guarantee defects, and the buyer's obligation to pay the price in a sale contract; and the lessor's obligation to deliver the leased property and the lessee's obligation to pay the rent and not to trespass on the leased property in a lease contract.

These obligations and their like in every contract have been regulated by the Sharia as effects of the contract that creates them according to need, achieving the necessary balance between the contracting parties in rights and duties. Thus, the contracting parties are dispensed from mentioning this type of obligations in every contract, relying on what is established in the Sharia texts. The set of this type of obligations in every contract is called in the terminology of jurists of Sharia: 'Mقتضى العقد' (the requisite of the contract).
The reference for knowing this type and determining what enters into Mقتضى العقد is the Sharia texts and the opinions of jurists in the specific chapter for each named contract (1), i.e., specified contracts.
B) As for other contractual obligations, they are not obligatory upon either contracting party unless the other party stipulates them in the contract. Such as if the seller of a horse stipulates that he continue to use it after the sale for the rest of the month, or the buyer stipulates that the seller deliver the sold item to his house, or the donor stipulates compensation from the donee for the gift, or the seller stipulates that he is not liable for hidden defects that appear in the sold item, or the lessor stipulates payment of rent in advance for the entire lease period. All that and its like, which are not required by Sharia texts or juristic ijtihad, are not part of Mقتضى العقد and do not devolve upon the contracting party except by stipulation.

We will see in the discussion on the authority of contractual will what stipulations of obligations are permissible and what are not.
(1) A named contract is every contract that has a name distinguishing it from others and for which Sharia has organized specific rulings, such as sale, lease, etc. (Reference: Fiqh 1/46, Fiqh 3/47).
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage is a legal-theoretical exposition on the effects of contracts and the nature of obligations. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, opens by distinguishing between the primary effect of a contract (الحكم الأصلي) – what is automatically understood to occur upon the contract's conclusion, such as transfer of ownership in a sale – and obligations (الالتزام) which require separate inquiry because they are not automatically realized by the mere contract, such as the payment of the price or delivery of the sold item. He illustrates with examples: hearing of a sale automatically conveys ownership transfer, but not payment or delivery; hearing of a marriage does not imply dowry payment; hearing of a deposit does not imply that the depositary has refrained from using the deposit (since that obligation requires ongoing action).

The author then states a second result: in case of dispute between contracting parties regarding performance of obligations, the burden of proof lies on the party claiming performance. For example, if the buyer denies receiving the sold item, the seller must prove delivery; if the seller denies receiving the price, the buyer must prove payment. This is a procedural rule consistent with Hanafi fiqh.

Next, the author explains the theory of 'Mقتضى العقد' (the requisite of the contract). Obligations arising from a contract are of two types:

Type A: Obligations that are inherently required by the contract, so the contracting party is bound without explicit stipulation. These are regulated by Sharia to ensure balance. Examples include the seller's duty to deliver and guarantee against defects, the buyer's duty to pay, the lessor's duty to deliver the leased property, and the lessee's duty to pay rent and not misuse the property. This set of obligations is called 'Mقتضى العقد' – what the contract necessarily implies. The reference for determining these obligations is Sharia texts and juristic opinions specific to each named contract (like sale, lease).

Type B: Other obligations that are not automatically implied; they require explicit stipulation in the contract. Examples include the seller stipulating continued use of the sold horse, the buyer stipulating home delivery, the donor stipulating compensation, the seller excluding liability for hidden defects, or the lessor demanding advance rent. These are valid only if stipulated and are not part of Mقتضى العقد.

This distinction is fundamental for understanding contractual relations in Islamic law, as it clarifies which duties are automatic and which require additional agreement. The author also notes that later discussion will cover the limits of contractual stipulations.

This passage reflects the Hanafi jurisprudential approach to contract theory, emphasizing the role of Sharia in defining default obligations while permitting party autonomy through stipulations.

Key Terms
الالتزام (al-iltizām): An obligation that arises from a contract but requires performance by the obligated party; it is not automatically realized by the contract alone but needs execution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a sale contract, which of the following is considered an obligation (iltizām) rather than the primary effect?
A) Transfer of ownership from seller to buyer.
B) Buyer's duty to pay the price.
C) The contract's validity upon offer and acceptance.
D) Both A and C.

Key Takeaway
Contracts automatically produce a primary effect (like ownership transfer), but obligations like payment and delivery require proof of performance in case of dispute.

Muqtada al-ʿAqd: The Automatic Requisite of a Contract
Theory of muqtada al-ʿaqd and classification of contractual obligations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which obligations are part of muqtada al-ʿaqd (the automatic requisite) of a named contract and which require explicit stipulation, and explain the role of Sharia texts in determining muqtada al-ʿaqd.

Core Idea
Sharia automatically imposes essential obligations in each contract; everything else must be explicitly agreed.

In Islamic law, every named contract like sale or lease comes with a set of default obligations that the law imposes automatically. These are known as 'muqtada al-ʿaqd' (the requisite of the contract). They exist to maintain balance between parties without needing to be written down. Other obligations, however, only bind if explicitly stipulated. In this lesson, you will learn to classify contractual obligations into these two categories and understand how Sharia texts define the scope of muqtada al-ʿaqd for each contract type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of obligations
From the passage, list the obligations that are automatically required (Type A) and those that require stipulation (Type B). Note the examples: for sale, automatic obligations include delivery and guarantee against defects; for lease, automatic obligations include delivery of the property and non-trespass.
Type A obligations are part of muqtada al-ʿaqd; Type B are not.
2
Determine the reference for muqtada al-ʿaqd
The passage states that muqtada al-ʿaqd is determined by 'Sharia texts and opinions of jurists' specific to each named contract. Understand that you must consult the fiqh chapter on that contract to know what is automatic.
For example, the Hanafi school holds that seller's guarantee against hidden defects is part of muqtada al-ʿaqd in sale; other schools may differ.
3
Apply the classification to new examples
Given a novel contractual term, decide whether it falls into Type A (automatic because it's already in Sharia) or Type B (needs stipulation). If in doubt, research the specific contract's rulings.
A term like 'seller must deliver to buyer's door' is Type B because it goes beyond default delivery obligation.

Source Text

Chapter (39): Effects of the Contract - Obligation

What a person understands to occur simply by hearing of the conclusion of a valid contract is the original ruling of that contract. And what is not understood to occur by the contract, but rather knowledge of its occurrence requires inquiry, is an obligation.

Whoever hears that someone has sold his car understands that its ownership has transferred to the buyer, so he becomes its owner. So if the buyer denies receiving the sold item, then the seller must prove delivery.
And if the seller denies receiving the price, then the buyer must prove payment. And so on for every obligation.

7/39 - How the contract creates obligations and the theory of Mقتضى العقد:

The obligations that the contract creates between the two parties are of two types:
A) Obligations required by the contract, so the contracting party is considered obligated to them without needing to stipulate them explicitly by the other party. Such as the seller's obligation to deliver the sold item and to guarantee defects, and the buyer's obligation to pay the price in a sale contract; and the lessor's obligation to deliver the leased property and the lessee's obligation to pay the rent and not to trespass on the leased property in a lease contract.

These obligations and their like in every contract have been regulated by the Sharia as effects of the contract that creates them according to need, achieving the necessary balance between the contracting parties in rights and duties. Thus, the contracting parties are dispensed from mentioning this type of obligations in every contract, relying on what is established in the Sharia texts. The set of this type of obligations in every contract is called in the terminology of jurists of Sharia: 'Mقتضى العقد' (the requisite of the contract).
The reference for knowing this type and determining what enters into Mقتضى العقد is the Sharia texts and the opinions of jurists in the specific chapter for each named contract (1), i.e., specified contracts.
B) As for other contractual obligations, they are not obligatory upon either contracting party unless the other party stipulates them in the contract. Such as if the seller of a horse stipulates that he continue to use it after the sale for the rest of the month, or the buyer stipulates that the seller deliver the sold item to his house, or the donor stipulates compensation from the donee for the gift, or the seller stipulates that he is not liable for hidden defects that appear in the sold item, or the lessor stipulates payment of rent in advance for the entire lease period. All that and its like, which are not required by Sharia texts or juristic ijtihad, are not part of Mقتضى العقد and do not devolve upon the contracting party except by stipulation.

We will see in the discussion on the authority of contractual will what stipulations of obligations are permissible and what are not.
(1) A named contract is every contract that has a name distinguishing it from others and for which Sharia has organized specific rulings, such as sale, lease, etc. (Reference: Fiqh 1/46, Fiqh 3/47).
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage is a legal-theoretical exposition on the effects of contracts and the nature of obligations. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, opens by distinguishing between the primary effect of a contract (الحكم الأصلي) – what is automatically understood to occur upon the contract's conclusion, such as transfer of ownership in a sale – and obligations (الالتزام) which require separate inquiry because they are not automatically realized by the mere contract, such as the payment of the price or delivery of the sold item. He illustrates with examples: hearing of a sale automatically conveys ownership transfer, but not payment or delivery; hearing of a marriage does not imply dowry payment; hearing of a deposit does not imply that the depositary has refrained from using the deposit (since that obligation requires ongoing action).

The author then states a second result: in case of dispute between contracting parties regarding performance of obligations, the burden of proof lies on the party claiming performance. For example, if the buyer denies receiving the sold item, the seller must prove delivery; if the seller denies receiving the price, the buyer must prove payment. This is a procedural rule consistent with Hanafi fiqh.

Next, the author explains the theory of 'Mقتضى العقد' (the requisite of the contract). Obligations arising from a contract are of two types:

Type A: Obligations that are inherently required by the contract, so the contracting party is bound without explicit stipulation. These are regulated by Sharia to ensure balance. Examples include the seller's duty to deliver and guarantee against defects, the buyer's duty to pay, the lessor's duty to deliver the leased property, and the lessee's duty to pay rent and not misuse the property. This set of obligations is called 'Mقتضى العقد' – what the contract necessarily implies. The reference for determining these obligations is Sharia texts and juristic opinions specific to each named contract (like sale, lease).

Type B: Other obligations that are not automatically implied; they require explicit stipulation in the contract. Examples include the seller stipulating continued use of the sold horse, the buyer stipulating home delivery, the donor stipulating compensation, the seller excluding liability for hidden defects, or the lessor demanding advance rent. These are valid only if stipulated and are not part of Mقتضى العقد.

This distinction is fundamental for understanding contractual relations in Islamic law, as it clarifies which duties are automatic and which require additional agreement. The author also notes that later discussion will cover the limits of contractual stipulations.

This passage reflects the Hanafi jurisprudential approach to contract theory, emphasizing the role of Sharia in defining default obligations while permitting party autonomy through stipulations.

Key Terms
مقتضى العقد (muqtada al-ʿaqd): The set of obligations that a contract automatically imposes on the parties without need for explicit stipulation, as determined by Sharia texts and juristic consensus for each named contract.
العقد المسمى (al-ʿaqd al-musammā): A contract that has a specific name in Sharia and has been given particular rulings, such as sale (bayʿ) or lease (ijāra).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a lease contract, which of the following is NOT part of muqtada al-ʿaqd according to the passage?
A) Lessor's obligation to deliver the leased property.
B) Lessee's obligation to pay the rent.
C) Lessee's obligation not to trespass on the leased property.
D) Lessor's obligation to repaint the property every year.

Key Takeaway
Muqtada al-ʿaqd is the set of obligations Sharia automatically attaches to each named contract; everything else must be explicitly stipulated.

Muqtada al-‘Aqd and Stipulated Conditions: The Two Types of Contractual Obligations
Distinguishing implied obligations from those requiring stipulation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between obligations that are automatically required by a contract (muqtada al-‘aqd) and obligations that require explicit stipulation, and apply this classification to examples.

Core Idea
The Sharia automatically embeds certain obligations into contracts — these are muqtada al-‘aqd; everything else needs a stipulation.

This passage from Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa introduces a foundational distinction in Hanafi contract theory: some obligations are inherent to the contract (muqtada al-‘aqd) and do not need to be spelled out, while others only bind if explicitly agreed. Understanding this classification is critical for a mufti when determining whether a contractual term is already implied or is an additional stipulation that must be evaluated for validity.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two categories
Identify that the passage divides contractual obligations into those that are automatic effects of a contract (muqtada al-‘aqd) and those that only become binding if explicitly stipulated.
Automatic effects are determined by Sharia to ensure balance between parties.
2
Define muqtada al-‘aqd
Muqtada al-‘aqd is the set of obligations that the Sharia attaches to a contract without needing mention. Its source is Sharia texts and juristic opinions specific to each named contract.
Imam al-Zarqa uses a footnote to define 'named contract' (al-‘aqd al-musamma).
3
Analyze the examples
The passage gives examples of obligations that are NOT muqtada: seller's continued use, delivery to house, compensation for gift, waiver of hidden defects, advance rent. Each requires stipulation.
These examples illustrate the practical boundary between implied and stipulated terms.
4
Understand the role of named contracts
Each named contract (bay', ijara, etc.) has its own specific muqtada al-‘aqd, determined by detailed fiqh rules in the respective chapters.
General principles are applied contextually per contract type.

Thus the contracting parties are dispensed from mentioning this type of obligation in every contract, relying on what is established in legal texts. Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage is a conceptual classification of contractual obligations. It opens by stating that the Sharia has regulated certain obligations as automatic effects (athar) of a contract to maintain balance between parties. These automatic obligations are collectively called 'muqtada al-‘aqd' (the requirement of the contract). The author then clarifies that the source for determining what falls under muqtada al-‘aqd is the Sharia texts and juristic opinion for each named contract. The second category (B) covers obligations that are not automatic; they only become binding if explicitly stipulated by one party. Examples include using the sold item after sale, delivery to buyer's house, compensation for a gift, waiver of hidden defect guarantee, and advance rent payment. The author notes that these are not part of muqtada al-‘aqd. A footnote defines 'named contract' (al-‘aqd al-musamma) as a contract with a specific name and dedicated rulings, like sale or lease. The passage ends by promising a later discussion on which stipulations are valid. This classification is fundamental in Hanafi contract law for understanding default and negotiated terms.

Key Terms
مقتضى العقد (muqtada al-‘aqd): The set of obligations that the Sharia automatically attaches to a contract as its effects, without need for stipulation.
العقد المسمى (al-‘aqd al-musamma): A contract with a specific name recognized by Sharia, having its own detailed rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Sharia pre-defines certain obligations for each named contract; these are binding without mention, while all other obligations require explicit stipulation.

Bindingness of Contracts: Immediate Effect of Offer and Acceptance
Defining contractual bindingness and its immediate effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-luzum and explain that under Hanafi law, a contract becomes binding immediately upon offer and acceptance, without need for additional acts.

Core Idea
A contract binds both parties immediately upon offer and acceptance; only mutual rescission can release them.

This passage defines the fundamental concept of contractual bindingness (luzum) in Hanafi fiqh. It establishes that the contract becomes binding at the moment of offer and acceptance, requiring both parties to adhere unless they mutually agree to cancel. This principle underpins the reliability of contracts in economic life and is a cornerstone for muftis advising on contractual obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the chapter's two sections
The author divides the chapter into two parts: (1) when bindingness attaches, and (2) non-binding contracts. This passage focuses on the first.
2
Define al-luzum
Luzum means neither party can unilaterally dissolve the contract. Only iqalah (mutual cancellation) can release them. The contract is a restrictive bond.
3
Recall contract formation
A contract is formed by two decisive wills expressed through offer and acceptance (ijab wa qabul). Bindingness attaches immediately upon the linking of offer to acceptance.
4
Recognize practical consequence
Because bindingness is immediate, no party may revoke after the contract is concluded. This gives contracts their utility in transactions.
5
Note the ikhtilaf on khiyar al-majlis
The passage mentions khiyar al-majlis (option of the session) as a point of scholarly difference. The Hanafi school does not recognize it; contracts are binding at once.
This ikhtilaf will be explored in the Fatwa Under Review block.

آثار العقد - اللزوم

الأول: في بيان متى تثبت صفة اللزوم للعقد؟
الثاني : في نظرية عدم اللزوم، والعقود غير اللازمة.

متى تثبت صفة اللزوم للعقد؟

٢/٤٠ - قلنا : إن اللزوم معناه أن لا يستطيع أحد الطرفين بعد العقد التحلل من قيده ما لم يتفقا على الإقالة، فإن العقد رابطة تقيد المتعاقدين، وإن إرادة أحد الطرفين لا تستطيع هدم العقد ولا تعديله.

مزاياه في بناء الأعمال والحياة الاكتسابية . وقد رأينا فيما سبق أن العقد يتكوّن من إرادتين جازمتين مظهرهما بالتعبير المعرب عنهما، وهو إيجاب وقبول أو ما يقوم مقامهما .
ومن مقتضى ذلك أن يتم العقد وينبرم بمجرد الإيجاب والقبول أو ما يقوم مقامهما فوراً دون توقف على فعل آخر فتثبت آثار العقد، ويصبح لازماً بمجرد تمامه، فلا يكون لأحدهما أن يرجع في العقد بعد الانعقاد الحاصل من ارتباط الإيجاب بالقبول.
Chapter Forty: Effects of the Contract - Bindingness (Luzum). 1/40 - This chapter is divided into two sections: First: Explanation of when the attribute of bindingness is established for the contract? Second: The theory of non-bindingness and non-binding contracts. First Section: When does the attribute of bindingness become established for the contract? Option of the session (Khiyar al-Majlis) and differences of ijtihad concerning it. 2/40 - We said: Indeed, bindingness (luzum) means that neither party after the contract can free themselves from its restriction unless they agree on cancellation (iqalah), for the contract is a bond that ties the contracting parties, and the will of one party cannot demolish or modify the contract. Bindingness is a fundamental, necessary idea in contracts; without it, the contract would lose its most important advantage in building work and economic life. And we have seen previously that the contract consists of two decisive wills manifested by expression representing them, which is offer and acceptance (ijab wa qabul) or what takes their place. And from that requirement, it follows that the contract is completed and concluded immediately upon offer and acceptance or what takes their place, without waiting for another action; thus the effects of the contract are established, and it becomes binding immediately upon its completion, so that neither of them may revoke the contract after the conclusion resulting from linking offer to acceptance.
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on the bindingness of contracts (luzum al-‘aqd). It first outlines two main topics: (1) when bindingness attaches, and (2) non-binding contracts. The author defines luzum as the inability of either party to unilaterally dissolve the contract; only mutual agreement (iqalah) can release them. The contract is a restrictive bond. The author emphasizes that luzum is essential for contracts to serve their purpose in business and economic life. He recalls that a contract is formed by two decisive wills expressed through offer and acceptance. Consequently, the contract is perfected and binding immediately upon the completion of offer and acceptance, without needing any further act. This means neither party may retract after the contract is concluded. The mention of khiyar al-majlis (option of the session) is a prompt for later discussion on exceptions where contracts are not immediately binding, but the present passage establishes the general rule of immediate bindingness.

Key Terms
اللزوم (al-luzum): The attribute of a contract that prevents unilateral revocation; bindingness.
الإقالة (al-iqalah): Mutual rescission of a contract by both parties.
خيار المجلس (khiyar al-majlis): The option for both parties to revoke the contract during the session of contracting until they separate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Under Hanafi law, a contract becomes binding (lāzim) immediately upon the completion of offer and acceptance; neither party may unilaterally revoke it.

Khiyar al-Majlis: Hanafi Position on Contractual Bindingness
Explains the disagreement on khiyar al-majlis and Hanafi position

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi position on khiyar al-majlis to determine the bindingness of a sale contract and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using textual interpretation (ta'wil).

Core Idea
A binding contract becomes binding immediately upon offer and acceptance; no option after conclusion.

Islamic schools agree that once a contract is binding, it cannot be unilaterally rescinded. However, they differ on when bindingness begins. The Shafi'i and Hanbali schools hold that a sale contract remains revocable until the parties physically separate, based on a hadith. The Hanafi and Maliki schools maintain that bindingness is immediate, interpreting the same hadith as referring to the period before acceptance. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi reasoning and its implications for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the agreed principle
Identify the common ground: all schools agree that a binding contract cannot be rescinded unilaterally. This sets the foundation for the dispute.
2
Identify the point of disagreement
Pinpoint the exact issue: at what moment does the contract acquire bindingness (luzum)? The disagreement centers on the meaning of 'separation' in the hadith.
3
Analyze the Hanafi ta'wil of the hadith
Examine how the Hanafi school interprets 'option' and 'separation' in the hadith. The option is the period between offer and acceptance; separation means conclusion of the session by rejection or acceptance, not physical parting.
4
Apply the Hanafi position to a contemporary scenario
Consider a modern sale conducted via chat. According to the Hanafi school, once the offeree says 'I accept', the contract is immediately binding, and neither party can withdraw without mutual consent.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Assess the practical difference: in Hanafi practice, a sale is final after offer and acceptance; in Shafi'i practice, either party may withdraw until the session ends. This affects contractual security and dispute resolution.

Source Text

.
غير أن الاجتهادات الإسلامية مختلفة في الوقت الذي يكتسب العقد اللازم صفة اللزوم:
- فذهب الاجتهادان الشافعي والحنبلي إلى أنه في عقد البيع وسائر العقود اللازمة القابلة للفسخ كالإجارات والصلح عن مال بمال، لا يكتسب العقد صفة اللزوم إلا بعد أن ينقضي مجلس العقد بتفرق العاقدين بأبدانهما، أما قبل ذلك فلكل واحد من العاقدين أن يرجع عن العقد.
ومستند هذا الاجتهاد ما ورد في الحديث الثابت عن النبي أنه قال: «المتبايعان بالخيار ما لم يتفرقا . وحكمة ذلك فسح المجال للتروي والتبصر كي لا يكون العاقد مأخوذاً يبديهته. ويسمون هذا الخيار «خيار المجلس».

وأصحاب هذا الاجتهاد يؤولون ذلك الحديث الوارد في خيار المجلس تأويلاً ،آخر وينزلون الخيار والتفرق الواردين فيه على معنى يتفق مع مذهبهم ويستدلون على اللزوم الفوري في العقود اللازمة، ومنها البيع، بأدلة أخرى من الكتاب والسنة والقياس . ( ر ) كتاب الملكية ونظرية العقد» للأستاذ الشيخ محمد أبي زهرة، ف/۱۰۷).
وهذا الاجتهاد يتفق والنظر الحقوقي الحديث، وهو أقطع للخلاف بين المتبايعين وأجرى مع الحاجة العملية إلى البنات السريع في مؤاخذة المرء بإرادته الجازمة المنشئة في التصرفات المدنية، ومتفق أيضاً نظرة الشارع الإسلامي في سائر العقود الأخرى التي هي أعظم من البيع شأناً وأكبر خطراً كعقد النكاح، إذ لم يجعل في شيء منها خياراً طبيعياً بعد تمام القبول .
أنَّ
وخير تأويل نراه لذلك الحديث الوارد في خيار المجلس هو المراد بالخيار فيه هو خيار الطرفين خلال إجراء العقد في الفترة التي بين الإيجاب والقبول ؛ وأنَّ المراد بالتفرق هو انقضاء مجلس العقد سلباً أو إيجاباً : أي أن الموجب مخيَّرٌ في الاستمرار على إيجابه أو الرجوع عنه قبل القبول، كما أنَّ الطرف الآخر مخيَّرٌ في أن يقبل أو لا يقبل حتى يتفرقا، حتى ينقضي مجلس العقد، إذ يستلزم انقضاؤه تفرقهما. أي
وهذا التفرق يكون :
- إما بالإعراض أو الرفض الصريح؛ فيبطل الإيجاب ولا يصح أن يبنى عليه قبول.
- وإما بالقبول؛ فينبرم العقد، ويُلزم كل من الطرفين بمقتضاه (۱) .
* (۱) يصرح معظم ناقلي هذا الخلاف الاجتهادي في خيار المجلس بأن تأويل هذا الحديث الوارد في خيار المتبايعين عند الحنفية هو أن التفرق المذكور فيه محمول على تفرق أقوالهما بوقوع القبول وانبرام العقد. (ر ) الهداية والكفاية، أوائل البيع). لكن في ذلك شيئاً التكلف من في تأويل معنى التفرق.
فالأفضل ما بيناه هنا، فيكون غرض الحديث إنما هو بيان مدة ذلك الخيار الثابت للمتبايعين كليهما بين الايجاب والقبول وتحديدها بانقضاء المجلس.
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. This is what our jurists affirmed as a principle in contracts, from which no contract deviates except a category of contracts whose nature requires non-bindingness, called 'non-binding contracts', and except what requires an option for either contracting party by their stipulation or by legal requirement, as will be explained shortly. Islamic jurisprudence is agreed that when a contract acquires bindingness, neither party may revoke except by mutual agreement, because revoking the contract changes the legal situation established between the two parties; thus revocation depends on mutual consent, like the original contract. However, Islamic jurisprudence differs on the time at which the binding contract acquires the quality of bindingness. The Shafi'i and Hanbali schools held that in a sale contract and all other binding contracts subject to rescission, like leases and settlements for property, the contract does not acquire bindingness until the session of contract ends with the physical separation of the contracting parties; before that, each contracting party may withdraw from the contract. The basis of this view is the authentic hadith from the Prophet (PBUH) that he said: 'The two parties to a sale have the option as long as they have not separated.' The wisdom behind this is to allow room for deliberation and insight so that the contracting party is not taken by surprise. They call this option 'khiyar al-majlis' (option of the session). The Hanafi and Maliki schools held that once the binding contract is concluded, it becomes binding immediately upon completion of offer and acceptance or what takes their place in action or indication; neither party may revoke except with the consent of the other. This is the basis of judicial decisions in all Islamic countries today. The proponents of this view interpret the hadith on khiyar al-majlis differently, and they apply the option and separation mentioned in it in a sense consistent with their school, and they argue for immediate bindingness in binding contracts, including sale, with other proofs from the Quran, Sunnah, and analogy. (See: Kitab al-Milkiyya wa Nazariyyat al-'Aqd by Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra, p. 107). This view is consistent with modern legal theory, it is more decisive in resolving disputes between buyers and sellers, more in line with the practical need for quick binding when a person's decisive will is expressed in civil transactions, and also consistent with the Islamic legislator's view in other contracts that are greater in significance and risk, like marriage, in which no natural option was placed after completion of acceptance. The best interpretation we see of the hadith on khiyar al-majlis is that the option meant therein is the option of the two parties during the process of the contract in the period between offer and acceptance; and that separation meant is the end of the session of contract negatively or positively: that is, the offeror has the option to continue his offer or withdraw it before acceptance, and the other party has the option to accept or not until they separate, meaning until the session ends, because its ending entails their separation. This separation is either by express rejection or refusal, which voids the offer and no acceptance can be built upon it, or by acceptance, which concludes the contract and binds both parties by its terms. (1) * (1) Most transmitters of this jurisprudential difference regarding khiyar al-majlis state that the interpretation of this hadith on khiyar al-majlis among the Hanafis is that separation mentioned therein is carried on the separation of their statements by the occurrence of acceptance and conclusion of the contract. (See: al-Hidayah wa al-Kifayah, beginning of Buyu'). But there is some artificiality in interpreting the meaning of separation. The best is what we have explained here, so that the purpose of the hadith is to clarify the duration of that option established for both buyers between offer and acceptance and to limit it by the end of the session.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy discussion on the rule of khiyar al-majlis (option of the session) and its application across the four Sunni schools. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by stating the agreed principle that once a contract becomes binding, neither party may unilaterally rescind. The disagreement concerns when bindingness is achieved. The Shafi'i and Hanbali schools hold that bindingness occurs only after the parties physically separate, relying on the hadith 'al-bayyi'ān bil-khiyār mā lam yatafarraqā' (The two parties to a sale have the option as long as they have not separated). Thus, during the session, each may withdraw. This is called khiyar al-majlis. The Hanafi and Maliki schools, on the other hand, hold that bindingness is immediate upon completion of offer and acceptance. They interpret the hadith differently: the option (khiyar) referred to is the option either party has before acceptance occurs, i.e., the offeror may retract before acceptance, and the offeree may decline. The term 'separation' (tafarruq) is understood as the conclusion of the session by either rejection or acceptance. Al-Zarqa favors this interpretation, noting that it aligns with modern legal needs for certainty in transactions and avoids the artificiality found in the traditional Hanafi explanation that 'separation of statements' is meant. He also points out that no similar option exists in contracts of greater significance like marriage. The author's preferred view is that the hadith's purpose is to define the period of option between offer and acceptance, not after. This discussion is crucial for understanding Hanafi fatwa methodology: the Hanafi position is based on istihsan and practical necessity, but the text acknowledges the authentic hadith, thus requiring interpretive effort (ta'wil) to harmonize with school principles.

Key Terms
خيار المجلس (khiyar al-majlis): The option for either contracting party to rescind the contract during the session of contract until they physically separate, according to Shafi'i and Hanbali schools.
العقد اللازم (al-'aqd al-lazim): A contract that is binding, meaning neither party can unilaterally rescind without the other's consent.
التفرق (al-tafarruq): Separation; in the context of khiyar al-majlis, it can mean physical separation of bodies or, in Hanafi interpretation, separation of statements (i.e., conclusion by acceptance).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does a binding contract (e.g., a sale) become binding (lazim)?
A) Only after the parties physically separate from the session.
B) Immediately upon completion of offer and acceptance.
C) After a three-day option period.
D) Only if both parties agree to make it binding.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes itself by rejecting khiyar al-majlis after contract conclusion, interpreting the hadith as referring to the pre-acceptance stage, thus ensuring contractual certainty.

Khiyar al-Majlis: The Hanafi Reinterpretation
Explains the Hanafi reinterpretation of the khiyar al-majlis hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi reinterpretation of the khiyar al-majlis hadith and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the meaning of tafarruq.

Core Idea
The option of the council applies only before contract conclusion; 'separation' means the end of the council, not physical parting.

This passage dives into a key intra-Hanafi controversy: how to interpret the famous hadith 'The two parties in a sale have the option as long as they have not separated.' The author offers a refinement that aligns the hadith with the Hanafi principle of immediate binding in binding contracts. Understanding this dispute is crucial for a mufti because it directly affects the binding moment of a sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and its apparent meaning
Recall the hadith: 'البيعان بالخيار ما لم يتفرقا'. The apparent meaning is that both parties have a right to withdraw until they physically part. This is the basis for khiyar al-majlis.
The author takes this hadith as the starting point.
2
Understand the Hanafi principle of immediate binding
The Hanafi school holds that binding contracts (like sale) are immediately binding after offer and acceptance. The author argues that the hadith must be interpreted consistently with this principle.
This principle is derived from Quran, Sunnah, and qiyas.
3
Analyze the reinterpretation of 'tafarruq'
The author redefines 'tafarruq' as the end of the contract council, not physical separation. The council ends either by explicit rejection (making the offer void) or by acceptance (concluding the contract). Thus, the option only exists during the council before acceptance.
This is the key reinterpretation.
4
Compare with the alternative Hanafi reading
Another Hanafi reading takes 'tafarruq' literally as the parties physically parting, meaning the option continues after acceptance until they separate. The author criticizes this as artificial (تكلف).
This alternative is recorded in al-Hidayah and al-Kifayah.
5
Evaluate the author's preferred view and its implications for fatwa
The author's view is chosen because it is more consistent with legal theory, practical needs, and the overall scheme of Islamic contracts. For a mufti, this view means the contract is binding immediately after acceptance, which provides certainty and finality.
This affects how a mufti resolves disputes in sales.

Commentary
This passage addresses the Hanafi interpretation of the hadith on khiyar al-majlis. The author begins by noting that the Hanafi school uses additional proofs from Quran, Sunnah, and qiyas to argue for immediate binding in binding contracts like sale. The author then presents a preferred interpretation: the option of the council (khiyar al-majlis) applies only during the period between offer and acceptance, not after the contract is concluded. 'Separation' (tafarruq) is interpreted as the conclusion of the council by either rejection or acceptance. The footnote clarifies that many scholars attribute to the Hanafis a different interpretation—that tafarruq means the separation of statements by acceptance—but the author finds that interpretation artificial. The author's preferred view is that the hadith sets the duration of the option as the time until the council ends.

Key Terms
خيار المجلس (khiyar al-majlis): The option of the council: a right for both parties to withdraw from a contract before the session ends, limited to the period between offer and acceptance.
التفرق (al-tafarruq): The end of the contract council, either by explicit rejection or acceptance, not necessarily physical separation.
الإيجاب (al-ijab): The offer made by one party to initiate the contract.
القبول (al-qabul): The acceptance of the offer by the other party, which concludes the contract.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi reinterpretation redefines 'tafarruq' as the end of the contract council, limiting khiyar al-majlis to the period before contract conclusion, ensuring immediate binding.

Theory of Non-Binding Contracts: Two Causes
Outlines the two causes for non-binding contracts and their branches.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between inherent non-bindingness (due to contract nature) and acquired non-bindingness (due to external circumstances) and apply the classification to identify non-binding contracts.

Core Idea
Non-bindingness in contracts arises from either the contract's nature or external circumstances like invalidity, duress, or options.

This lesson introduces the Hanafi classification of non-binding contracts. The author explains that bindingness may be absent either as an inherent feature of certain contract types (e.g., istisna') or due to external factors that strip bindingness from normally binding contracts. Understanding this dichotomy is foundational for assessing contract revocability in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Recognize that non-bindingness has two sources: inherent (nature of contract) and acquired (external circumstances).
2
Examine the inherent category
Note that contracts like istisna' are inherently non-binding; their non-bindingness is an observed characteristic of the contract type.
The passage mentions three types but only names istisna' explicitly.
3
Examine the acquired category
Identify three cases: fasid (invalidity), ikrah (duress), and khiyarat (contractual options), which remove bindingness from otherwise binding contracts.
These are not inherent; they are external conditions that affect the contract's bindingness.
4
Map the branches
Understand that the passage prefaces two branches of study: one for inherently non-binding contracts and one for circumstances that remove bindingness.

Section Two: Theory of Non-Bindingness and Non-Binding Contracts. Non-bindingness by virtue of the nature of the contract: the three types of this section - the contract of istisna'. 2- Removal of bindingness from binding contracts and its cases: (a) case of invalidity, (b) case of duress, (c) case of contractual options. 3/40 - Bindingness in general may be absent from some contracts, so that each of the two parties or only one of them may dissolve the contract bond and rescind it by his mere will without depending on the consent of the other party. And the absence of this bindingness in the contract is sometimes absolute and sometimes restricted to specific circumstances; and it goes back to one of two causes: - Either because the nature and purpose of the contract require non-bindingness, in which case the contract is called non-binding, as non-bindingness is an observed characteristic in the type of contract. - Or due to the occurrence of general circumstances that necessitate the removal of bindingness from the binding contracts themselves, and require granting the contracting party the right to rescind them, whereby the binding contract becomes non-binding. Thus, the research in this section branches into two branches: 524

Commentary
This passage introduces the Hanafi theory of non-binding contracts. The author classifies non-bindingness into two categories: (1) contracts that are inherently non-binding due to their nature, such as istisna' (manufacturing contract), which is revocable until completion; and (2) binding contracts that become non-binding due to external factors like invalidity (fasid), duress (ikrah), or the presence of contractual options (khiyarat). The key distinction is that non-bindingness can be either an intrinsic characteristic of the contract type or an acquired status due to specific circumstances. This classification forms the basis for the subsequent detailed study of non-binding contracts.

Key Terms
اللزوم (al-luzum): The bindingness of a contract, meaning it cannot be unilaterally rescinded.
العقد غير اللازم (al-'aqd ghayr al-lazim): A contract that is not binding, allowing one or both parties to rescind it without the other's consent.
الفساد (al-fasad): Invalidity: a defect in the contract that makes it non-binding, subject to rescission.
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Duress: a condition that vitiates consent and makes a normally binding contract non-binding.
الخيارات العقدية (al-khiyarat al-'aqdiyyah): Contractual options: rights to rescind or confirm the contract within a specified period, agreed upon or by law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Non-binding contracts in Hanafi fiqh arise from either the contract's inherent nature or external circumstances such as invalidity, duress, or options.

The Binding Nature of Arbitration in Hanafi Law
Explaining the revocable and binding stages of arbitration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the binding nature of arbitration and apply it to a scenario.

Core Idea
Arbitration is revocable before the ruling; after the ruling it becomes binding.

Arbitration (tahkim) is a contract where disputing parties appoint a third person to settle their dispute. In Hanafi law, this contract is initially revocable, like an agency. Once the arbitrator issues a ruling, the contract becomes binding. This lesson clarifies the stages and the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act of tahkim
Recognize that arbitration is a contract between two disputing parties and the arbitrator.
It is not a court judgment but a private agreement.
2
State the Hanafi position on bindingness before the ruling
Each party may annul the contract and dismiss the arbitrator before the ruling.
This mirrors the revocability of a wakala (agency) contract.
3
State the position after the ruling
Once the ruling is issued, arbitration becomes binding and irrevocable.
The ruling has the effect of a court judgment.
