وعلى كل يتوقف الرجوع في الهبة على القضاء أو الرضاء، فلا تعود الهبة إلى ملك الواهب بمجرد رجوعه بل حتى يرضى الموهوب له، أو يقضي القاضي للواهب بالرجوع عند انتفاء جميع موانعه
(۱) يجب أن يلحظ أن المال الموهوب لو كان عقاراً وسجلت الهبة في السجل العقاري وفقاً للمراسم التي توجبها النظم العقارية لدينا لا يكون هذا التسجيل مانعاً للواهب من الرجوع .
غير أن الدائرة المدنية في محكمة التمييز السورية (محكمة النقض) قد ذهبت أخيراً إلى عكس ذلك، فاعتبرت تسجيل الهبة في السجل العقاري مانعاً للواهب من الرجوع بحجة أن هذا التسجيل يقوم مقام القبض اللازم شرعاً لتمام الهبة في العقار الموهوب، وأن قيود السجل العقاري تعتبر قطعية بالنظر القانوني وخاصة بمقتضى المادتين /١٣ و ۱۷ من القانون ذي الرقم/ ۱۸۸/ اللتين توجبان بقاء الحق العقاري المكتسب بالتسجيل لصاحبه وعدم قبول طعن يبطله إلا في حالات مخصوصة عينها التشريع العقاري نفسه.
ر قرار محكمة التمييز السورية الصادر في ١ جمادى الأولى / ١٣٦٨ هـ = ٢٨ شباط / ١٩٤٩ م برقم // ۱۳/ أساس مدني / ۹۹/ ، وكذا القرار الصادر قبله في ١٠ صفر ١٣٦٧ هـ = ٢٣ كانون الأول ١٩٤٧ م ، برقم / ١٧٣/ أساس/ ٢٠١). ولا يخفى أن هذا الاعتبار غير سديد، فإن الرجوع في الهبة العقارية ليس طعناً في قيود السجل العقاري، وإنما هو سبب جديد موجب لإعادة نقل الملكية إلى الواهب بمقتضى حق الرجوع الذي منحه إياه التشريع. وإن المادتين / ۱۳ و ۱۷ المستند إليهما إنما معناهما منع الادعاء ببطلان الحقوق العقارية المسجلة عن طريق الطعن في صحة اكتساب هذه الحقوق وتسجيلها، لا منع الادعاء بمقتضى الأسباب الحادثة المشروعة في المعاملات مما يوجب حدوثه إعادة نقل الملكية.
فحق الواهب في الرجوع يشبه حق المشتري بخيار الشرط، فإن من اشترى العقار واشترط لنفسه الخيار مدة معينة وسجل العقد بهذا الشرط، تنتقل إليه ملكية المبيع لأن الخيار المشروط للمشتري لا يمنع انتقال الملكية إليه (ر: المجلة ٣٠٩)، ومن أنه يحق بعد ذلك للمشتري أن يستعمل حقه في فسخ البيع العقاري المسجل ولا يعتبر ذلك مخلاً بمبدأ قطعية القيود العقارية، لأنه ليس طعناً في هذه القيود، وإنما هو استعمال حق مشروع بإعادة الملكية بمقتضى خيار الشرط المسوّغ لفسخ العقد.
فأي فرق يمكن أن يرى بين خيار أوجبه الشرط السائغ، وخيار أوجبه الشرع؟ وليس تسجيل العقد العقاري إلا من قبيل المراسم الشكلية التي أوجبها القانون، ليصبح بها العقد على العقار في قوة العقد المنفذ على المنقول، فلا يمنع هذا التسجيل أي حق
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Jurisprudence. 2 - Arbitration: It is when two disputing parties resort to a person they choose by mutual consent to decide the conflict between them, instead of a judge. He rules. Each of the two parties may annul the contract of arbitration and dismiss the arbitrator before he gives his ruling. As for after the issuance of the ruling, no, rather arbitration becomes binding (see: Majalla / 1847). 3 - Will: This is an act of unilateral will; the testator may revoke it and withdraw from it as long as he is alive. When he dies, it becomes binding within the limit of one-third of his wealth; and the right of withdrawal does not pass to his heirs. 4 - Gift: In gift there is some element of non-bindingness, for it is permissible to withdraw from it and reclaim the gifted object unless prevented by one of seven known barriers in its section. If any of these barriers to withdrawal exist, the gift becomes binding thereby, and it is not permissible for the donor to withdraw from it. Among those barriers that make the gift binding are: the donee being a spouse of the donor or one of his close relatives (mahram); likewise, the destruction of the gifted property in the hands of the donee, or its leaving his ownership by other transfer causes such as sale of ownership. In any case, withdrawal from a gift depends on a court order or consent; so the gift does not return to the donor's ownership merely by his withdrawal, but only if the donee consents, or the judge rules for the donor to withdraw when all barriers are absent. (1) It must be noted that if the gifted property is real estate and the gift has been registered in the land registry according to the procedures required by our land laws, this registration does not prevent the donor from withdrawing. However, the Civil Chamber of the Syrian Court of Cassation (the Supreme Court) has recently taken the opposite view, considering the registration of the gift in the land registry as a barrier to the donor's withdrawal, arguing that this registration stands in place of the legally required possession (qabd) for completion of the gift in real estate, and that land registry records are considered final from a legal perspective, especially according to Articles 13 and 17 of Law No. 188, which require the preservation of the real right acquired by registration for its owner and the inadmissibility of any challenge to invalidate it except in specific cases specified by the land legislation itself. See: Syrian Court of Cassation decision issued on 1 Jumada al-Ula 1368 AH = 28 February 1949 AD, No. 13/ Civil Basis /99, and also the decision issued before it on 10 Safar 1367 AH = 23 December 1947 AD, No. 173/ Basis/201). And it is obvious that this consideration is unsound, because withdrawal from a gift of real estate is not a challenge to the land registry records, but rather a new cause requiring the retransfer of ownership to the donor by virtue of the right of withdrawal granted to him by legislation. And the meaning of Articles 13 and 17 relied upon is to prevent claims of invalidity of registered real rights by challenging the validity of acquisition and registration of these rights, not to prevent claims based on new legitimate transactional causes that necessitate retransfer of ownership. For the donor's right of withdrawal resembles the buyer's right under a condition of option (khiyar al-shart); he who buys real estate and stipulates for himself an option for a specified period and registers the contract with this condition, ownership of the sold property transfers to him because the option stipulated for the buyer does not prevent the transfer of ownership (see: Majalla 309), and then the buyer has the right to exercise his right to rescind the registered real estate sale, and this is not considered a violation of the principle of finality of land records, because it is not a challenge to these records, but rather an exercise of a legitimate right to retransfer ownership by virtue of the stipulated option that justifies rescission of the contract. So what difference can be seen between an option created by a valid stipulation and an option created by the Law? And the registration of a real estate contract is merely a formal procedure required by law, so that the contract on real estate becomes equivalent to an executed contract on movables; thus, this registration does not prevent any right.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage, taken from a modern introduction to Islamic jurisprudence on the theory of contracts, discusses the binding nature (luzum) of three specific legal acts: arbitration, will, and gift. The author groups them together because each shares an initial non-binding character that becomes binding upon a subsequent event or condition. In arbitration ('tahkim'), the parties may revoke their agreement until the arbitrator issues a ruling; after that, the ruling is binding. In a will ('wasiyya'), the testator may revoke it at any time during his life, but upon death it becomes binding, though limited to one-third of the estate. For a gift ('hiba'), the donor may generally revoke and reclaim the gifted item unless barred by specific obstacles, such as close kinship (e.g., spouse, mahram relatives) or destruction of the item. Moreover, revocation of a gift requires either the donee's consent or a court order. The author then critiques a modern Syrian court ruling that treated official registration of a gift of real estate as an automatic bar to revocation. He argues that registration is merely a formal requirement and does not affect the substantive right of withdrawal granted by the Shari'a. He compares it to a buyer's right of rescission under a stipulated option (khiyar al-shart) in a sale contract: even if the sale is registered, the buyer can still rescind within the option period. Similarly, the donor's right to revoke a gift is a substantive legal right that is not nullified by registration. This passage illustrates the Hanafi position that the legal effect of acts is governed by their intrinsic nature and not by formalities, unless the formality itself is given a material effect by the law.

Key Terms
التحكيم (al-taḥkīm): A contract where two disputing parties appoint a third person to settle their dispute; initially revocable, becomes binding after the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, when does arbitration become binding?
A) Upon signing the arbitration agreement.
B) After the ruling is issued.
C) After both parties accept the ruling.
D) After the ruling is enforced.

Key Takeaway
Arbitration is non-binding until the arbitrator rules; after that, it is irrevocably binding.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

5 min
The Binding Nature of a Will in Hanafi Law
Explaining the revocability of a will during life and its binding effect after death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a will is revocable during the testator's life and binding upon death within the one-third limit.

Core Idea
A will is a unilateral act, revocable until death, then binding within one-third of the estate.

A will (wasiyya) is a disposition of property effective after death. In Hanafi law, it is considered a unilateral act (irada mufrada), meaning the testator can change or revoke it anytime while alive. Upon death, it becomes binding, but only up to one-third of the estate. The right to revoke does not pass to heirs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal nature of a will
Recognize that a will is not a contract but a unilateral act (tasarruf al-irada al-mufrada).
This explains its revocability.
2
State the period of revocability
The testator may revoke the will at any time during his life.
No consent from beneficiaries is needed.
3
State when it becomes binding
Upon death, the will becomes binding, but only within one-third of the estate.
This is a restriction to protect heirs' rights.
4
Note the non-transferability of revocation right
The heirs do not inherit the right to revoke the will after the testator's death.
Once binding, it is final.

وهو أن يلجأ طرفان مختصمان إلى شخص يختارانه برضاهما ليفصل في الخلاف الذي بينهما، بدلاً من القاضي. يحكم.
فلكل من الطرفين أن يفسخ عقد التحكيم ويعزل المحكم قبل أن يحكم.
أما بعد صدور الحكم منه فلا بل يصبح التحكيم لازماً (ر: المجلة / ١٨٤٧).
٣ - الوصية :
وهذه من تصرفات الإرادة المنفردة فيجوز للموصي فسخها والرجوع فيها ما دام حياً. فإذا مات انقلبت لازمة في حدود ثلث ماله؛ ولا ينتقل حق الرجوع إلى ورثته ..

ففي الهبة شيء من معنى عدم اللزوم، إذ يجوز الرجوع فيها واسترداد الموهوب ما لم يمنع منه أحد موانع سبعة معروفة في بابها. فإذا وجد شيء من موانع الرجوع هذه تصبح الهية معه لازمة لا يسوغ للواهب الرجوع فيها.
ومن تلك الموانع التي تصبح معها الهبة لازمة كون الموهوب له زوجاً للواهب أو من أقاربه المحارم ؛ وكذا هلاك المال الموهوب عند الموهوب له أو خروجه عن ملكه أو بغيره من الأسباب الناقلة ببيع للملكية .
وعلى كل يتوقف الرجوع في الهبة على القضاء أو الرضاء، فلا تعود الهبة إلى ملك الواهب بمجرد رجوعه بل حتى يرضى الموهوب له، أو يقضي القاضي للواهب بالرجوع عند انتفاء جميع موانعه

Commentary
This passage, taken from a modern introduction to Islamic jurisprudence on the theory of contracts, discusses the binding nature (luzum) of three specific legal acts: arbitration, will, and gift. The Gift: Revocability and Barriers, and a Modern Application
Explaining the revocability of gifts, barriers to revocation, and a modern case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi ruling on gift revocability, list the barriers to revocation, and evaluate a modern court decision regarding gift registration.

Core Idea
A gift is revocable unless specific barriers exist; registration alone does not prevent revocation.

A gift (hiba) initially allows the donor to reclaim the gifted property. However, certain barriers (like close kinship or destruction of the item) make the gift binding. The donor must obtain the donee's consent or a court order to revoke. This lesson also examines a modern Syrian court decision that incorrectly treated land registration as a barrier to revocation, and the author's critique based on Hanafi principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule of hiba
A gift is initially non-binding; the donor may revoke it unless barriers exist.
This distinguishes gift from sale.
2
List the barriers (mawani') that make a gift binding
From the passage: donee is spouse or mahram relative, destruction of property, transfer of ownership by donee.
These barriers prevent revocation.
3
Explain the procedure for revocation
Revocation requires the donee's consent or a court order; mere declaration is insufficient.
The property does not automatically revert.
4
Analyze the Syrian court decision and the author's critique
The court argued that land registration bars revocation. The author rebuts that registration is a mere formality and does not negate the substantive right to revoke.
The author compares to khiyar al-shart in sale.

ومن تلك الموانع التي تصبح معها الهبة لازمة كون الموهوب له زوجاً للواهب أو من أقاربه المحارم ؛ وكذا هلاك المال الموهوب عند الموهوب له أو خروجه عن ملكه أو بغيره من الأسباب الناقلة ببيع للملكية .
وعلى كل يتوقف الرجوع في الهبة على القضاء أو الرضاء، فلا تعود الهبة إلى ملك الواهب بمجرد رجوعه بل حتى يرضى الموهوب له، أو يقضي القاضي للواهب بالرجوع عند انتفاء جميع موانعه
(۱) يجب أن يلحظ أن المال الموهوب لو كان عقاراً وسجلت الهبة في السجل العقاري وفقاً للمراسم التي توجبها النظم العقارية لدينا لا يكون هذا التسجيل مانعاً للواهب من الرجوع .
غير أن الدائرة المدنية في محكمة التمييز السورية (محكمة النقض) قد ذهبت أخيراً إلى عكس ذلك، فاعتبرت تسجيل الهبة في السجل العقاري مانعاً للواهب من الرجوع بحجة أن هذا التسجيل يقوم مقام القبض اللازم شرعاً لتمام الهبة في العقار الموهوب، وأن قيود السجل العقاري تعتبر قطعية بالنظر القانوني وخاصة بمقتضى المادتين /١٣ و ۱۷ من القانون ذي الرقم/ ۱۸۸/ اللتين توجبان بقاء الحق العقاري المكتسب بالتسجيل لصاحبه وعدم قبول طعن يبطله إلا في حالات مخصوصة عينها التشريع العقاري نفسه.
ر قرار محكمة التمييز السورية الصادر في ١ جمادى الأولى / ١٣٦٨ هـ = ٢٨ شباط / ١٩٤٩ م برقم // ۱۳/ أساس مدني / ۹۹/ ، وكذا القرار الصادر قبله في ١٠ صفر ١٣٦٧ هـ = ٢٣ كانون الأول ١٩٤٧ م ، برقم / ١٧٣/ أساس/ ٢٠١). ولا يخفى أن هذا الاعتبار غير سديد، فإن الرجوع في الهبة العقارية ليس طعناً في قيود السجل العقاري، وإنما هو سبب جديد موجب لإعادة نقل الملكية إلى الواهب بمقتضى حق الرجوع الذي منحه إياه التشريع. وإن المادتين / ۱۳ و ۱۷ المستند إليهما إنما معناهما منع الادعاء ببطلان الحقوق العقارية المسجلة عن طريق الطعن في صحة اكتساب هذه الحقوق وتسجيلها، لا منع الادعاء بمقتضى الأسباب الحادثة المشروعة في المعاملات مما يوجب حدوثه إعادة نقل الملكية.
فحق الواهب في الرجوع يشبه حق المشتري بخيار الشرط، فإن من اشترى العقار واشترط لنفسه الخيار مدة معينة وسجل العقد بهذا الشرط، تنتقل إليه ملكية المبيع لأن الخيار المشروط للمشتري لا يمنع انتقال الملكية إليه (ر: المجلة ٣٠٩)، ومن أنه يحق بعد ذلك للمشتري أن يستعمل حقه في فسخ البيع العقاري المسجل ولا يعتبر ذلك مخلاً بمبدأ قطعية القيود العقارية، لأنه ليس طعناً في هذه القيود، وإنما هو استعمال حق مشروع بإعادة الملكية بمقتضى خيار الشرط المسوّغ لفسخ العقد.
فأي فرق يمكن أن يرى بين خيار أوجبه الشرط السائغ، وخيار أوجبه الشرع؟ وليس تسجيل العقد العقاري إلا من قبيل المراسم الشكلية التي أوجبها القانون، ليصبح بها العقد على العقار في قوة العقد المنفذ على المنقول، فلا يمنع هذا التسجيل أي حق
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Jurisprudence. 2 - Arbitration: It is when two disputing parties resort to a person they choose by mutual consent to decide the conflict between them, instead of a judge. He rules. Each of the two parties may annul the contract of arbitration and dismiss the arbitrator before he gives his ruling. As for after the issuance of the ruling, no, rather arbitration becomes binding (see: Majalla / 1847). 3 - Will: This is an act of unilateral will; the testator may revoke it and withdraw from it as long as he is alive. When he dies, it becomes binding within the limit of one-third of his wealth; and the right of withdrawal does not pass to his heirs. 4 - Gift: In gift there is some element of non-bindingness, for it is permissible to withdraw from it and reclaim the gifted object unless prevented by one of seven known barriers in its section. If any of these barriers to withdrawal exist, the gift becomes binding thereby, and it is not permissible for the donor to withdraw from it. Among those barriers that make the gift binding are: the donee being a spouse of the donor or one of his close relatives (mahram); likewise, the destruction of the gifted property in the hands of the donee, or its leaving his ownership by other transfer causes such as sale of ownership. In any case, withdrawal from a gift depends on a court order or consent; so the gift does not return to the donor's ownership merely by his withdrawal, but only if the donee consents, or the judge rules for the donor to withdraw when all barriers are absent. (1) It must be noted that if the gifted property is real estate and the gift has been registered in the land registry according to the procedures required by our land laws, this registration does not prevent the donor from withdrawing. However, the Civil Chamber of the Syrian Court of Cassation (the Supreme Court) has recently taken the opposite view, considering the registration of the gift in the land registry as a barrier to the donor's withdrawal, arguing that this registration stands in place of the legally required possession (qabd) for completion of the gift in real estate, and that land registry records are considered final from a legal perspective, especially according to Articles 13 and 17 of Law No. 188, which require the preservation of the real right acquired by registration for its owner and the inadmissibility of any challenge to invalidate it except in specific cases specified by the land legislation itself. See: Syrian Court of Cassation decision issued on 1 Jumada al-Ula 1368 AH = 28 February 1949 AD, No. 13/ Civil Basis /99, and also the decision issued before it on 10 Safar 1367 AH = 23 December 1947 AD, No. 173/ Basis/201). And it is obvious that this consideration is unsound, because withdrawal from a gift of real estate is not a challenge to the land registry records, but rather a new cause requiring the retransfer of ownership to the donor by virtue of the right of withdrawal granted to him by legislation. And the meaning of Articles 13 and 17 relied upon is to prevent claims of invalidity of registered real rights by challenging the validity of acquisition and registration of these rights, not to prevent claims based on new legitimate transactional causes that necessitate retransfer of ownership. For the donor's right of withdrawal resembles the buyer's right under a condition of option (khiyar al-shart); he who buys real estate and stipulates for himself an option for a specified period and registers the contract with this condition, ownership of the sold property transfers to him because the option stipulated for the buyer does not prevent the transfer of ownership (see: Majalla 309), and then the buyer has the right to exercise his right to rescind the registered real estate sale, and this is not considered a violation of the principle of finality of land records, because it is not a challenge to these records, but rather an exercise of a legitimate right to retransfer ownership by virtue of the stipulated option that justifies rescission of the contract. So what difference can be seen between an option created by a valid stipulation and an option created by the Law? And the registration of a real estate contract is merely a formal procedure required by law, so that the contract on real estate becomes equivalent to an executed contract on movables; thus, this registration does not prevent any right.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage, taken from a modern introduction to Islamic jurisprudence on the theory of contracts, discusses the binding nature (luzum) of three specific legal acts: arbitration, will, and gift. The author groups them together because each shares an initial non-binding character that becomes binding upon a subsequent event or condition. In arbitration ('tahkim'), the parties may revoke their agreement until the arbitrator issues a ruling; after that, the ruling is binding. In a will ('wasiyya'), the testator may revoke it at any time during his life, but upon death it becomes binding, though limited to one-third of the estate. For a gift ('hiba'), the donor may generally revoke and reclaim the gifted item unless barred by specific obstacles, such as close kinship (e.g., spouse, mahram relatives) or destruction of the item. Moreover, revocation of a gift requires either the donee's consent or a court order. The author then critiques a modern Syrian court ruling that treated official registration of a gift of real estate as an automatic bar to revocation. He argues that registration is merely a formal requirement and does not affect the substantive right of withdrawal granted by the Shari'a. He compares it to a buyer's right of rescission under a stipulated option (khiyar al-shart) in a sale contract: even if the sale is registered, the buyer can still rescind within the option period. Similarly, the donor's right to revoke a gift is a substantive legal right that is not nullified by registration. This passage illustrates the Hanafi position that the legal effect of acts is governed by their intrinsic nature and not by formalities, unless the formality itself is given a material effect by the law.

Key Terms
الهبة (al-hiba): A gift contract that is initially non-binding; the donor may revoke unless specific barriers exist (e.g., close kinship, destruction of property). Revocation requires consent or court order.
الرجوع (al-rujūʿ): The act of withdrawing or revoking a contract or disposition, such as revoking a gift or rescinding a sale under an option.
اللزوم (al-luzūm): The quality of being binding and irrevocable. Contradistinguished from 'adam al-luzum (non-bindingness).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, which of the following would NOT prevent a donor from revoking a gift?
A) The donee is the donor's child.
B) The donee has sold the gifted property.
C) The gifted property has been destroyed.
D) The gift has been registered in the land registry.

Key Takeaway
A gift is revocable unless a barrier exists; registration is not a barrier.

Land Registration Does Not Bar Gift Revocation: A Hanafi Critique
Critical refinement of a court decision on gift revocation and land registration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that formal land registration does not affect the donor's substantive right to revoke a gift, and critique the contrary position using qiyas with buyer's options.

Core Idea
Registration is a formality; it does not create a new impediment to revocation.

In this passage, the author addresses a modern legal issue: does official land registration of a gift prevent the donor from revoking it under Hanafi law? The Syrian Court of Cassation ruled that it does, equating registration with possession. The author rejects this, arguing that registration is merely procedural and does not affect the substantive Sharia right to revoke. He draws an analogy with the buyer's options under a contract, which also survive registration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the opposing ruling
The Syrian Court of Cassation held that land registration of a gift prevents the donor from revoking, considering registration equivalent to possession and definitive.
This ruling is a modern application of Hanafi principles with civil law influence.
2
Understand the Hanafi rule on gift revocation
Hanafi law allows a donor to revoke a gift unilaterally unless impediments like close kinship or compensation exist.
Possession (qabd) is required for the gift to be complete, but once complete, revocation is still possible.
3
Examine the court's reasoning
The court relied on Articles 13 and 17 of Land Law No. 188, which make registered rights definitive and bar challenges.
The court equated registration with Sharia possession.
4
Analyze the author's critique
Revocation is not a challenge to registration but a new cause of transfer (sabab hadith). The laws only bar challenges to the initial acquisition, not subsequent lawful causes.
The author distinguishes between attacking the original registration and seeking a new transfer based on a fresh right.
5
Apply qiyas with buyer's options
The donor's right resembles a buyer's khiyar al-shart or khiyar al-'ayb: these options also survive registration and allow rescission.
If registration does not bar the buyer's option, it should not bar the donor's option, which is similar in nature.
6
Conclude that registration does not bar revocation
Since possession itself does not prevent revocation, registration (which is merely a formality) also does not prevent it.
The author dismisses the court's analogy between registration and possession as inconsistent.

Chapter (40) Effects of the Contract - Bindingness. The gift returns to the ownership of the donor merely by his revocation, but only if the donee consents, or the judge decrees the donor's revocation when all impediments are absent. (1) It must be noted that if the gifted property is real estate and the gift is registered in the land registry according to the formalities required by our land regulations, this registration does not prevent the donor from revoking. However, the Civil Chamber of the Syrian Court of Cassation recently took the opposite view, considering land registration of the gift a barrier to revocation on the grounds that this registration substitutes for the possession required by Sharia for completion of a gift of real estate, and that land registry entries are considered definitive in legal terms, especially under Articles 13 and 17 of Law No. 188, which require the preservation of registered real estate rights for their owner and reject challenges that would nullify them except in specific cases specified by the land legislation itself. Reference: Decision of the Syrian Court of Cassation issued on 1 Jumada al-Awwal 1368 AH = 28 February 1949 AD, No. 13/Civil/99, and the earlier decision on 10 Safar 1367 AH = 23 December 1947 AD, No. 173/201. It is obvious that this consideration is unsound, because revocation of a real estate gift is not a challenge to the land registry entries, but rather a new cause requiring the transfer of ownership back to the donor by virtue of the right of revocation granted to him by legislation. The two articles 13 and 17 relied upon only mean preventing claims of invalidity of registered real estate rights by challenging the correctness of acquiring them, not preventing claims based on new lawful reasons arising in transactions that necessitate retransfer of ownership. The donor's right to revoke resembles the buyer's right under a stipulated option, for if one buys property and stipulates an option for a fixed period and registers the contract with this condition, ownership of the sold item transfers to him because the option stipulated for the buyer does not prevent transfer of ownership (see: Majalla 309), and thereafter the buyer has the right to use his right to rescind the registered real estate sale, and this is not considered a violation of the definitiveness of land records, because it is not a challenge to the records but rather an exercise of a legitimate right to return ownership under the option that justifies rescission of the contract. What difference can be seen between an option caused by a valid condition and an option caused by the Sharia? And registration of a real estate contract is only one of the formalities required by law, making the contract on real estate as enforceable as a contract on movables, so this registration does not prevent any right. = 529. Later valid after the contract, and therefore the sale of real estate and its registration also do not prevent the buyer from using the option of defect or option of description, for example, in rescinding it under the applicable civil provisions if a pre-existing defect appears or a description contrary to what was stipulated appears, and no waiver of the option exists, so reflect. And if the registration of a real estate gift is considered equivalent to possession of the gifted property - as the Court of Cassation reasoned in its two decisions discussed - then possession of the gifted property does not prevent revocation of the gift, so likewise does registration.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the legal effect of land registration on the Hanafi ruling that a donor may revoke a gift unless certain impediments exist. The author argues that registration is merely a formality and does not prevent the right to revoke, in contrast to a decision by the Syrian Court of Cassation. He compares the donor's right to the buyer's right under a stipulated option (khiyar al-shart), which also does not prevent transfer of ownership but allows rescission. The key point is that revocation is not a challenge to the registration but a new cause for retransfer. The footnote on page 501 continues this argument, emphasizing that the same principle applies to options for defect and description, and concludes that if registration substitutes for possession, it should not bar revocation since possession itself does not bar it.

Key Terms
الرجوع (al-ruju'): The donor's right to take back the gifted property unilaterally.
تسجيل العقار (tasjil al-'iqar): Official registration of a property in the land registry, considered a formality.
القبض (al-qabd): Taking possession, which is required for completion of a gift of real estate in Sharia.
السبب الحادث (al-sabab al-hadith): A new legal cause that arises after the original transaction, creating a fresh right (e.g., revocation decision).
المراسم الشكلية (al-marasim al-shakliyya): Formal procedures required by civil law for enforceability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Land registration is a civil formality that does not create a new impediment to the donor's right to revoke a gift under Hanafi law.

Removing Bindingness from Binding Contracts: Three Causes
Rasm rule explaining causes that remove bindingness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three causes that remove bindingness from binding contracts and apply the Hanafi tarjih on duress (ikrāh) as suspended (mawquf) in a fatwa.

Core Idea
Binding contracts lose bindingness through invalidity, duress, or option rights.

Most contracts in Hanafi fiqh are binding (lāzim) and cannot be dissolved without mutual consent. However, Shari'ah provides exceptions to protect contractual integrity and the will of the parties. In this lesson, we examine three general causes that can strip a contract of its bindingness: fasād (invalidity), ikrāh (duress), and khiyārāt (options). Understanding these causes is essential for a mufti to correctly analyze contract disputes and issue fatwas on rescission rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three causes
Read the passage and list the three situations that remove bindingness: (a) fasād, (b) ikrāh, (c) khiyārāt. Note that each cause has distinct rulings regarding who may rescind and when.
These are not the only causes, but the primary general ones.
2
Analyze the ikrāh dispute
Focus on the ikrāh section. The passage notes an ijtihādī difference: Is a contract made under duress fāsid (invalid) or mawqūf (suspended)? Identify the two positions and the mufta bihi view.
Abū Ḥanīfa held fasād; the Ṣāḥibayn held mawqūf. The relied-upon view is mawqūf.
3
Distinguish types of khiyārāt
The passage divides options into two categories: those established by agreement (khiyār al-sharṭ, khiyār al-naqd) and those imposed by law (due to defects of consent). Also note that some options require judicial verification (e.g., khiyār al-ʿayb).
Contrast with options that directly remove bindingness, like khiyār al-sharṭ.

الفصل (٤٠) آثار العقد - اللزوم
وعندئذ يكون للمستصنع حق الفسخ بمقتضى خيار فوات الوصف المشروط لا بمقتضى عدم اللزوم في عقد الاستصناع .
الشعبة الثانية :
سلب اللزوم عن العقود اللازمة
٦/٤٠ ـ إن ما سوى هذه العقود الاحد عشر السالفة البيان هي عقود لازمة لزوماً مطلقاً في جميع الأحوال العادية، فلا يسوغ لأحد عاقديها أن يتحلل منها إلا برضى الآخر، كما بينا .
غير أن هناك أسباباً عامة يمكن أن تعتري العقود اللازمة فتجعل للعاقدين أو لأحدهما حق الفسخ، فإذا وجد شيء من تلك الأسباب في عقد لازم يسلب منه صفة اللزوم ما دام السبب الموجب لحق الفسخ قائماً . وتلك الأسباب قد تقدَّمت الإشارة إلى بعضها في بعض المناسبات، وهي ترجع إما إلى ضرورة احترام الشرائط الشرعية لصحة العقد، وإما إلى ضرورة احترام إرادة العاقد وصيانة حقوقه الناشئة العقد. من وعن هذا كان سَلْبُ صفة اللزوم عن العقود اللازمة إنما يتجلى في
الحالات الثلاث التالية :
٧/٤٠ - ( أ ) حالة الفساد :
فإن العقد الفاسد يحق لكل من الطرفين وللحاكم أيضاً فسخه ما لم يمنع من الفسخ مانع شرعي؛ فهو ما دام قابلاً للفسخ، عقد مسلوب اللزوم (ر : البدائع (٣٠٠/٥).
وسنرى أيضاً معنى الفساد وحكمه في نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية (ر:
ف / ١/١٥٦ و ٥/٥٦).
٤٠ / ٨ - (ب) حالة الإكراه :
فإن العقد الواقع بالإكراه يحق لعاقده المستكره عليه فسخه أو إمضاؤه بعد زوال الإكراه، كما تقدَّم في موضعه، فهو في النتيجة عقد مسلوب اللزوم في حقه، سواء اعتبر بالإكراه فاسداً أو موقوفاً، على الخلاف الاجتهادي السابق الذكر في صفته ( ر )) ف ٤/٣٤ الحاشية).
٩/٤٠ - (ج) حالة الخيارات العقدية :
فإنَّ ثبوت الخيار لأحد العاقدين في فسخ العقد معناه سلب صفة اللزوم من العقد في أصله . ولذا اعتبر الفقهاء من شرائط لزوم العقود اللازمة أن لا يكون فيها خيار لأحد العاقدين (المجلة/ ١١٤ - ١١٦).
وهذه الخيارات منها ما يتوقف ثبوته على اتفاق إرادة العاقدين عليه، فلا يثبت إلا باشتراطهما فيعتبر من قبيل الشروط العقدية، وذلك خيار الشرط وخيار النقد اللذان سلف إيضاح معناهما في بحث القياس (ر: ف ١١/ ثامناً).
ومنها ما يثبت بإيجاب الشرع لمصلحة أحد العاقدين صيانة لحقوقه فيكون نتيجة لبعض عيوب الرضا المتقدّمة في بحث شوائب الإرادة، من خلابة، أو تدليس عيب، أو تجزئة صفقة إلخ ... (ر: ف ١/٣٥ - ١/٣٧).
فيحق لصاحب الخيار فسخ العقد أو إمضاؤه، ما لم يوجد مسقط للخيار أو مانع شرعي يمنع ممارسته (۱) ، مما هو معروف في مواطنه ومباحثه من الأبواب الفقهية، وفي المواد / ٣۰۰ - ٣٦٠ من المجلة.
هذا، وإنَّ بعض تلك الخيارات العقدية يتوقف على القضاء، فلا يثبت لصاحبه ولا يقبل منه الفسخ قبل قضاء القاضي كما في خيار العيب لأنه من المواطن التي تحتاج إلى خبرة وتحقيق قضائي في وجود العيب المسوغ للفسخ .
(۱) كما لو وجد في المبيع عيب قديم وحدث فيه عند المشتري عيب حادث، فيمتنع عليه فسخ البيع بسبب العيب القديم.
Chapter (40): Effects of the Contract - Bindingness
And accordingly, the person who ordered the manufacture has the right to rescind by virtue of the option of failure of the stipulated description, not by virtue of lack of bindingness in the contract of manufacturing.
Second Section: Removing Bindingness from Binding Contracts
6/40 - Indeed, other than these eleven contracts previously explained are binding contracts absolutely in all normal circumstances, so it is not permissible for either of the two contracting parties to dissolve them except with the consent of the other, as we have explained.
However, there are general causes that can affect binding contracts and give the parties or one of them the right to rescind. So if any of those causes exists in a binding contract, it removes the characteristic of bindingness from it as long as the cause giving the right to rescind persists. Those causes have been previously referred to in some contexts, and they return either to the necessity of respecting the shar'i conditions for the validity of the contract, or to the necessity of respecting the will of the contracting party and safeguarding his rights arising from the contract. From this, the removal of the characteristic of bindingness from binding contracts is manifested in the following three situations:
7/40 - (a) The case of invalidity (fasād):
For the invalid contract (al-ʿaqd al-fāsid), each of the two parties and also the ruler (ḥākim) has the right to rescind it as long as no shar'i impediment prevents rescission; so as long as it is subject to rescission, it is a contract stripped of bindingness (see: al-Badāʾiʿ 5/300).
And we will also see the meaning of invalidity and its ruling in the theory of shar'i confirmations (see: F/1/156 and 5/56).
8/40 - (b) The case of duress (ikrāh):
For the contract made under duress, the coerced party has the right to rescind it or ratify it after the duress ceases, as previously mentioned in its place. So in the result, it is a contract stripped of bindingness with respect to him, whether it is considered due to duress as invalid (fāsid) or suspended (mawqūf), according to the ijtihādī disagreement previously mentioned regarding its description (see: F/4/34 footnote).
9/40 - (c) The case of contractual options (khiyārāt ʿaqdiyyah):
For the establishment of an option for one of the two contracting parties to rescind the contract means stripping the characteristic of bindingness from the contract in its origin. Therefore, the jurists considered among the conditions for the bindingness of binding contracts that there should be no option for either contracting party (Majalla articles 114-116).
Among these options, some depend for their establishment on the agreement of the wills of the two contracting parties, so they are not established except by their stipulation, and thus they are considered a type of contractual condition. These are the option of stipulation (khiyār al-sharṭ) and the option of payment (khiyār al-naqd), whose meanings have already been clarified in the discussion of analogy (see: F/11/eighth).
And among them are those that are established by the requirement of the Law for the benefit of one of the contracting parties to safeguard his rights, so they are a result of some defects of consent previously mentioned in the discussion of defects of will, such as deception (khilābah), fraudulent misrepresentation of a defect (tadlīs ʿayb), splitting a transaction (tajziʾat ṣafqah), etc. (see: F/1/35-1/37).
So the holder of the option has the right to rescind the contract or ratify it, as long as there is no ground that extinguishes the option or a shar'i impediment preventing its exercise (1), as is well-known in its places and discussions from the fiqh chapters, and in Majalla articles 300-360.
Moreover, some of those contractual options depend on the judiciary, so they are not established for their holder nor is rescission accepted from him before a judge's judgment, as in the option of defect (khiyār al-ʿayb), because it is among the matters that require judicial expertise and verification of the existence of the defect justifying rescission.
(1) As if there is an old defect in the sold item and a new defect occurs with the buyer, then he is prevented from rescinding the sale due to the old defect.
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage is a rasm rule from al-Zarqā's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhī al-ʿĀmm, discussing the removal of bindingness (luzūm) from contracts that are normally binding. The author first clarifies that the eleven contracts previously listed (such as gift, loan, etc.) are not binding, while all other contracts are binding absolutely in normal circumstances. However, bindingness can be removed in three situations: (a) invalidity (fasād) – a contract that is defective (fāsid) can be rescinded by either party or the judge, as long as no legal impediment prevents rescission; (b) duress (ikrāh) – a contract made under duress gives the coerced party the right to rescind or ratify after duress ends, and there is a scholarly disagreement whether such a contract is considered fāsid or mawqūf (suspended); (c) contractual options (khiyārāt) – the existence of an option inherently removes bindingness, and options are either stipulated by the parties (like khiyār al-sharṭ and khiyār al-naqd) or imposed by law due to defects in consent (like deception or fraud). Some options require judicial verification, such as khiyār al-ʿayb (option for defect). The author also notes exceptions, e.g., if a new defect occurs after the old one, the buyer cannot rescind for the old defect.

Key Terms
عقد لازم (ʿaqd lāzim): A contract that is binding on both parties, cannot be dissolved without mutual consent or a legal cause.
فساد (fasād): A defect in a contract that makes it valid in essence but missing a condition, rendering it subject to rescission by either party before execution.
إكراه (ikrāh): Duress or coercion that overcomes a person's will, giving the coerced party the right to rescind or ratify the contract after duress ceases.
خيار (khiyār): An option or right to rescind or affirm a contract, either by agreement or by law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which of the following is NOT a cause that removes bindingness from a binding contract?
A) Invalidity (fasād)
B) Duress (ikrāh)
C) Mutual agreement to rescind
D) Contractual options (khiyārāt)

Key Takeaway
Bindingness is not absolute; it can be removed by three causes: fasād, ikrāh, and khiyārāt, each with its own rules.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Retroactive Rescission (Faskh Mustanid) in Hanafi Contracts
Explains retroactive rescission and lists contractual options.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of retroactive rescission (faskh mustanid) in Hanafi law, identify the three causes that produce it (defect, duress, option), and distinguish it from non-retroactive rescission.

Core Idea
Rescission due to defect, duress, or option cancels the contract from its start; all other rescissions only affect the future.

In Hanafi contract theory, not all rescissions are equal. Some have a powerful retroactive effect, treating the contract as if it never existed. This lesson explores the concept of 'retroactive rescission' (faskh mustanid) as explained by Mustafa al-Zarqa, and its implications for the bindingness (luzum) of contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
Recognize that bindingness is removed due to fasad (defect), ikrah (duress), or khiyar (option). Note that rescission in these cases does not require a court decree.
2
Distinguish retroactive from non-retroactive
Understand that in these cases rescission is 'mustanid'—it reaches back to nullify the contract ab initio, unlike rescission in non-binding contracts which is 'muqtasir'—only future effect.
3
Apply to examples
See how this applies to khiyar al-shart (option of condition) and khiyar tafarrur al-safqah (option of separation of the transaction), where the party exercises the option without court involvement.
4
Recognize the breadth of options
The passage notes over twenty options arising from defects in consent, showing the scope of retroactive rescission in Hanafi fiqh, including khiyar al-kammiyyah (option of quantity) and khiyar kashf al-hal (option of disclosure).

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه

/٣ - ٣٦٠

وفصلت أحكامها بفصول خاصة بمناسبة البيوع في المواد / ۳۰۰ وبمناسبة الإجارة في المواد / ٤٩٧ - ٥٢١ ، وبمناسبات أخرى متفرقة كالقسمة وغيرها.

والفقه القانوني الحديث يتجه إلى توسيع نطاق فسخ العقود اللازمة في الحالات الطارئة، ولا سيما حالة إخلال أحد العاقدين بتنفيذ التزاماته مواعيدها، مما سَلَف بيانه. وهذا يتفق مع روح الفقه الإسلامي.
Chapter (40) Effects of the Contract - Bindingness. Some of them do not require judicial decree, rather the holder of an option, after its cause is realized, may exercise his option to rescind the contract, and his rescission is effective before a decree is issued, as in the case of the option of condition and the option of separation of the transaction. 10/4 - It must be noted in these three cases in which bindingness is removed from binding contracts due to defect, duress, or option, that rescission of the contract in them is retroactive (mustanidan) not limited (muqtasiran), meaning it has a retroactive effect that extends to the past, making the contract as if it had never been concluded originally. This is contrary to the ruling of rescission due to lack of bindingness in non-binding contracts as previously mentioned (see paragraph 7/40). 11/40 - These contractual options that arise from situations that taint the will of the contracting party with a defect of consent and strip bindingness from the binding contract are numerous, not limited to what was previously mentioned in the discussion of defects of consent. Jurists have counted more than twenty options, including the option of quantity and the option of disclosure of the situation, and others (1). Their detailed rulings are found in their appropriate places in fiqh books (see: al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar at the beginning of the chapter on option of condition 4/45-46; al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir by Ibn Nujaym with the commentary of al-Hamawi, beginning of rulings on rescissions, vol. 2, art., pp. 194-195). The Majalla covered seven basic options in its research: the options of condition, description, cash payment, specification, sight, defect, and fraud by deception. (1) Option of quantity: as when someone buys something for the money in his pocket or bag without specifying its amount; the seller has the option when he knows its amount. Option of disclosure of the situation: as in a sale by a measure or a stone weight whose amount is not known in customary standard units; the buyer has the option when he knows the capacity of the measure and the weight of the stone. (See: Radd al-Muhtar 4/22 and (27)). Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh /3 - 360. Their rulings were detailed in special chapters in the context of sales in articles /300, and in the context of leasing in articles /497-521, and in other scattered contexts such as division and others. Modern legal jurisprudence tends to expand the scope of rescission of binding contracts in emergency situations, especially the case of one contractor breaching his obligations on their dates, as previously explained. This aligns with the spirit of Islamic jurisprudence.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm discusses the effects of contracts, focusing on the concept of bindingness (luzum). The author first notes that in certain options, the contract can be rescinded without a court decree, as with the option of condition (khiyar al-shart) and the option of separation of the transaction (khiyar tafarrur al-safqah). Then section 10/40 explains the key legal point: in three cases—defect (fasad), duress (ikrah), and options (khiyar)—the rescission is 'retroactive' (mustanid), meaning it cancels the contract from its inception, as if it never existed. This differs from rescission in non-binding contracts (like those that are permissible/ja'iz), where rescission only operates for the future. Section 11/40 lists the broad category of contractual options that arise from defects in consent, noting that jurists have identified over twenty types, such as khiyar al-kammiyyah (option of quantity) and khiyar kashf al-hal (option of disclosure). The author refers to standard Hanafi references (al-Durr al-Mukhtar, Radd al-Muhtar) and the Majalla, which codified seven basic options. The passage concludes with a remark on modern legal trends aligning with Islamic law in expanding rescission rights. This commentary follows the author's order, clarifying the distinction between retroactive and non-retroactive rescission, which is fundamental in Hanafi contract theory.

Key Terms
فسخ مستند (faskh mustanid): Rescission that has a retroactive effect, cancelling the contract from its inception as if it never existed.
خيار (khiyar): A contractual option that allows a party to rescind or confirm a contract after its conclusion, based on a defect in consent or a stipulated condition.
لزوم (luzum): The legal bindingness of a contract, meaning neither party can unilaterally rescind without cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The contract is terminated only from the time of rescission.
B) The contract is considered void ab initio, as if it never existed.
C) The contract is suspended until a court decree.
D) The contract remains valid but unenforceable.

Key Takeaway
Retroactive rescission (faskh mustanid) nullifies the contract from its inception in cases of defect, duress, and option; this is key to Hanafi contract theory.

Principle of Party Autonomy in Contracts
Introduction to the principle and its four freedoms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four freedoms of contractual party autonomy and apply them to evaluate the validity of a novel contract in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Contractual autonomy is the bedrock of Hanafi contract law, bounded only by explicit prohibitions.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa introduces the principle of party autonomy, a cornerstone of Hanafi contract theory. You'll learn how the Shari'ah grants contracting parties wide freedom to create and shape agreements, limited only by clear prohibitions. The passage also outlines four specific freedoms that comprise this principle, which will help you understand the flexibility inherent in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the context
Understand that contracts have general effects and that named contracts have predefined rulings. This sets the stage for why party autonomy is important.
Named contracts are those like sale and ijarah, but they are not exhaustive.
2
Define the principle
Define 'سلطان الإرادة العقدية' as the freedom of the contractor's will in forming contracts and determining their effects, within Shari'ah limits.
The principle is a legal maxim in Hanafi usul.
3
Identify the four freedoms
List the four aspects: (1) freedom to contract, (2) freedom from formalities, (3) freedom to create new contract types, and (4) freedom to set terms (implied).
The fourth freedom is implied; the passage mentions three explicitly but refers to four aspects.
4
Connect to Shari'ah limits
Note the condition 'must not contradict Shari'ah' – this restricts freedom to avoid riba, gharar, maysir, etc.
This is the shart in the legal definition.
5
Apply to a novel contract
Use the principle to assess a modern contract not found in classical texts. If it avoids prohibitions, it is valid under party autonomy.
This shows the mufti's role in extending fiqh to new transactions.

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه / ٣ - ٣٦٠ وفصلت أحكامها بفصول خاصة بمناسبة البيوع في المواد / ۳۰۰ وبمناسبة الإجارة في المواد / ٤٩٧ - ٥٢١ ، وبمناسبات أخرى متفرقة كالقسمة وغيرها. والفقه القانوني الحديث يتجه إلى توسيع نطاق فسخ العقود اللازمة في الحالات الطارئة، ولا سيما حالة إخلال أحد العاقدين بتنفيذ التزاماته مواعيدها، مما سَلَف بيانه. وهذا يتفق مع روح الفقه الإسلامي. ٥٣٦ : : 

الفصل الحادي والأربعون سلطان الإرادة العقدية : نظرة عامة ١/٤١ - تمهيد: تجلى لنا من المباحث السابقة أنَّ للعقود آثاراً عامة كما أن لكل منها موضوعاً وأثراً خاصين يتميز بهما عن سواه. ورأينا أيضاً أنَّ من العقود عقوداً مسماة تولى التشريع تنظيم أحكامها وآثارها بين العاقدين بحيث تكون تلك الآثار نتائج أصلية ينتجها العقد دون أن يحتاج العاقدان إلى اشتراطها وتعدادها. إن مبدأ سلطان الإرادة العقدية موضوعه حرية إرادة العاقد في أصل العقد ونتائجه، وحدود تلك الحرية، أي مدى اعتبارها في نظر الشرع. ٢/٤١ - النواحي الأربع لحرية التعاقد : فهذا المبدأ يتعلق بأربع حريات في النواحي العقدية الأربع التالية : ۱ - حرية العاقد في أصل التعاقد مع غيره . ۲ - وحريته في إنشاء العقود والالتزامات بمجرد التراضي، دون تقييده بقيود شكلية ومراسم مخصوصة بحيث لا يعتبر تعاقده إذا لم تتحقق صورها. ٣ - وحريته في إنشاء ما يشاء من أنواع العقود في حدود حقوقه الشخصية مهما كان موضوعها دون تقيد بأنواع العقود المسماة التي أقرها التشريع، ونظم لكل منها أحكاماً خاصة. ۵۳۷
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. Its rulings were detailed in special chapters on the occasion of sales in articles 300, and on the occasion of ijarah in articles 497-521, and on other various occasions such as partition and others. Modern legal fiqh tends to expand the scope of rescission of binding contracts in emergency cases, especially in the case of one of the contracting parties failing to fulfill his obligations on time, as previously explained. This is consistent with the spirit of Islamic fiqh. 

Chapter Forty-One: Contractual Party Autonomy: An Overview
1/41 - Introduction: It has become clear to us from the previous discussions that contracts have general effects, just as each of them has a specific subject and effect that distinguishes it from others. We also saw that among the contracts are named contracts whose rulings and effects between the contracting parties have been regulated by legislation, such that those effects are original results produced by the contract without the need for the contractors to stipulate and enumerate them. The principle of contractual party autonomy deals with the freedom of the contractor's will regarding the contract itself and its consequences, and the limits of that freedom, i.e., the extent of its recognition in the eyes of the Shari'ah. 
2/41 - The Four Aspects of Freedom of Contract: This principle relates to four freedoms in the following four contractual aspects:
1 - The contractor's freedom in the very act of contracting with another.
2 - His freedom to create contracts and obligations by mere mutual consent, without being restricted by formalities and specific rituals such that his contract is not considered if its forms are not fulfilled.
3 - His freedom to create whatever types of contracts he wishes within the limits of his personal rights, regardless of their subject matter, without being restricted to the named contracts that legislation has approved and for which it has organized specific rulings.
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage introduces the principle of party autonomy in contractual matters, a foundational concept in legal theory. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, sets the stage by noting that contracts have both general effects and specific subjects. He emphasizes that certain named contracts (عقود مسماة) have their effects predefined by law, meaning parties do not need to stipulate every detail. The core idea is the freedom of will in both entering into contracts and determining their outcomes, subject to Shari'ah limits. The passage then enumerates four distinct freedoms: (1) freedom to contract or not, (2) freedom from excessive formalities, recognizing mere consent as sufficient, (3) freedom to create novel contracts beyond named types, and (4) impliedly freedom regarding terms (though the fourth is not explicitly stated in this portion). This aligns with the Hanafi emphasis on consent and flexibility, contrasting with systems that require strict formalities or limit contract types. The mention of modern legal trends aligning with Islamic fiqh shows the relevance of these principles.

Key Terms
سلطان الإرادة العقدية (sultan al-irada al-'aqdiyya): The principle that contracting parties have freedom of will in forming contracts and determining their effects, within Shari'ah limits.
عقود مسماة ('uqud musamma): Named contracts whose rulings are pre-determined by Shari'ah, such as sale, ijarah, etc.
حرية التعاقد (hurriyat al-ta'aqud): Freedom of contract, encompassing the four aspects listed: freedom to contract, freedom from formalities, freedom to choose contract types, and freedom to set terms.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many freedoms does the principle of contractual party autonomy encompass?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Party autonomy in Hanafi fiqh grants wide freedom to create and shape contracts, limited only by explicit Shari'ah prohibitions.

The Principle of Contractual Will: Four Freedoms and Islamic Limits
Introduces the principle of contractual will and its four freedoms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four freedoms of contractual will according to Islamic law and explain how public interest limits these freedoms.

Core Idea
Islamic law liberates contractual will but subjects it to public interest, balancing freedom and responsibility.

In this lesson, we explore the foundational principle of contractual will in Islamic jurisprudence. Derived from the works of Mustafa al-Zarqa, the passage explains that contracts are built on the free will of parties, but this freedom operates within four dimensions and is bounded by the Shari'ah's concern for public welfare. Understanding this principle is crucial for any mufti evaluating contractual validity.

Guided Path
1
Define the principle
Explain what is meant by 'authority of contractual will' (سلطان الإرادة العقدية) and its scope in Islamic law.
It concerns freedom of will in forming contracts and determining effects, within Shari'ah limits.
2
Identify the four freedoms
List and understand each freedom: freedom to contract, from formalities, to create new contracts, and to modify effects of nominate contracts.
These four areas cover the entire contractual process.
3
Recognize the Islamic contribution
How Islam liberated the will from pre-Islamic coercive practices, making mutual consent (tarāḍī) the foundation.
Even in marriage, a woman's consent is required.
4
Understand the limits
Where the law overrides will for public interest: compulsory contracts for debt, monopoly, expropriation, etc.
These are exceptions, not contradictions to the principle.
5
Note the ikhtilaf
Recognize that juristic opinions (especially Hanafi vs. others) differ on the precise boundaries of contractual freedom.
This lesson sets the stage for future detailed comparisons.

ورأينا أيضاً أنَّ من العقود عقوداً مسماة تولى التشريع تنظيم أحكامها وآثارها بين العاقدين بحيث تكون تلك الآثار نتائج أصلية ينتجها العقد دون أن يحتاج العاقدان إلى اشتراطها وتعدادها.
إن مبدأ سلطان الإرادة العقدية موضوعه حرية إرادة العاقد في أصل العقد ونتائجه، وحدود تلك الحرية، أي مدى اعتبارها في نظر الشرع.

Chapter Forty-One: The Authority of Contractual Will: A General Overview
1/41 - Introduction:
From the previous discussions, it became clear to us that contracts have general effects, and also that each contract has a specific subject and effect distinguishing it from others. We also saw that among contracts there are nominate contracts that the law has undertaken to regulate their rulings and effects between the contracting parties, such that those effects are original results produced by the contract without the parties needing to stipulate or enumerate them. The principle of the authority of contractual will is concerned with the freedom of the will of the contracting party in the original contract and its results, and the limits of that freedom, i.e., the extent to which it is considered in the view of the Lawgiver.

2/41 - The Four Aspects of Freedom of Contract:
This principle relates to four freedoms in the following four contractual areas:
1 - The freedom of the party to originally contract with another.
2 - His freedom to create contracts and obligations merely by mutual consent, without being restricted by formalities and specific ceremonies such that his contract would not be considered if their forms are not fulfilled.
3 - His freedom to create whatever types of contracts he wishes within the limits of his personal rights, whatever their subject matter, without being restricted to the nominate contracts approved by the lawgiver, each of which has specific rulings.
4 - His freedom to determine the effects of a nominate contract and modify its original results between the two contracting parties by stipulating whatever conditions they wish according to their mutual will.
These are the basic areas to which the principle of the authority of contractual will relates.

3/41 - The contractual will has passed through stages in legal history during which it had no authority; rather, it was paralyzed and crippled, not looking to the free mutual consent that Islamic law made the constitution of contracting, and recognized for the parties' will a respected authority in everything that does not violate the public interest. Although juristic opinions differ on its limits, as we will see.
A - Regarding the first aspect, Islamic legislation came to eliminate all elements of contractual coercion and liberated the freedom of will in forming the contract. The Quran and Sunnah made a person's consent a constitutional basis for every contract that will bind him, even in the marriage of women, whom people used to think that the will in marrying them was not theirs but from the rights of the family heads. Islam decided that an adult girl, even if a virgin, is the one with choice and will in her marriage and her husband.
Islamic law did not approve a contract that binds a person to an obligation he has not explicitly consented to, except what the rules of justice and public interest require, such as what the governing authority does in the name of justice to establish right, like selling the assets of a debtor who procrastinates in paying his debt, selling monopolized goods against monopolizers when their monopoly harms the public, and expropriation for public interests.
Such compulsory contracts are necessary supports for just legislation, and the purpose of establishing a government in a state; modern positive laws that adopt the absolute principle of contractual will have also approved them.
B - As for the second aspect, concerning formalities, they were not before
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage is a general overview of the principle of 'the authority of contractual will' (سلطان الإرادة العقدية) in Islamic law. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that this principle governs the freedom of the contracting parties' will in forming contracts and determining their effects, and its limits according to the Shari'ah.

First, he distinguishes contracts that have specific effects regulated by law (nominate contracts) and those that leave more freedom. The principle of contractual will encompasses four freedoms: (1) freedom to enter into a contract, (2) freedom from formality—contracts are formed by mere consent without prescribed ceremonies, (3) freedom to create new types of contracts beyond the nominate ones, and (4) freedom to modify the effects of nominate contracts by stipulating conditions.

Al-Zarqa then notes that Islamic law liberated the contractual will from prior restrictions, making free consent the foundation. However, he emphasizes that this freedom is not absolute; it is limited by public interest. For example, compulsory contracts may be imposed by the state for justice and public good, such as selling a debtor's assets or expropriation. This shows that Islamic law balances individual freedom with social welfare.

Finally, he mentions that juristic opinions (ijtihad) differ on the exact limits of this freedom, which will be discussed later. The passage sets the stage for a detailed exploration of how the Hanafi school and others apply this principle.

Key Terms
سلطان الإرادة العقدية (sulṭān al-irādat al-'aqdiyya): The authority or sovereignty of the will of the contracting parties in forming contracts and determining their effects, within the limits set by Islamic law.
العقود المسماة (al-'uqūd al-musammāt): Nominate contracts that have specific names and regulations in Islamic law, such as sale, lease, marriage, etc., where many effects are automatically applied without stipulation.
التراضي (al-tarāḍī): Mutual consent; the free agreement of both parties without coercion, which is the foundation of a valid contract in Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the four freedoms related to the authority of contractual will?
A) Freedom to enter into a contract.
B) Freedom from formality (contract by mere consent).
C) Freedom to choose the governing law of the contract.
D) Freedom to create new types of contracts beyond nominate ones.

Key Takeaway
The authority of contractual will in Islamic law encompasses four freedoms but is limited by public interest, as codified in the Hanafi school's approach to contract.

The Principle of Contractual Will in Islamic Law: Three Aspects
بيان النواحي الثلاث التي أطلقت فيها الشريعة حرية الإرادة العقدية

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three aspects of the principle of contractual will and explain how Islamic law differs from previous legal systems in each aspect.

Core Idea
Islamic law liberated contracts: free consent, no formalities, and freedom to create new types.

This lesson explores the revolutionary principle of contractual will in Islamic law. Unlike earlier legal traditions, Islamic law grants broad freedom to contracting parties, limited only by public interest. We will examine the three key areas where this freedom operates: freedom from coercion, freedom from formalities, and freedom to create new contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three aspects
The passage lists three fundamental aspects of contractual will: (a) freedom from coercion in forming contracts, (b) abolition of formalities (contracts are consensual), and (c) freedom to create new contracts beyond named ones.
The author calls these 'النواحي الأساسية' (fundamental aspects).
2
Analyze the Islamic position on each aspect
For each aspect, note the contrast with pre-Islamic systems and the limited exceptions allowed. For coercion: eliminated except for public interest (e.g., sale of debtor's assets). For formalities: abolished except for marriage (requires witnesses). For new contracts: allowed and later categorized by jurists.
The exceptions are justified by justice and public utility.
3
Understand the historical significance
Islamic law was unique in its time for being purely consensual, a fact highlighted by the author and supported by modern scholars like al-Sanhoury. This was a departure from Roman and other ancient laws.
The passage states this was 'unprecedented in the history of legislation'.
4
Apply to modern fatwa practice
A mufti must recognize the principle of contractual will when evaluating new contracts or conditions, ensuring they do not violate public interest.
The Hanafi school is particularly flexible in financial contracts.

ج - وأما الناحية الثالثة وهي حرية العاقدين في إنشاء عقود تخرج عن نطاق العقود المسماة التي قررها التشريع في موضوعها والتزاماتها، فسنرى في أول بحث تصنيف العقود أنَّ الفقه الإسلامي أقر نشوء عقود جديدة، ثم ما لبث الفقهاء أن أسموها فانتظمت في سلك العقود المسماة، وقررت لها أحكام خاصة .

Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Jurisprudence. 4 - And his freedom to determine the effects of the named contract and modify its original consequences between the two contracting parties by stipulating whatever conditions they wish according to their will. These are the fundamental aspects to which the principle of the authority of contractual will applies. 3/41 - And contractual will has passed through stages in the history of legislation where it had no authority, but was shackled and paralyzed, with no regard to free mutual consent, which Islamic law made the constitution of contracting and established for it a respected authority for the will of the contractors in everything that does not harm the public interest. Although juristic efforts differ in its limits as we will see. A - As for the first aspect, Islamic legislation came to eliminate all elements of contractual coercion, and liberated the freedom of will in forming the contract. The Qur'an and Sunnah made a person's consent a constitutional basis in every contract that will bind him, even in the marriage of women, who people used to think and know that the will in their marriage was not theirs but from the rights of the family heads. So Islam established that the adult woman, even if a virgin, is the one who has choice and will in her marriage and her husband. Islamic law did not approve a contract that binds a person to an obligation he has not clearly consented to, except what is required by the rules of justice and the interest of the community, which the ruling authority exercises in the name of justice to establish rights, such as selling the assets of a debtor who procrastinates in paying his debt, selling monopolized goods against their monopolizers when their monopoly harms the community, and expropriation for public utilities. Such compulsory contracts are necessary supports for just legislation, and are the purpose of establishing a government in a state; modern positive laws that adopted the absolute principle of contractual will have approved them. B - As for the second aspect, namely formalities, before Islam there was no nation with a fixed legislative history that knew that mere mutual consent generates a binding contract and obligations without passing through various forms and types of formalities, whether in Roman law or others, until Islam came and removed the shackles and restrictions from the necks of contracts, and made mere mutual consent the generator of the contract in whatever way it appeared between the contractors, and excepted only one contract which it subjected to the formality of announcement by witnessing, namely the marriage contract as previously mentioned (see: f 6/27 footnote). All modern positive laws that have recently adopted the principle of contractual will in the East and West have passed through long stages of various formal restrictions in creating contracts, except Islamic law, which established the rules of contract as a new creation liberated from all formal restrictions that were imposed by customs and habits in the pre-Islamic Arabian Jahiliyyah and the laws of surrounding nations; and this was among the strange and unprecedented phenomena in the history of legislation. This unique feature of Islamic law is indebted to one important factor: its religious source. C - As for the third aspect, which is the freedom of the contractors to create contracts that go beyond the scope of the named contracts that legislation has established in their subject matter and obligations, we will see at the beginning of the discussion on classification of contracts that Islamic jurisprudence approved the emergence of new contracts, then soon the jurists gave them names and they became organized within the group of named contracts, and specific rulings were determined for them. (1) This is what the eminent scholar al-Sanhoury opines in explaining this strange feature of Islamic law, and it is correct. (See: Theory of Contract by al-Sanhoury, section 92, p. 94 footnote 1). And Professor Abd al-Razzaq al-Sanhoury also said in his discussion of contracting by correspondence: 'The contract in Islamic jurisprudence is built on the agreement of the two wills without reservation; it is consensual to a degree that goes beyond the laws that adopt the consensuality of the contract.' (See: Theory of Contract, section 303).
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on the theory of contracts in Islamic jurisprudence. It explains the principle of 'the authority of contractual will' (سلطان الإرادة العقدية) and its three fundamental aspects. The author begins by noting that contractual will has gone through historical stages where it was fettered, until Islamic law liberated it, making free consent the constitutional basis of contracting, limited only by public interest. Then he details three aspects: (1) Elimination of coercion: The Qur'an and Sunnah made consent essential, even for women's marriage, overturning pre-Islamic customs. Only limited compulsory contracts are allowed for justice and public necessity (e.g., sale of debtor's assets, anti-monopoly measures, expropriation). (2) Abolition of formalities: Unlike previous legal systems (Roman, etc.), Islam made contracts purely consensual, requiring no specific formalities except for marriage, which requires witnesses. This was revolutionary in legal history. (3) Freedom to create new contracts: Islamic law allowed the emergence of unnamed contracts, which jurists later classified and regulated, showing flexibility beyond the named contracts. The author cites al-Sanhoury to support that Islamic law is more consensual than modern laws.

Key Terms
سلطان الإرادة العقدية (sulṭān al-irādah al-ʿaqdiyyah): مبدأ أن لإرادة المتعاقدين سلطاناً في إنشاء العقود وتحديد آثارها، مقيداً بالمصلحة العامة
الإكراه العقدي (al-ikrāh al-ʿaqdī): إجبار الشخص على التعاقد دون رضاه، وهو باطل في الشريعة إلا لضرورة عامة
مراسم شكلية (marāsim shakliyyah): إجراءات صورية أو كتابية أو شهادية يلزم بها العقد من أجل انعقاده
عقود مسماة (ʿuqūd musammāh): العقود التي نص عليها الفقهاء وبينوا أحكامها مثل البيع والإجارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
ما هي الناحية التي استثنى فيها الشرع الإسلامي قاعدة الرضائية وأوجب شكلية الإشهاد؟
A) بيع أموال المدين المماطل
B) عقد النكاح
C) الاستملاك للمصالح العامة
D) العقود الجديدة غير المسماة

Key Takeaway
Islamic law grants broad contractual freedom, limited only by public interest, and is more consensual than many modern laws.

سلطان الإرادة العقدية في الفقه الإسلامي
Explaining the principle of contractual will and its four aspects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the principle of contractual will in Islamic law, identify its four aspects, and apply the Quranic evidence to support the consent-based nature of contracts.

Core Idea
Islamic law freed contracts from formalities, making mutual consent alone sufficient, except for marriage.

This lesson explores the foundational principle of contractual will in Islamic law. You will see how Islamic law uniquely liberated contracts from pre-Islamic formalities, relying on mutual consent as the core. We will examine four aspects of this principle and the Quranic evidence that supports it.

Guided Path
1
Understand the historical context
Compare Islamic law's consent-based approach with pre-Islamic and modern positive laws that imposed formalities.
2
Identify the four aspects of contractual will
List: (1) freedom to contract, (2) consent-based nature, (3) freedom to create new contract types, (4) freedom to modify effects through conditions.
These are the four dimensions where the parties' will operates.
3
Analyze the Quranic evidence
Examine verse 4:29 'trade by mutual consent' as the primary dalil for consent-based contracts.
Apply Hanafi usul: the general command implies permissibility and validity.
4
Recognize the exception
Note that marriage requires witnesses (ishhad) as a formality, unlike other contracts.
This is the only contract with a form requirement.
5
Appreciate the flexibility
Understand that Islamic law does not restrict contract types; anything not explicitly prohibited is permissible.
This allows for modern contracts like franchise or insurance.
