الفصل (٤١) سلطان الإرادة العقدية - نظرة عامة
جميع تلك الإسلام أمة ذات تاريخ تشريعي ثابت تعرف أن مجرد التراضي يولد عقداً ملزماً والتزامات دون أن تمر بصور وأنواع مختلفة من المراسيم الشكلية سواء في ذلك التشريع الروماني وغيره حتى جاء الإسلام فألقى الأغلال والقيود عن أعناق العقود، وجعل مجرد التراضي مولداً للعقد بأي طريق كان ظهوره بين العاقدين، ولم يستثن إلا عقداً واحداً أخضعه لشكلية الإعلان بالإشهاد عليه وهو عقد النكاح كما تقدم (ر: ف ٦/٢٧ الحاشية).
وجميع الشرائع الوضعية الحديثة التي أقرت أخيراً مبدأ سلطان الإرادة العقدية في الشرق والغرب قد مرَّت أصولها بمراحل طويلة من القيود الشكلية المختلفة في إنشاء العقود، إلا الشريعة الإسلامية فإنها أنشأت قواعد العقد إنشاءاً جديداً محرّراً من جميع القيود الشكلية التي كانت تفرضها الأعراف والعادات في جاهلية العرب، وشرائع الأمم المحيطة بها؛ وقد كان من الظواهر الغريبة غير المعهودة في تاريخ التشريع. وهذه المزيَّة التي تفرد بها الشرع الإسلامي مدينة لعامل واحد هام هو المصدر الديني فيه.
وأما الناحية الثالثة وهي حرية العاقدين في إنشاء عقود تخرج عن نطاق العقود المسماة التي قررها التشريع في موضوعها والتزاماتها، فسنرى في أول بحث تصنيف العقود أنَّ الفقه الإسلامي أقر نشوء عقود جديدة، ثم ما لبث الفقهاء أن أسموها فانتظمت في سلك العقود المسماة، وقررت لها أحكام خاصة.
وليس في الشرع الإسلامي ما يدل على أي حصر لأنواع العقود وتقييد الناس بها. فكل موضوع لم يمنعه الشرع بالنص الصريح ولا تقتضي القواعد والأصول الشرعية منعه يجوز أن يتعاقد عليه الناس ويلزمون فيه بعقودهم؛ وحينئذ يخضع التعاقد فيه للقواعد والشرائط العامة في العقود، أهلية العاقد، وقابلية المحل إلخ.
وأما الناحية الرابعة وهي مدى سلطان إرادة العاقدين على تحديد آثار العقد المسمى وتعديل أحكامه الأصلية في حقوقهما الخاصة بينهما بشروط يتفقان عليها في التعاقد، أي إنشاء ما يشاءان من التزامات وقيود في العقد بإرادتهما، فهي أهم نواحي سلطان الإرادة في نظر الحقوق الحديثة. وموقف الفقه الإسلامي في هذه الناحية يختلف سعة وضيقاً باختلاف المذاهب الاجتهادية فيه لكن أوسع مذاهبه يتفق مع ما استقرت عليه النظريات القانونية في التشريع الحقوقي الحديث.
وسنعرض في الفصول التالية مبدأ سلطان الإرادة العقدية في الفقه الإسلامي ثم نعرض في الفصل / ٤٤/ خلاصة عن الموضوع في الفقه الأجنبي.
سلطان الإرادة العقدية في نصوص الشريعة:
٤/٤١ - إن نصوص الكتاب والسنة التي هي أصل الاجتهادات الفقهية في الشريعة الإسلامية قد بلغت في شأن سلطان الإرادة العقدية غاية العموم والمرونة.
ويمكن تصنيف هذه النصوص، بحسب موضوعها، في النواحي الأربع المتقدمة من سلطان الإرادة:
٥/٤١ - (أ) و (ب) في حرية التعاقد وفي رضائيته المطلقة:
قد جاء في ذلك قول الله عز وجل: ﴿لَا تَأْكُلُوا أَمْوَالَكُم بَيْنَكُم بِالْبَاطِلِ إِلَّا أَن تَكُونَ تِجَارَةٌ عَن تَرَاضٍ مِنكُمْ﴾ (سورة النساء ٢٩/٤).
Chapter (41) The Authority of Contractual Will – An Overview.
All of that [shows that] Islam is a nation with a fixed legislative history, knowing that mere mutual consent generates a binding contract and obligations without passing through various types of formal decrees – both in Roman legislation and others – until Islam came and cast off the fetters and restrictions from the necks of contracts, making mere consent a generator of the contract by any means of expression between the contracting parties, excepting only one contract which it subjected to the formality of public announcement by witnesses, namely the marriage contract as previously mentioned (cf. footnote 6/27).
And all modern positive laws that have finally adopted the principle of the authority of contractual will in the East and West have passed through long stages of various formal restrictions in creating contracts, except for Islamic law, which established the rules of contract anew, liberated from all formal restrictions that were imposed by the customs and traditions of pre-Islamic Arabs and the laws of surrounding nations; and this was among the strange and unprecedented phenomena in legislative history. This unique characteristic of Islamic law is indebted to one important factor: its religious source.
As for the third aspect – the freedom of the contracting parties to create contracts outside the scope of nominate contracts that the law has prescribed in their subject matter and obligations – we will see at the beginning of the discussion on classification of contracts that Islamic jurisprudence approved the emergence of new contracts, then the jurists soon named them, so they were organized among the nominate contracts, and specific rulings were prescribed for them.
There is nothing in Islamic law that indicates any restriction on the types of contracts or binding people to them. So every subject that the law has not prohibited by explicit text, and that the legal rules and principles do not require to be prohibited, may be contracted upon by people and they are bound by their contracts; then the contract is subject to the general rules and conditions of contracts: capacity of the contracting party, suitability of the subject matter, etc.
As for the fourth aspect – the extent of the contracting parties' will in determining the effects of a nominate contract and modifying its original rulings in their private rights through conditions they agree upon in the contract, i.e., creating whatever obligations and restrictions they wish in the contract by their will – this is the most important aspect of the authority of will in the view of modern law. The position of Islamic jurisprudence in this aspect varies in breadth and narrowness according to the independent schools of thought therein, but the broadest of its schools agrees with what legal theories have settled upon in modern legal legislation.
We will present in the following chapters the principle of the authority of contractual will in Islamic jurisprudence, then we will present in Chapter 44 a summary of the subject in foreign jurisprudence.
The Authority of Contractual Will in the Texts of the Sharia:
4/41 – Indeed, the texts of the Book and the Sunnah, which are the source of jurisprudential ijtihad in Islamic law, have reached the utmost generality and flexibility concerning the authority of contractual will.
These texts can be classified, according to their subject, into the four preceding aspects of the authority of will:
5/41 – (a) and (b) On freedom of contract and its absolute consent-based nature:
Allah Almighty said: {Do not consume one another's wealth unjustly unless it is a trade by mutual consent from you} (Surah an-Nisa 4:29).
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy exposition on the principle of 'authority of contractual will' (سلطان الإرادة العقدية) in Islamic jurisprudence. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa (a Hanafi scholar), explains that Islamic law uniquely freed contracts from formalities, making mutual consent (taradi) the core element for a binding contract, except for marriage which requires witnesses. He contrasts this with pre-Islamic and modern positive laws that historically imposed various formalities. The passage then outlines four aspects of contractual will: (1) freedom to contract, (2) consent-based nature, (3) freedom to create new contract types beyond nominate ones, and (4) freedom to modify the effects of nominate contracts through conditions. The author emphasizes that Islamic law does not restrict contract types; anything not prohibited by explicit text or legal principles is permissible. This shows the flexibility and comprehensiveness of Islamic contract law. The Quranic verse (4:29) is cited as the primary evidence for the consent-based nature of contracts. The passage sets the stage for further discussion comparing Islamic and foreign jurisprudence.

Key Terms
سلطان الإرادة العقدية (sultan al-irada al-'aqdiyya): The principle that the will of the contracting parties is the primary source for creating and determining the effects of a contract, with minimal formalities.
تراضي (taradi): Mutual consent or agreement of the contracting parties, considered the foundation of a valid contract.
عقد رضائي ('aqd rida'i): A consensual contract that is formed by mutual consent without formalities.

Key Takeaway
Islamic law's principle of contractual will grants broad freedom to parties, requiring only mutual consent and adherence to Sharia limits, with marriage as the sole exception requiring formalities.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Contractual Autonomy in Islamic Law
Foundational principle of contractual autonomy with Quranic evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the principle of contractual autonomy in Islamic law, identify its Quranic evidence, and apply it to determine the permissibility of a novel contract type.

Core Idea
Islamic law grants parties broad freedom to contract, limited only by explicit prohibitions and general Shariah principles.

In this lesson, we explore a foundational principle of Islamic contract law: contractual autonomy. Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa explains that Shariah does not prescribe a fixed list of permissible contracts. Instead, any contract not explicitly prohibited is valid, provided it meets general conditions. The Quran provides the primary evidence for this freedom, emphasizing mutual consent and the obligation to fulfill agreements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
Define 'sultan al-iradah al-'aqdiyyah' as the freedom of contracting parties to create, modify, and determine the effects of contracts through their will.
This principle is the foundation of Islamic contract law.
2
Locate the textual evidence
Identify the Quranic verses cited: 4:29 (trade by mutual consent), 4:4 (voluntary gift), 5:1 (fulfill contracts), and 17:34 (fulfill covenant). Note how each supports an aspect of contractual autonomy.
These verses are primary sources (dalil naqli).
3
Understand the classification
Classify the verses into the four aspects: (A) freedom to contract, (B) mutual consent, (C) creation of new contracts, (D) binding force.
The author groups these aspects to show the comprehensive nature of the principle.
4
Note the limitation
Recognize that the principle is not absolute; it is subject to explicit prohibitions and general Shariah rules (e.g., no riba, no gharar).
The default is permissibility, but specific prohibitions override.
5
Apply the principle to a new case
Given a novel contract (e.g., a cryptocurrency-based partnership), apply the principle: check for explicit prohibition, verify mutual consent, and ensure no conflict with Shariah objectives. Conclude permissibility if none found.
This is a practical skill for fatwa and legal analysis.

ومن الواضح أن العقد يتضمن تعهداً ضمنياً باحترام نتائجه والالتزام بها فهذه النصوص تشمل جميع أنواع العقود وما يستجد منها دون تقييد بالمعروف منها قبلاً ما دامت لا تصادم نصوص الشريعة ومقاصدها وقواعدها العامة.
وهذه النصوص وأمثالها تفيد أن عقد الإنسان وتعهده الذي باشره (١) يقول شيخ الإسلام ابن تيمية رحمه الله في كتابه «قاعدة العقود» تعليقاً على هاتين الآيتين: ففي التبرعات علق الحكم بطيب النفس، وفي المعاوضات على الحكم بالتراضي - وهو الرضا من الجانبين - لأن كلاً من المتعاوضين يطلب ما عند الآخر ويرضى به بخلاف المتبرع فإنه لم يبذل له شيء يرضى به ولكن قد تسمح نفسه بالبذل، وهو طيب النفس .

The Sixth Chapter: The Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Jurisprudence. In Islamic law, there is nothing indicating any restriction on the types of contracts or limiting people to them. So every subject that the Law has not prohibited by explicit text and which the legal rules and principles do not require to be prohibited, it is permissible for people to contract upon and bind themselves by their contracts; then the contracting therein is subject to the general rules and conditions in contracts: capacity of the contracting party, suitability of the subject matter, etc. D - As for the fourth aspect, which is the extent of the control of the will of the contracting parties over determining the effects of a named contract and modifying its original rulings regarding their private rights between themselves by conditions they agree upon in the contract, i.e., creating whatever obligations and restrictions they wish in the contract by their will, it is the most important aspect of the control of will in the view of modern law. The position of Islamic jurisprudence on this aspect varies in breadth and narrowness according to the different ijtihadi schools, but the broadest of its schools agrees with what legal theories in modern legal legislation have settled upon. We will present in the following chapters the principle of contractual autonomy in Islamic jurisprudence, then we will present in Chapter 44 a summary of the subject in foreign jurisprudence. Contractual Autonomy in the Texts of Shariah: 41/4 - Indeed, the texts of the Book and the Sunnah, which are the source of jurisprudential ijtihads in Islamic Shariah, have reached the utmost generality and flexibility regarding contractual autonomy. These texts can be classified, according to their subject, into the four aforementioned aspects of the control of will: 41/5 - (A) and (B) Regarding freedom of contract and its absolute mutual consent: The saying of Allah Almighty has come in that: {Do not consume your wealth among yourselves unjustly except through trade by mutual consent from you} (Surah an-Nisa 4:29). And His saying regarding the dowry of women and not diminishing any of their financial rights: {If they give up willingly to you anything of it, then consume it in satisfaction and wholesomeness} (Surah an-Nisa 4:4). These texts and their like indicate that the basis for entitlement to another's wealth or the legalization of any of his rights is the consent of its owner, either through trade and exchange, or through gift and relinquishment with full willingness and choice—that is, the operative factor is the will of the owner, who legally has complete freedom in concluding a contract of exchange or gift and commitment. 41/6 - (C) Regarding the creation of types of contracts and the binding force of the contract. In this aspect, there also came the saying of Allah Almighty in the Quran: {O you who believe, fulfill your contracts} (Surah al-Ma'ida 5:1) and His saying: {Fulfill the covenant; indeed, the covenant is ever inquired about} (Surah al-Isra' 17:34). It is clear that the contract implicitly includes a commitment to respect its results and adhere to them. So these texts encompass all types of contracts and whatever may emerge among them, without restricting them to those previously known, as long as they do not contradict the texts of Shariah, its objectives, and its general rules. And these texts and their like indicate that a person's contract and commitment that he undertakes... (1) Shaykh al-Islam Ibn Taymiyyah, may Allah have mercy on him, said in his book 'Qa'idat al-'Uqud' commenting on these two verses: 'In voluntary gifts, the ruling is attached to willingness of the soul, and in transactions, the ruling is attached to mutual consent—which is the consent of both parties—because each of the two transactors seeks what the other has and is pleased with it, unlike the donor, for nothing is given to him that he would be pleased with, but his soul may permit the giving, and that is willingness of the soul.' (1) Ref: Kitab Qa'idat al-'Uqud by Ibn Taymiyyah, chapter on consent in contracts and who is allowed to rescind if not satisfied.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
Passage type: Foundational principle (legal philosophy) on contractual autonomy. The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, opens the sixth chapter on the theory of contracts. He states that Islamic law does not limit the types of contracts; any agreement not prohibited by explicit text or general principles is permissible. Then he focuses on the fourth aspect: the parties' freedom to modify the effects of named contracts through agreed conditions. He notes that among Islamic schools, the broadest (which includes the Hanafi school) aligns with modern legal theory. The author then presents the scriptural evidence: Quranic verses on mutual consent (4:29), voluntary gift (4:4), and fulfilling contracts (5:1, 17:34). These texts demonstrate that consent is the basis for ownership transfer and that all contracts, including new types, are binding as long as they do not violate Shariah. The commentary by Ibn Taymiyyah clarifies that in gifts the condition is willingness of the giver, while in sales it is mutual consent. This passage establishes the Hanafi principle of contractual freedom within the limits of Shariah, which is fundamental to Islamic contract law.

Key Terms
سلطان الإرادة العقدية (sultan al-iradah al-'aqdiyyah): The power of the contracting parties to freely create, modify, and determine the effects of contracts through their will.
تراض (taradin): Mutual consent of both parties in a contract.
عقد ('aqd): A contractual agreement between two or more parties creating legal obligations.
تعهد (ta'ahhud): A commitment or undertaking, often synonymous with a contractual obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for entitlement to another's property under Islamic law?
A) The ruler's permission
B) Written documentation
C) The owner's consent, either through trade or gift
D) Customary practice

Key Takeaway
Islamic law grants broad contractual autonomy: any contract not explicitly prohibited and based on mutual consent is binding and valid.

Consent as Basis for Property Transactions
States the Quranic basis for consent as foundation of property rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal principle that consent is the basis for entitlement to another's property and apply it to distinguish between gifts and sales.

Core Idea
Consent is the foundation of all property transfers; without it, no transaction is valid.

This lesson explores a foundational principle in Islamic financial law: that ownership and entitlement to another's property hinge on the owner's consent. Derived from the Quranic verse on dowry (4:4), this principle governs both commercial exchanges and gratuitous transfers. Understanding the nuances between mutual consent (taradi) and willingness of the soul (tayyib al-nafs) is essential for correctly applying Hanafi rulings in transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic evidence
Recognize the verse cited (Quran 4:4) and its context regarding women's dowries. The verse permits taking from the dowry only if the wife willingly gives it away.
The verse is a primary dalil for the consent principle.
2
Extract the legal principle
From the verse and similar texts, derive that the basis for entitlement to another's property or rights is only the owner's consent.
This principle is expressed as a qawida: الأصل في استحقاق مال الغير إنما هو رضا صاحبه.
3
Distinguish between exchange and gift
In commercial exchanges (sales, leases) the standard is mutual consent (taradi), while in gifts a higher standard of willingness (tayyib al-nafs) is required because the donor receives no compensation.
Ibn Taymiyyah's commentary highlights this distinction.
4
Apply the principle to furuat
Review the examples in the qawida furuat: sales require mutual consent, gifts require willingness. Also note the istithna' of compulsory sales to satisfy debts, where necessity overrides consent.
This shows the principle's scope and exceptions.

Source Text

Chapter (41) The Authority of Contractual Will - General Overview. And His statement, exalted, regarding women's dowries and not diminishing any of their financial rights: "But if they give up willingly to you anything of it, then consume it with pleasure and satisfaction" (Surah An-Nisa 4:4). So these texts and their like indicate that the basis for entitlement to another's property or for legalizing any of their rights is only the consent of its owner, either by way of trade and exchange, or by way of gift and relinquishment with willingness and choice, i.e., the operative factor in that is the will of the property owner who legally has complete freedom in concluding a contract of exchange or gift. (1) Shaykh al-Islam Ibn Taymiyyah (may Allah have mercy on him) says in his book 'Qa'idat al-'Uqud' commenting on these two verses: "In gifts, the ruling is attached to willingness of the soul, and in exchanges to mutual consent - which is consent from both sides - because each of the two exchanging parties seeks what the other has and is pleased with it, unlike the donor who has not given something to be pleased with, but his soul may be generous in giving, and that is willingness of the soul." Ref: Book 'Qa'idat al-'Uqud' by Ibn Taymiyyah, chapter on consent in contracts and who may rescind if not satisfied.
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage states the foundational legal principle that the rightful basis for taking another's property or any of their rights is their own consent. The author cites Quran 4:4 which, in the context of dowry, commands that if women willingly give up part of their dowry, men may consume it with pleasure. The verse establishes that consent is the operative element in financial transactions, whether commercial (معاوضة) or gratuitous (تبرع). The footnote by Ibn Taymiyyah distinguishes between the two: in gifts, the ruling depends on the giver's inclination (طيب النفس), while in exchanges it depends on mutual agreement (تراضي). This principle is universally accepted in the Hanafi madhhab, which treats consent as the core of contractual validity, with formal expression (ijab and qabul) being external indicators of that internal consent. Thus, any transaction lacking genuine consent is defective in Hanafi law.

Key Terms
رضا (rida): the willing acceptance of a party to a transaction
طيب النفس (tayyib al-nafs): willingness of the soul, a level of consent beyond mere silence
تراضي (taradi): mutual consent between the two parties

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Formal contract documentation
B) Consent of the owner
C) State regulation
D) Customary practice

Key Takeaway
The rightful basis for taking another's property or rights is only the owner's consent, whether in exchange or gift; this principle is grounded in Quran 4:4 and universally upheld in the Hanafi school.

Binding Force of Contracts: The Maxim 'أَوْفُوا بِالْعُقُودِ'
Expands the ruling from Quranic verses to cover all contracts, with examples of non-binding contracts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'أَوْفُوا بِالْعُقُودِ' to distinguish between contracts that are binding in effect before rescission and those that are void, and state the Hanafi position on the binding nature of non-binding contracts.

Core Idea
Every contract binds until rescinded; 'non-binding' only means revocable, not unenforceable.

This lesson explores the foundational Quranic command to fulfill contracts and how Hanafi jurists interpret it to cover all types of agreements, including those termed 'non-binding'. You will see how this maxim ensures stability in economic dealings while respecting contractual freedom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary dalil
Locate the Quranic verses cited (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:1 and Surah al-Isra 17:34) and note that they are the basis for the obligation.
The verses use the imperative 'أوفوا' which in Hanafi usul indicates obligation (wujub).
2
Understand the scope of the command
Recognize that the word 'العقود' is general (amm), covering all contracts, named and unnamed, as long as they do not conflict with Shariah.
The passage explicitly states: 'وإطلاق هذه النصوص يشمل العقود المسماة وغير المسماة'.
3
Grasp the principle of bindingness
The contractor voluntarily restricts his own will; this restriction is an expression of freedom, and the commitment is binding.
The maxim 'من قيد نفسه فهو حر' (he who restricts himself is free) encapsulates this.
4
Apply to non-binding contracts
Even contracts termed 'غير لازم' (non-binding) have binding effects before rescission. The word 'non-binding' refers only to the possibility of future termination.
Examples from the text: agency, loan for use (ariyah), and arbitration.
5
Work through the furuat
Analyze the specific cases (agency, ariyah, arbitration) to see how the principle is applied in different chapters of fiqh.
These examples show that prior actions remain valid and enforceable.

(۱) يلحظ في هذا المقام أنه تستوي في ذلك جميع العقود، ما يسمى منها في اصطلاح الفقهاء عقوداً لازمة، أو غير لازمة، لأن معنى عدم اللزوم في اصطلاح الفقهاء هو إمكان رجوع العاقد عن الاستمرار على حكم العقد بحسب موضوع العقد وطبيعته لكنه قبل الرجوع ملتزم بالنتائج : فتصرف الوكيل قبل علمه بفسخ الوكالة ملزم للموكل ونافذ عليه، وانتفاع المستعير بالعارية قبل رجوع المعير جار على حكم المجانية ليس للمعير أن يطلب عنه عوضاً، وصدور الحكم من المحكم قبل رجوع أحد الخصمين عن التحكيم ملزم للطرفين وهلم جراً ... أي إن العقد الذي يعتبر غير لازم في أصله ليس للرجوع فيه أثر كما تقدم (ر: ف ٧/٤٠) تقدم رجعي (۲) سنرى في بحث تصنيف العقود آخر نظرية العقد أن العقد المسمى هو الذي أقر الشرع له اسماً خاصاً ورتب له أحكاماً كالبيع والإجارة والرهن.
6/41 - (c) Regarding the creation of types of contracts, and regarding the binding force of the contract. Also on this point, the Almighty said in the Quran: 'O you who have believed, fulfill contracts' (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:1) and His saying: 'And fulfill the covenant; indeed, the covenant will be asked about' (Surah al-Isra 17:34). It is clear that the contract includes an implicit commitment to respect its consequences and adhere to them. So these texts include all types of contracts and whatever may arise among them without restriction to those previously known, as long as they do not conflict with the texts of Shariah, its objectives, and its general rules. And these texts and their like indicate that a person's contract and commitment that he undertook with his free will are binding upon him in their consequences, and his will is restricted by his will, so that confidence and reassurance regarding the results of economic dealings are generated, and a person knows how to plan his action, because that depends on stability in those results between the dealing parties, as previously stated (ref: f 2/40). And this bindingness means full respect for the freedom of the contractor and for the rights arising from his contract for others, for 'he who restricts himself is free.' And the generality of these texts includes named and unnamed contracts, and indicates the absence of restriction to specific types of contracts. (1) It is noted in this context that all contracts are equal in this regard, whether they are called in the jurists' terminology binding or non-binding, because the meaning of non-bindingness in the jurists' terminology is the possibility of the contractor withdrawing from continuing with the ruling of the contract according to the subject and nature of the contract, but before withdrawal he is bound by the consequences: so the action of the agent before knowledge of the revocation of agency is binding on the principal and effective against him, and the benefit of the borrower of a loan for use before the lender's revocation proceeds on the basis of gratuitousness, the lender cannot demand compensation for it, and the issuance of a ruling by an arbitrator before one of the litigants withdraws from arbitration is binding on both parties, and so on... meaning that a contract that is considered non-binding in its origin has no effect of revocation, as previously stated (ref: f 7/40). (2) We will see in the discussion on classification of contracts at the end of contract theory that the named contract is one for which Shariah has prescribed a specific name and assigned rulings, such as sale, lease, and pledge.
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage establishes the binding force of contracts as a foundational principle in Islamic law, citing two key Quranic verses: 'Fulfill contracts' (5:1) and 'Fulfill the covenant' (17:34). The author argues that these verses cover all types of contracts, both existing and future, provided they do not conflict with Shariah principles. The bindingness arises from the contractor's own free will, so restricting oneself by one's own commitment is an exercise of freedom. The footnote clarifies that even so-called 'non-binding' contracts (like agency, loan for use, arbitration) have binding effects before revocation; the non-binding nature merely allows future withdrawal, not retroactive nullification. Hanafi jurists distinguish between binding (لازم) and non-binding (غير لازم) contracts, but both create obligations until rescinded. For instance, a principal is bound by the agent's acts until he revokes the agency. The rule 'من قيد نفسه فهو حر' (he who restricts himself is free) encapsulates this idea: voluntary assumption of obligations is an expression of freedom.

Key Terms
العقد (al-‘aqd): a contract or commitment voluntarily undertaken, implying rights and obligations
الإلزام (al-ilzam): bindingness; the quality of a contract that obliges the parties to fulfill its consequences
العقد غير اللازم (al-‘aqd ghayr al-lazim): a contract that permits one party or both to rescind it unilaterally

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Quranic command to fulfill contracts applies to all agreements, named or unnamed, and even 'non-binding' contracts have binding effects before rescission.

Freedom of Conditions and the Fixed Effects of Named Contracts
Discusses fixed effects of named contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a named contract, list its essential effects, and apply the rule that conditions contradicting these effects are void in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Named contracts have fixed essential effects that cannot be altered by contrary conditions.

When you enter a named contract like marriage or sale, certain legal consequences automatically arise. The Shariah has defined these effects as integral to the contract's purpose. This lesson explores the boundary between freedom to stipulate conditions and the inviolability of these essential effects, a key concept in Hanafi contract theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the named contract and its essential effects
From the passage, name the contract (e.g., marriage, sale) and list the effects mentioned: for marriage, enjoyment, maintenance, inheritance, etc. These are the 'athar al-aqd' that cannot be negated.
Focus on the specific effects listed; they vary by contract.
2
Distinguish between essential and non-essential effects
Essential effects are those inherent to the contract's nature. Conditions that seek to exclude them are void. Non-essential effects may be modifiable by agreement. Recognise this distinction from the text's emphasis on fixed rulings.
Non-essential effects are not discussed here but are implied by the existence of valid conditions.
3
Apply the rule to a condition that contradicts an essential effect
Take a condition like 'no inheritance in marriage'. Determine if it contradicts an essential effect. Since inheritance is listed as an effect, such a condition is void; the effect remains. The marriage remains valid.
This is a key Hanafi position; the condition is fasid but does not nullify the contract.
4
Identify the complementary maxim for valid conditions
The text implies that conditions compatible with the contract are valid. Reference the maxim 'Muslims are bound by their conditions' which allows freedom within the contract's scope.
This maxim does not override essential effects; it operates within the permissible range.

7/41 - (d) Regarding freedom of contractual conditions and the extent of their consideration. There are also many texts in Shariah from the Quran and verbal and practical Sunnah that establish for named contracts, which Shariah has approved, effects, rulings, and obligations that bind the parties in each contract according to its subject. So the marriage contract entails the permissibility of enjoyment between the sexes, the obligation of the woman to follow the man, the obligation of maintenance for the woman upon the man, the establishment of lineage, custody rights, prohibition of in-law relations, and inheritance between spouses and between them and children, etc. Likewise, the contract of sale, lease, pledge, guarantee, and others, each has been established. Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage addresses the freedom to stipulate conditions in contracts, but within the confines of the fixed effects that Shariah has attached to each named contract type. For example, in marriage (nikah), there are inherent consequences like legality of intimacy, mutual rights, and inheritance that cannot be contracted away. Similarly, sale, lease, pledge, and guarantee have their own essential rulings. The footnote (likely placed here erroneously in the OCR) reiterates the point from the previous section about all contracts, binding or not, having binding effects before rescission. The key takeaway for Hanafi fiqh is that while parties may include conditions (شروط) in their contracts, conditions that contradict the very purpose of the contract or its essential effects are void (شرط فاسد). For instance, a condition in a marriage contract that there will be no mutual inheritance is invalid because it negates an inherent effect. However, conditions that are compatible with the contract type are permissible and must be fulfilled according to the maxim 'المسلمون على شروطهم'. The last part defines 'named contract' as one with a specific name and rulings in Shariah, like sale, lease, and pledge.

Key Terms
العقد المسمى (al-‘aqd al-musamma): a contract for which Shariah has prescribed a specific name and assigned distinct rulings
الشروط العقدية (al-shurut al-‘aqdiyya): conditions stipulated by the parties within a contract
آثار العقد (athar al-‘aqd): the legal consequences and obligations that automatically arise from a valid contract

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The condition is valid and must be followed
B) The condition is void but the marriage is still valid
C) The condition makes the marriage contract void
D) The condition is valid only if agreed before the marriage

Key Takeaway
Named contracts have fixed essential effects that cannot be negated by stipulation; the Hanafi mufti must recognise this limit on freedom of contract.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

8 min
Freedom of Contract and Conditions in Islamic Law
Explaining the binding nature of contracts and the authority to modify effects via conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on contractual freedom, distinguish between named and unnamed contracts, and apply the maxims 'Muslims are bound by their conditions' and 'Every condition not in the Book of Allah is invalid' to identify valid conditions.

Core Idea
A contract is binding by the contractor's own will, but conditions are valid only if they do not contradict Sharia objectives.

Islamic contract law grants parties broad freedom to shape their agreements. The Prophet ﷺ affirmed this by saying 'Muslims are bound by their conditions.' Yet this freedom is not absolute. This lesson explores the balance between the binding nature of contracts and the limits placed on conditions, drawing on the two foundational hadiths that govern contractual stipulations.

Guided Path
1
Understand the binding nature of contracts
Recognize that a contract entered into freely is binding in its consequences, generating trust and stability. The principle 'the one who restricts himself is free' (مقيد نفسه حر) captures this.
2
Recognize the generality of contracts
Islamic law does not limit contracts to named types (e.g., sale, marriage). Both named and unnamed contracts are valid, allowing flexibility for new transactions.
3
Identify the authority to stipulate conditions
Contracting parties may modify the standard effects of a contract by adding or subtracting obligations through conditions. This is the essence of 'sulṭān al-irādah' (authority of the will).
4
Interpret the two key hadiths on conditions
The hadith 'Muslims are bound by their conditions' establishes validity of conditions. The second hadith 'Every condition not in the Book of Allah is invalid' limits this: 'Book of Allah' means the entire Sharia, so only conditions contradicting Sharia rules or objectives are invalid.
The story of Barirah illustrates: a condition contrary to the established rule of wala' (patronage) is void.
5
Apply the principle to distinguish valid and invalid conditions
A condition is valid if it does not contradict a definitive Sharia rule, oppose the contract's nature, or involve prohibited elements (e.g., riba, gharar).

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
بإرادته الحرة ملزم له بنتائجه، ومقيد لإرادته بإرادته، كي تتولد الثقة والاطمئنان إلى نتائج التعامل الاقتصادية، ويعلم الإنسان كيف يبني تصرفه، لأن ذلك متوقف على الاستقرار في تلك النتائج بين المتعاملين، كما تقدم (ر: ف ٢/٤٠).
وهذا الإلزام معناه تمام الاحترام لحرية العاقد وللحقوق الناشئة بعقده لغيره، فإنَّ مقيد نفسه حر.
وإطلاق هذه النصوص يشمل العقود المسماة وغير المسماة، يفيد عدم التقييد بأنواع محدودة من العقود.
٧/٤١ - (د) في حرية الشروط العقدية، ومدى اعتبارها :
ورد في الشريعة أيضاً نصوص كثيرة من القرآن والسنة القولية والعملية تثبت للعقود المسماة التي أقرها الشرع آثاراً وأحكاماً والتزامات تربط الطرفين في كل عقد بحسب موضوعه.
فعقد النكاح يترتب عليه حل الاستمتاع بين الجنسين، ووجوب متابعة المرأة للرجل، ووجوب النفقة للمرأة على الرجل، وثبوت النسب، وحقوق الحضانة وحرمة المصاهرة، والإرث بين الزوجين ثم بينهما وبين الأولاد إلخ .
وكذا عقد البيع والإجارة والرهن والكفالة ،وغيرها، كل منها قد ثبتت في الشرع له أحكام منها ما بينه الكتاب والسنة القولية أو العملية مباشرة، ومنها ما أضافه الاجتهاد استنباطاً.
أما سلطة العاقدين على تعديل هذه الآثار التي قررتها النصوص للعقود إما بالنقص منها، وإما بإضافة التزامات على أحد الطرفين المتعاقدين لا يستلزمها أصل العقد، وذلك بشروط يشترطانها في التعاقد، هذه السلطة، فقد ورد حولها نصوص عامة وخاصة مختلفة :
١ - ففي القرآن وردت آيات عامة منها الآيتان اللتان أوردناهما في الفقرتين السابقتين .
٢ - وفي السنة النبوية جاء عن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام أنه قال : المسلمون على شروطهم .
وقال أيضاً: كل شرط ليس في كتاب الله فهو باطل» أي كل شرط ينافي مقاصد شريعة الله .
(انظر في تخريج هذين الحديثين ما تقدم ف ٢/١٠ الحاشية). والكتاب هنا مصدر (كتب) بمعنى أوجب وشرع أي كل شرط ليس في حكم الله وشرعه فهو باطل؛ وذلك بأن يكون الشرط منافياً لقواعد الشريعة أو مقاصدها؛ هكذا فسره العلماء المحققون .
(ر) المغني لموفق الدين بن قدامة الحنبلي ٤٤٨/٨ ، وكشاف القناع ٥٣/٣). وليس معنى كتاب الله هنا القرآن لأنّ القرآن إنما بين أصول الشريعة، ولم يتضمن تفصيل الشروط العقدية المقبولة والممنوعة كما تقدم بيانه هناك. واستعمال الكتاب بالمعنى الذي بيناه كثير في لسان الشرع كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ الصلوة كانت على المؤمنين كتبا موقونًا ﴾ [سورة النساء ١٠٣/٤] أي فريضة محددة الأوقات. وكذا قوله تعالى عن عدم جواز نكاح المرأة المعتدة: ﴿وَلَا تَعْدِمُوا عُقْدَةَ النكاح حتى يبلغ الكِتَبُ أَجَلَهُ ﴾ [سورة البقرة ٢٣٥/٢] أي حتى تنتهي العدة التي فرضها وكتبها الشرع على المرأة بانحلال النكاح السابق. وأصل هذا الحديث أن بريرة كانت أمة رقيقة، فأرادت عائشة أم المؤمنين رضي الله عنها أن تشتريها وتعتقها. ومن المقرر شرعاً بقول الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام أن المولى إذا أعتق عبده يكون للمولى المعتق حق الولاء - أي حق الإرث من عبده العتيق في مرتبة معينة من مراتب الميراث ترغيباً في إعتاق الأرقاء - فأراد أصحاب
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Jurisprudence. By his free will, [a contract] is binding upon him in its consequences, and restricts his will by his own will, so that trust and confidence in the results of economic dealings are generated, and a person knows how to structure his transaction, because that depends on stability in those results between the contracting parties, as previously stated (see: Section 2/40). And this binding means full respect for the freedom of the contractor and for the rights arising from his contract for others, for the one who restricts himself is free. And the generality of these texts includes named and unnamed contracts, indicating no restriction to limited types of contracts. 7/41 - (D) On the freedom of contractual conditions and the extent of their validity: There have also been many texts in Sharia from the Quran and the oral and practical Sunnah that establish for the named contracts approved by Sharia effects, rulings, and obligations that bind both parties in each contract according to its subject. For the marriage contract, it entails the permissibility of enjoyment between the sexes, the obligation of the wife to follow the husband, the obligation of maintenance for the wife upon the husband, the establishment of lineage, custody rights, the prohibition of marriage due to affinity, inheritance between the spouses and then between them and children, etc. Likewise, the contracts of sale, lease, pledge, guarantee, etc., each of them has established in Sharia rulings, some of which are explained directly by the Book and the oral or practical Sunnah, and some of which are added by ijtihad through deduction. As for the authority of the contracting parties to modify these effects that the texts have established for contracts, either by reducing them or by adding obligations on one of the contracting parties that the original contract does not require, through conditions they stipulate in the contract—this authority, there have come various general and specific texts concerning it: 1 - In the Quran, there are general verses, including the two verses we mentioned in the previous two paragraphs. 2 - In the Prophetic Sunnah, it came from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'Muslims are bound by their conditions.' And he also said: 'Every condition that is not in the Book of Allah is invalid'—i.e., every condition that contradicts the objectives of the Sharia of Allah. (See the takhrij of these two hadiths in Section 2/10 footnote). And 'the Book' here is a verbal noun from 'kataba' meaning 'obliged' and 'legislated', so it means every condition that is not in the ruling and legislation of Allah is invalid, because the condition contradicts the rules or objectives of Sharia; this is how the verifying scholars interpreted it. (See al-Mughni by Muwaffaq al-Din Ibn Qudamah al-Hanbali 8/448, and Kashshaf al-Qina' 3/53). And 'Book of Allah' here does not mean the Quran, because the Quran only explains the principles of Sharia, and does not include the details of acceptable and prohibited contractual conditions, as previously explained there. And the use of 'al-kitab' in the sense we explained is frequent in the language of Sharia, as in His statement: 'Indeed, prayer has been upon the believers a decree at specified times' [Surah al-Nisa 4:103] meaning an obligatory duty with specified times. Likewise, His statement regarding the prohibition of marrying a woman in her waiting period: 'And do not resolve the marriage contract until the prescribed period reaches its end' [Surah al-Baqarah 2:235] meaning until the waiting period that Sharia has prescribed and written for the woman following the dissolution of the previous marriage. And the origin of this hadith is that Barirah was a slave girl, and Aisha, the Mother of the Believers, may Allah be pleased with her, wanted to buy her and free her. And it is established in Sharia by the statement of the Messenger, peace be upon him, that if the master frees his slave, the freeing master has the right of patronage (wala')—i.e., the right of inheritance from his freed slave in a certain rank of inheritance to encourage the freeing of slaves—so the owners wanted...
Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm discusses the principle of contractual freedom in Islamic law. The author explains that a contract entered into by a person's free will is binding in its consequences, which generates trust and stability in economic transactions. This binding nature is a manifestation of respect for the freedom of the contractor and the rights of others; the one who restricts himself is free. The general texts (Quran and Sunnah) apply to both named contracts (e.g., sale, marriage, lease) and unnamed contracts, indicating that Islamic law does not limit contracts to specific types.

The passage then focuses on the authority of contractors to modify the effects established by Sharia for named contracts through conditions. The author cites two key hadiths: 'Muslims are bound by their conditions' and 'Every condition not in the Book of Allah is invalid.' The author clarifies the latter, explaining that 'the Book of Allah' here refers to the divine legislation, not just the Quran. Conditions are invalid only if they contradict the rules or objectives of Sharia. The author supports this interpretation by referring to the usage of 'al-kitab' in Quranic verses to mean obligation or decree. The original context of the hadith (the story of Barirah and the rights of wala') illustrates how conditions that contravene established Sharia rules are void.

Thus, the passage establishes a balanced view: contractors have broad freedom to stipulate conditions, but this freedom is bounded by the requirement that conditions do not violate Sharia principles or objectives.

Key Terms
العقد المسمى (al-‘aqd al-musammā): A contract that has a specific name in Sharia and for which specific effects and rulings are prescribed, such as sale, marriage, lease.
العقد غير المسمى (al-‘aqd ghayr al-musammā): A contract that does not have a specific name in Sharia but falls under general principles of contract law, such as various modern contracts.
سلطان الإرادة (sulṭān al-irādah): The authority or power of the contractor's will to determine the effects of the contract and to modify them through conditions.
شرط (sharṭ): A condition stipulated by the contracting parties in a contract, which may add, subtract, or modify the legal effects of the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the hadith 'Every condition not in the Book of Allah is invalid' mean?
A) Any condition not found in the Quran is invalid.
B) Any condition that contradicts the rules or objectives of Sharia is invalid.
C) All conditions are invalid except those explicitly mentioned in the Quran.
D) Conditions are only valid if they appear in the Qur'an or Sunnah.

Key Takeaway
Contracts are binding, and conditions are valid unless they contradict Sharia principles.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
The Foundational Qawa'id for Contractual Conditions
Examining the scope of contractual conditions through Quran and Sunnah texts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two maxims, explain their relationship, and identify conditions that conflict with Sharia objectives.

Core Idea
Muslims are bound by conditions, except those that contradict Allah's law.

This lesson unpacks the two core hadith that govern contractual conditions in Islamic law. You will learn the precise meaning of 'Kitab Allah' and how the Hanafi school interprets these texts to balance party autonomy with Sharia objectives. The story of Barirah serves as a concrete illustration of when a condition is void.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the two hadith
Write down the two hadith: 'المسلمون على شروطهم' and 'كل شرط ليس في كتاب الله فهو باطل'. Understand that they work together: the first establishes the default validity of conditions, the second restricts it.
Both are sahih; the second is a dabt (restrictive sub-maxim) to the first.
2
Define 'Kitab Allah'
The passage explains that 'Kitab Allah' here does not mean the Qur'an alone, but Allah's entire legislation—the rules and objectives of Sharia. Verify this with the examples given from Qur'anic usage (e.g., 'kitaban mawqutan').
This is a crucial Hanafi interpretation that avoids overly literal reading.
3
Analyze the Barirah incident
Read the story: the sellers wanted to stipulate that wala' (inheritance) remains with them after Aisha frees Barirah. The Prophet voided this condition. Explain why it contradicts Sharia objectives (encouraging manumission).
The condition would deter the buyer from freeing the slave, undermining the maqsad of itq.
4
Relate the two maxims
Apply the maxims: the condition was agreed upon, so prima facie valid under 'المسلمون على شروطهم', but it conflicted with Sharia objectives, so it falls under 'كل شرط ليس في كتاب الله' and is void. Understand that the second acts as a restriction on the first.
The relationship is complementary: the second defines the limit of the first.

الفصل (٤١) سلطان الإرادة العقدية - نظرة عامة
في الشرع له أحكام منها ما بينه الكتاب والسنة القولية أو العملية مباشرة، ومنها ما أضافه الاجتهاد استنباطاً.
مدى سلطة العاقد على تعديل آثار العقود
أما سلطة العاقدين على تعديل هذه الآثار التي قررتها النصوص للعقود إما بالنقص منها، وإما بإضافة التزامات على أحد الطرفين المتعاقدين لا يستلزمها أصل العقد، وذلك بشروط يشترطانها في التعاقد، هذه السلطة، فقد ورد حولها نصوص عامة وخاصة مختلفة : ۱ - ففي القرآن وردت آيات عامة منها الآيتان اللتان أوردناهما في الفقرتين السابقتين.
ومدى
1
۲ - وفي السنة النبوية جاء عن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام أنه قال : المسلمون على شروطهم (۱) .
وقال أيضاً: كل شرط ليس في كتاب الله فهو باطل»(١) أي كل شرط ينافي مقاصد شريعة الله .
(۱) انظر في تخريج هذين الحديثين ما تقدم ف ٢/١٠ الحاشية). والكتاب هنا مصدر (كتب) بمعنى أوجب وشرع أي كل شرط ليس في حكم الله وشرعه فهو باطل؛ وذلك بأن يكون الشرط منافياً لقواعد الشريعة أو مقاصدها؛ هكذا فسره العلماء المحققون .
(ر) المغني لموفق الدين بن قدامة الحنبلي ٤٤٨/٨ ، وكشاف القناع ٥٣/٣). وليس معنى كتاب الله هنا القرآن لأنّ القرآن إنما بين أصول الشريعة، ولم يتضمن تفصيل الشروط العقدية المقبولة والممنوعة كما تقدم بيانه هناك. واستعمال الكتاب بالمعنى الذي بيناه كثير في لسان الشرع كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ الصلوة كانت على المؤمنين كتبا موقونًا ﴾ [سورة النساء ١٠٣/٤] أي فريضة محددة الأوقات. وكذا قوله تعالى عن عدم جواز نكاح المرأة المعتدة: ﴿وَلَا تَعْدِمُوا عُقْدَةَ النكاح حتى يبلغ الكِتَبُ أَجَلَهُ ﴾ [سورة البقرة ٢٣٥/٢] أي حتى تنتهي العدة التي فرضها وكتبها الشرع على المرأة بانحلال النكاح السابق. وأصل هذا الحديث أن بريرة كانت أمة رقيقة، فأرادت عائشة أم المؤمنين رضي الله عنها أن تشتريها وتعتقها. ومن المقرر شرعاً بقول الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام أن المولى إذا أعتق عبده يكون للمولى المعتق حق الولاء - أي حق الإرث من عبده العتيق في مرتبة معينة من مراتب الميراث ترغيباً في إعتاق الأرقاء - فأراد أصحاب
بريرة أن يشترطوا على عائشة في البيع أن يكون ولاء بريرة لهم إذا هي أعتقتها بعد الشراء، فأخبرت عائشة النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام بذلك، فقال: ما بال أناس يشترطون شروطاً ليست في كتاب الله؟ من اشترط شرطاً ليس في كتاب الله فليس له، وإن شرط مئة شرط شرط الله أحق وأوثق رواه البخاري في البيوع والمكاتب (ر: ف ٢/٢٠ حاشية).
ووجه منافاة هذا الشرط لمقاصد الشريعة فيما يظهر أن الشرع الإسلامي يهدف إلى ترغيب الناس في إعتاق الرقاب الرقيقة، وقد فَتَح لذلك أبواباً منها إلزامية ومنها اختيارية. ولذا جعل حق الإرث بالولاء لمن أعتق ثم لعصبة ورثته من بعده ترغيباً في الإعتاق. فإذا اشترط على مشتري العبد أنه إن أعتقه فولاؤه للبائع كان هذا الشرط مما يحمل المشتري على الاحتفاظ بملكية العبد المبيع وعدم إعتاقه، فيختل غرض الشارع وسياسته الشرعية في موضوع وحكم لهما اتصال بمقاصد الشريعة.
٣ - وروي أيضاً أن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام: نهى عن بيع وشرط هكذا روي مجملاً بطريق الحكاية وليس هذا النص متفقاً على ثبوته وصحة إسناده إلى النبي كما سنری (ر: ف ٤/٤٢).
ومع هذا ثبت أيضاً في السنة العملية أن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام اشترى في السفر من جابر بن عبدالله بعيراً وشرط لجابر ظهره إلى المدينة أي أنه اشترط له حق ركوبه بعد البيع حتى يصل عليه إلى المدينة. (ر: بداية المجتهد ۱۳۲/۲ وفتح القدير شرح الهداية باب البيع الفاسد ٧٦/٦).
٤ - وكذلك قرّر النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام صفة الأمانة حكماً أصلياً في العارية بقوله : ليس على المستعير غير المُغلِّ ضمان» (۱) .
ومع هذا ثبت في السنة العملية أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام استعار دروعاً وسلاحاً من صفوان بن أمية لحاجة المسلمين إليها في غزوة حنين، واشترط له ضمانها بقوله : «عارية مضمونة مؤداة» (۲) .
(۱) المُغل (بضم الميم وتشديد اللام) اسم فاعل من الإغلال) وهو الخيانة في الأموال ر النهاية، والمصباح). والحديث رواه الدارقطني، وقال الحافظ في التلخيص ٣/ ۹۷: وفي إسناده ضعيفان. ورواه الدارقطني من طريق أخرى ضعيفة بلفظ : «لا ضمان على مؤتمن .
(۲) وهذا ما أفسح مجالاً لبعض الاجتهادات أن تذهب إلى أن صفة الأمانة في العارية هي -
Chapter (41) Authority of Contractual Will - General Overview. In Sharia, rulings include what the Book and Sunnah, verbal or practical, directly state, and what ijtihad adds by deduction. The extent of the contracting party's authority to modify the effects of contracts. As for the authority of the contracting parties to modify these effects established by texts for contracts, either by reducing them or by adding obligations on one of the contracting parties not required by the original contract, through conditions they stipulate in the contract, this authority: various general and specific texts have been reported concerning it. 1 - In the Qur'an, there are general verses, including the two we mentioned in the previous two paragraphs. 2 - In the Prophetic Sunnah, the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Muslims are bound by their conditions.' He also said: 'Every condition not in the Book of Allah is void,' meaning every condition that contradicts the objectives of Allah's Sharia. (1) See the discussion of the authentication of these two hadith in what preceded section 2/10 footnote. And 'the Book' here is derived from 'kataba' meaning ordained and legislated, i.e., every condition not in Allah's ruling and legislation is void; because the condition is contrary to the rules of Sharia or its objectives; this is how the verifying scholars have interpreted it. (Reference: Al-Mughni by Muwaffaq al-Din ibn Qudamah al-Hanbali 8/448, and Kashshaf al-Qina' 3/53). And the meaning of 'Book of Allah' here is not the Qur'an, because the Qur'an only expounds the principles of Sharia, and does not contain the details of acceptable and prohibited contractual conditions as explained there. And the use of 'kitab' in the sense we have explained is frequent in the language of Sharia, as in His saying: 'Indeed, prayer has been upon the believers a timed ordinance' (Qur'an 4:103), i.e., a specific obligatory time. And likewise His saying regarding the impermissibility of marrying a woman in her 'iddah: 'And do not resolve on the marriage contract until the prescribed term has reached its end' (Qur'an 2:235), i.e., until the 'iddah which Sharia has made obligatory on the woman due to the dissolution of the previous marriage ends. And the origin of this hadith is that Barirah was a slave woman, and Aisha, the Mother of the Believers (may Allah be pleased with her) wanted to buy her and set her free. And it is established in Sharia by the Prophet's (PBUH) saying that if a master frees his slave, the manumitting master has the right of wala' - i.e., the right of inheritance from his freed slave in a specific order of inheritance to encourage manumission - so the owners of Barirah wanted to stipulate to Aisha in the sale that the wala' of Barirah would belong to them if she freed her after purchase. Aisha informed the Prophet (PBUH), and he said: 'What is the matter with people who stipulate conditions not in the Book of Allah? Whoever stipulates a condition not in the Book of Allah, it is not for him, even if he stipulates a hundred conditions. Allah's condition is more binding and reliable.' Narrated by Bukhari in the Book of Sales and the Book of Mukatabah. And the reason this condition contradicts the objectives of Sharia, as it appears, is that Islamic law aims to encourage people to manumit slaves, and for this it opened many doors, some obligatory and some optional. Therefore, it made the right of inheritance by wala' for the one who frees and then for his 'asaba heirs after him, as an encouragement to manumission. So if one stipulates on the buyer of a slave that if he frees him, his wala' belongs to the seller, this condition would prompt the buyer to retain ownership of the sold slave and not free him, thus disrupting the purpose of the Lawgiver and His legal policy in a matter and ruling connected to the objectives of Sharia. 3 - It is also narrated that the Prophet (PBUH) forbade a sale with a condition - this is narrated in summary as a story, and this text is not unanimously agreed upon as authentic and correctly attributed to the Prophet as we will see (ref: section 4/42). Nevertheless, it is also established in the practical Sunnah that the Prophet (PBUH) traveled and bought a camel from Jabir ibn Abdullah and stipulated for Jabir its back until Medina, i.e., he stipulated for him the right to ride it after the sale until he reached Medina. (Ref: Bidayat al-Mujtahid 2/132, Fath al-Qadir Sharh al-Hidayah, chapter on invalid sale 6/76). 4 - Similarly, the Prophet (PBUH) established the characteristic of trustworthiness (amanah) as an original ruling in loan (ariyah) by his saying: 'There is no liability on the borrower who is not dishonest.' (1) Yet, it is established in the practical Sunnah that he (PBUH) borrowed armor and weapons from Safwan ibn Umayyah due to the Muslims' need in the Battle of Hunayn, and he stipulated for him its guarantee by saying: 'A guaranteed and repaid loan.' (2). (1) 'Al-mughall' (with damm on meem and shaddah on lam) is the active participle from 'ighlal' which is dishonesty in wealth (an-Nihaya, al-Misbah). The hadith is narrated by al-Daraqutni; al-Hafiz said in al-Talkhis 3/97: 'Its chain contains two weak narrators.' Al-Daraqutni also narrated it through another weak chain with the wording: 'No liability on the trustee.' (2) This allowed room for some ijtihad to hold that the characteristic of trustworthiness in loan is -
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage discusses the authority of contracting parties to modify the effects of contracts through conditions. The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, opens by stating that Sharia rulings come directly from Quran and Sunnah or through ijtihad. He then focuses on the authority of parties to alter contractual effects by adding or reducing obligations via conditions. He presents four categories of evidence: (1) General Quranic verses (not detailed here but referenced from earlier paragraphs). (2) Two key hadith: 'Muslims are bound by their conditions' and 'Every condition not in the Book of Allah is void.' He explains that 'Book of Allah' here does not mean the Quran but Allah's legislation, so conditions contradicting Sharia objectives are void. He illustrates with the story of Barirah: the sellers wanted to condition that wala' (inheritance right) remain with them after Aisha freed her. The Prophet rejected this because it contradicts the Sharia objective of encouraging manumission. (3) A weak hadith forbidding a sale with a condition, but the practical Sunnah shows the Prophet himself entered a sale with a condition (buying a camel and allowing the seller to ride it). (4) The general rule that a borrowed item is an amanah (trust) not subject to guarantee, but the Prophet in practice guaranteed borrowed armor due to need. This shows that general principles can be modified by specific conditions when necessary, as long as they do not contravene higher Sharia objectives. The passage thus establishes that the default permissibility of conditions is restricted only when they oppose the maqasid al-shariah.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A stipulation made by one or both contracting parties that modifies the default effects of the contract.
كتاب الله (Kitab Allah): Not the Qur'an alone, but Allah's legislation including the rules and objectives derived from the Qur'an and Sunnah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi interpretation of 'كل شرط ليس في كتاب الله فهو باطل', which condition would be void?
A) A condition that the seller delivers the item in a month.
B) A condition that the buyer pays in installments.
C) A condition that the buyer never resells the item to a specific person.
D) A condition that the seller receives wala' after the buyer frees the slave.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that contractual conditions are valid unless they contradict a clear objective of Sharia, not simply because they are not mentioned in the Qur'an.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate

Prophetic Practice: Exceptions to General Principles in Contracts
Examining the scope of contractual conditions through Quran and Sunnah texts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two maxims to cases where the Prophet himself made stipulations that appear to modify default rulings, and explain how these examples refine the scope of contractual conditions.

Core Idea
The Prophet's own transactions show that conditions are valid unless they undermine Sharia objectives.

This lesson examines two incidents from the Prophet's life: his purchase of a camel with a condition allowing the seller to ride it, and his borrowing of armor with a guarantee. These examples illustrate that general principles (like no liability for a borrower) can be modified by specific conditions, provided those conditions do not contravene higher Sharia objectives. This deepens understanding of how the two maxims work in practice.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two maxims
Quickly review: 'المسلمون على شروطهم' (default validity) and 'كل شرط ليس في كتاب الله فهو باطل' (void if contravenes Sharia).
These are the lenses through which we examine the Prophetic examples.
2
Analyze the camel sale condition
The Prophet bought a camel from Jabir and stipulated that Jabir can ride it to Medina. This is a condition that modifies the default effect of sale (full transfer of ownership and possession). Apply the maxims: it is agreed, so prima facie valid; does it contradict any Sharia objective? No, it is a temporary benefit that does not harm anyone.
This condition is valid and shows that modifying the effects of a contract is allowed as long as it is not haram.
3
Analyze the borrowed armor condition
The Prophet borrowed armor from Safwan and stipulated it is 'guaranteed and repaid'. The default for a loan (ariyah) is that the borrower is an amanah (trustee) and not liable unless negligent. Here, the Prophet voluntarily accepted liability. Apply the maxims: agreed, prima facie valid; does it contradict Sharia? No, it is an additional obligation willingly accepted.
This shows that default rulings can be overridden by mutual agreement when they are not obligatory rules (like the permissibility of stipulating liability).
4
Synthesize the lesson
Both cases involve conditions that modify default rulings. They are valid because they do not oppose any maqsad. They illustrate that 'المسلمون على شروطهم' applies broadly, and 'كل شرط ليس في كتاب الله' only voids conditions that actually contradict Sharia.
The Prophet's practice itself becomes a dalil for the Hanafi position.

Chapter (41) Authority of Contractual Will - General Overview. In Sharia, rulings include what the Book and Sunnah, verbal or practical, directly state, and what ijtihad adds by deduction. The extent of the contracting party's authority to modify the effects of contracts. As for the authority of the contracting parties to modify these effects established by texts for contracts, either by reducing them or by adding obligations on one of the contracting parties not required by the original contract, through conditions they stipulate in the contract, this authority: various general and specific texts have been reported concerning it. 1 - In the Qur'an, there are general verses, including the two we mentioned in the previous two paragraphs. 2 - In the Prophetic Sunnah, the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Muslims are bound by their conditions.' He also said: 'Every condition not in the Book of Allah is void,' meaning every condition that contradicts the objectives of Allah's Sharia. (1) See the discussion of the authentication of these two hadith in what preceded section 2/10 footnote. And 'the Book' here is derived from 'kataba' meaning ordained and legislated, i.e., every condition not in Allah's ruling and legislation is void; because the condition is contrary to the rules of Sharia or its objectives; this is how the verifying scholars have interpreted it. (Reference: Al-Mughni by Muwaffaq al-Din ibn Qudamah al-Hanbali 8/448, and Kashshaf al-Qina' 3/53). And the meaning of 'Book of Allah' here is not the Qur'an, because the Qur'an only expounds the principles of Sharia, and does not contain the details of acceptable and prohibited contractual conditions as explained there. And the use of 'kitab' in the sense we have explained is frequent in the language of Sharia, as in His saying: 'Indeed, prayer has been upon the believers a timed ordinance' (Qur'an 4:103), i.e., a specific obligatory time. And likewise His saying regarding the impermissibility of marrying a woman in her 'iddah: 'And do not resolve on the marriage contract until the prescribed term has reached its end' (Qur'an 2:235), i.e., until the 'iddah which Sharia has made obligatory on the woman due to the dissolution of the previous marriage ends. And the origin of this hadith is that Barirah was a slave woman, and Aisha, the Mother of the Believers (may Allah be pleased with her) wanted to buy her and set her free. And it is established in Sharia by the Prophet's (PBUH) saying that if a master frees his slave, the manumitting master has the right of wala' - i.e., the right of inheritance from his freed slave in a specific order of inheritance to encourage manumission - so the owners of Barirah wanted to stipulate to Aisha in the sale that the wala' of Barirah would belong to them if she freed her after purchase. Aisha informed the Prophet (PBUH), and he said: 'What is the matter with people who stipulate conditions not in the Book of Allah? Whoever stipulates a condition not in the Book of Allah, it is not for him, even if he stipulates a hundred conditions. Allah's condition is more binding and reliable.' Narrated by Bukhari in the Book of Sales and the Book of Mukatabah. And the reason this condition contradicts the objectives of Sharia, as it appears, is that Islamic law aims to encourage people to manumit slaves, and for this it opened many doors, some obligatory and some optional. Therefore, it made the right of inheritance by wala' for the one who frees and then for his 'asaba heirs after him, as an encouragement to manumission. So if one stipulates on the buyer of a slave that if he frees him, his wala' belongs to the seller, this condition would prompt the buyer to retain ownership of the sold slave and not free him, thus disrupting the purpose of the Lawgiver and His legal policy in a matter and ruling connected to the objectives of Sharia. 3 - It is also narrated that the Prophet (PBUH) forbade a sale with a condition - this is narrated in summary as a story, and this text is not unanimously agreed upon as authentic and correctly attributed to the Prophet as we will see (ref: section 4/42). Nevertheless, it is also established in the practical Sunnah that the Prophet (PBUH) traveled and bought a camel from Jabir ibn Abdullah and stipulated for Jabir its back until Medina, i.e., he stipulated for him the right to ride it after the sale until he reached Medina. (Ref: Bidayat al-Mujtahid 2/132, Fath al-Qadir Sharh al-Hidayah, chapter on invalid sale 6/76). 4 - Similarly, the Prophet (PBUH) established the characteristic of trustworthiness (amanah) as an original ruling in loan (ariyah) by his saying: 'There is no liability on the borrower who is not dishonest.' (1) Yet, it is established in the practical Sunnah that he (PBUH) borrowed armor and weapons from Safwan ibn Umayyah due to the Muslims' need in the Battle of Hunayn, and he stipulated for him its guarantee by saying: 'A guaranteed and repaid loan.' (2). (1) 'Al-mughall' (with damm on meem and shaddah on lam) is the active participle from 'ighlal' which is dishonesty in wealth (an-Nihaya, al-Misbah). The hadith is narrated by al-Daraqutni; al-Hafiz said in al-Talkhis 3/97: 'Its chain contains two weak narrators.' Al-Daraqutni also narrated it through another weak chain with the wording: 'No liability on the trustee.' (2) This allowed room for some ijtihad to hold that the characteristic of trustworthiness in loan is -

Commentary
This passage discusses the authority of contracting parties to modify the effects of contracts through conditions. The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, opens by stating that Sharia rulings come directly from Quran and Sunnah or through ijtihad. He then focuses on the authority of parties to alter contractual effects by adding or reducing obligations via conditions. He presents four categories of evidence: (1) General Quranic verses (not detailed here but referenced from earlier paragraphs). (2) Two key hadith: 'Muslims are bound by their conditions' and 'Every condition not in the Book of Allah is void.' He explains that 'Book of Allah' here does not mean the Quran but Allah's legislation, so conditions contradicting Sharia objectives are void. He illustrates with the story of Barirah: the sellers wanted to condition that wala' (inheritance right) remain with them after Aisha freed her. The Prophet rejected this because it contradicts the Sharia objective of encouraging manumission. (3) A weak hadith forbidding a sale with a condition, but the practical Sunnah shows the Prophet himself entered a sale with a condition (buying a camel and allowing the seller to ride it). (4) The general rule that a borrowed item is an amanah (trust) not subject to guarantee, but the Prophet in practice guaranteed borrowed armor due to need. This shows that general principles can be modified by specific conditions when necessary, as long as they do not contravene higher Sharia objectives. The passage thus establishes that the default permissibility of conditions is restricted only when they oppose the maqasid al-shariah.

Key Terms
عارية (ariyah): A loan of a fungible item for use, to be returned after use.
ولاء (wala'): The right of inheritance that a manumitter has over the freed slave, as an incentive for freeing slaves.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
12 min
Scope of Contractual Conditions and Maqasid al-Shari'ah
Examining the scope of contractual conditions in light of prophetic practice and maqasid al-shari'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criteria for valid and invalid contractual conditions by analyzing Prophetic practice and Maqasid al-Shari'ah.

Core Idea
Contractual conditions are binding unless they contradict Maqasid al-Shari'ah.

In this passage, Mustafa al-Zarqa examines key Prophetic traditions to delineate the boundaries of contractual freedom. Through examples of conditional sale, borrowed goods, and the wala' condition, he shows that the Shari'ah balances party autonomy with higher objectives. This analysis is essential for muftis to determine which conditions are enforceable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three Prophetic examples
Locate the three cases: (1) Prophet's conditional sale of a camel; (2) Prophet's guaranteed borrowing of armor; (3) The story of Barirah and the wala' condition.
Each example illustrates a different aspect of contractual conditions.
2
For each example, determine the default ruling and the exception
Identify the default legal ruling (e.g., sale without condition, ariyah as trust) and the exception created by the Prophet's action or statement.
The Prophetic practice shows that exceptions to general rulings are possible through stipulation.
3
Analyze the conflict with Maqasid in the Barirah case
Understand why the wala' condition was invalid: it contradicted the Shari'ah's objective of encouraging emancipation by removing the incentive to free slaves.
Maqasid override party agreement when fundamental objectives are at stake.
4
Extract the two overarching principles
From the analysis, al-Zarqa derives two categories: conditions that are a field for party will (valid) and conditions that are prohibited because they affect fundamental rulings and Maqasid.
This provides a framework for muftis to evaluate any contractual condition.

الفصل (٤١) سلطان الإرادة العقدية - نظرة عامة
٨/٤١ - فهذه النصوص وأمثالها مما ورد في السنة عن مدى اعتبار الشروط العقدية تفيد :
أولاً: أنَّ هناك شروطاً تعتبر ميداناً لإرادة العاقدين أطلق فيه الشرع لإرادتهم سلطانها ضمن حدود حقوقهم في أحكام والتزامات تثبت بالعقد بين الطرفين على أساسها ما لم يشترط العقد المسمى قائماً مبدئياً : ويعتبر خلافها .

Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh
3 - It was also narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade a sale with a condition, but this is reported summarily by way of story, and this text is not agreed upon as authentic and soundly attributed to the Prophet as we will see. Nevertheless, it is also established in the practical Sunnah that the Prophet (peace be upon him) bought a camel from Jabir ibn Abdullah during a journey and stipulated for Jabir its back to Medina, i.e., he stipulated for him the right to ride it after the sale until he reached Medina.
4 - Likewise, the Prophet (peace be upon him) established the characteristic of trustworthiness as a original ruling in borrowing (ariyah) by his saying: 'There is no liability on the borrower except the one who misappropriates.' Nevertheless, it is established in the practical Sunnah that he (peace be upon him) borrowed armor and weapons from Safwan ibn Umayyah for the Muslims' need in the battle of Hunayn, and stipulated for him their guarantee by saying: 'A guaranteed loan, to be returned.'
[The story of] Barirah: that they stipulated to Aisha in the sale that the wala' (patronage) of Barirah would belong to them if she [Aisha] emancipated her after purchase. So Aisha informed the Prophet (peace be upon him) about that, and he said: 'What is the matter with people who stipulate conditions that are not in the Book of Allah? Whoever stipulates a condition not in the Book of Allah, it is not valid for him, even if he stipulates a hundred conditions. The condition of Allah is more binding and more reliable.' The reason this condition conflicts with the objectives of Shari'ah, as it appears, is that Islamic law aims to encourage people to emancipate slaves, and it has opened doors for that, including obligatory and optional means. Therefore, it made the right of inheritance by wala' for the one who emancipates, then for his agnatic heirs after him, as an encouragement for emancipation. So if it is stipulated to the buyer of a slave that if he emancipates him, his wala' belongs to the seller, this condition would induce the buyer to retain ownership of the sold slave and not emancipate him, thus disrupting the Legislator's purpose and legal policy in a matter and ruling connected to the objectives of Shari'ah.

Chapter (41): The Authority of Contractual Will - An Overview
8/41 - These texts and their like from the Sunnah regarding the consideration of contractual conditions indicate:
First: That there are conditions that are a field for the will of the contracting parties; the Lawgiver has given their will authority within the limits of their rights in rulings and obligations established by the contract between the parties on that basis, as long as no contrary condition is stipulated, and the named contract remains fundamentally valid.
Second: That there are conditions prohibited by law, with no authority for the will of the contracting parties, because they affect fundamental rulings that are considered part of the objectives of Islamic Shari'ah and its general system.
Source pages p. 515

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical analysis of contractual conditions (shurut) in Islamic law. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, examines three Prophetic traditions to illustrate the tension between general principles and exceptions, then extracts two overarching principles.

The first tradition concerns the prohibition of 'sale with a condition' (bai' wa shart). While a hadith reports this prohibition, al-Zarqa notes its weak authenticity. Moreover, the practical Sunnah shows the Prophet himself making a conditional sale: he bought a camel from Jabir and stipulated that Jabir could ride it to Medina. This indicates that not all conditional sales are prohibited; some are permissible if they serve a legitimate purpose.

The second tradition establishes that a borrowed item (ariyah) is held as a trust (amanah); the borrower is not liable for loss or damage unless he transgresses. Yet, in the Battle of Hunayn, the Prophet borrowed armor from Safwan and explicitly guaranteed its return ('ariyah madduna). This shows that the default ruling of trust can be altered by agreement, making the borrower liable if so stipulated.

The third case is the famous story of Barirah. When Barirah was sold to Aisha with the condition that her wala' (patronage right) remain with the sellers, the Prophet rejected this condition because it contravened the Shari'ah's objective of encouraging emancipation. The wala' right belongs to the emancipator, not the seller. Stipulating otherwise discourages the buyer from freeing the slave, undermining the maqsad (objective) of freeing slaves. Thus, such a condition is void even if agreed upon.

From these examples, al-Zarqa concludes two categories:
1. Conditions that are a legitimate field for the parties' will: as long as the core contract (e.g., sale) remains valid, parties may stipulate additional rights and obligations within their mutual rights. These conditions are binding.
2. Conditions that are prohibited: if they contradict fundamental Shari'ah objectives or its general system, the parties have no authority to stipulate them, and such conditions are null and void.

This analysis reflects the Hanafi approach to contractual freedom, balancing the general principle of permissibility (ibaha) with restrictions based on maqasid al-shari'ah.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A condition stipulated by one or both contracting parties in a contract, which may add, modify, or restrict obligations.
عارية (ariyah): A contract of loan for use, where one party gives an item to another for benefit without compensation, with the obligation to return it.
ولاء (wala'): The right of patronage (inheritance) that belongs to the emancipator of a slave, which cannot be sold or transferred.
مقاصد الشريعة (maqasid al-shari'ah): The higher objectives and purposes of Islamic law, such as preservation of religion, life, intellect, lineage, and property, and in this context, the encouragement of slave emancipation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the story of Barirah, why did the Prophet reject the condition that the wala' of Barirah remains with the sellers?
A) Because the condition was not written down.
B) Because it contradicted the purpose of encouraging emancipation.
C) Because the sellers were non-Muslims.
D) Because the condition was unclear.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position: contractual conditions are valid unless they conflict with Maqasid al-Shari'ah, as illustrated by the prohibition of selling wala'.
