Two Categories of Contractual Conditions
Defines two categories of contractual conditions based on Sharia texts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two categories of contractual conditions derived from Sharia texts and apply the distinction to classify a given condition as permissible or prohibited.

Core Idea
Sharia grants contractual will authority unless the condition contradicts essential objectives (maqasid).

This lesson extracts from the Sunnah a foundational binary classification of contractual conditions. For a mufti, this dichotomy is the first filter when evaluating any stipulation in a contract: does it fall within the freedom of the parties or does it violate the Sharia's essential rulings?

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of the categories
Note that the passage derives these categories from 'these texts and their like' in the Sunnah. Understand that the classification is text-based, not arbitrary.
The texts mentioned are not quoted here but referenced.
2
Define the first category
Conditions where the parties' will is given full authority within the limits of their rights and obligations, as long as the contract type remains intact and no contradictory condition is stipulated.
This category covers most standard contractual stipulations.
3
Define the second category
Conditions that are prohibited because they tamper with essential rulings that are part of Sharia objectives (maqasid) and its general system.
These conditions are void and cannot be made binding.
4
Internalize the boundary
The boundary is not arbitrary; it is determined by whether the condition conflicts with the contract's essence or a primary Sharia goal.
Examples would be conditions that legalize the prohibited or prohibit the lawful.

٥٤٥
Chapter (41) Authority of Contractual Will - General Overview
8/41 - These texts and their like from the Sunnah concerning the extent of consideration of contractual conditions indicate:
First: That there are conditions considered a field for the will of the contracting parties, in which the Lawgiver has given their will authority within the limits of their rights in rulings and obligations established by the contract between the parties on that basis, as long as the named contract exists in principle. And its opposite is considered.
Second: That there are conditions prohibited by the Lawgiver, in which the will of the contracting parties has no authority, because they touch upon essential rulings that are among the objectives of Islamic Sharia and its general system.
=
One [view: 'ariyah is a trust by] origin, but it becomes guaranteed if the borrower is conditioned to guarantee it, combining between the two proofs; and this is the best and most appropriate ijtihad regarding the nature of 'ariyah, although it is not the well-known [view]. It is one of two opinions in Hanafi ijtihad. The well-known schools hold that it is an absolute trust based on the first hadith, and upon this is the Maliki ijtihad and the preponderant view in Hanafi ijtihad.
Ref: Kitab al-'Ariyah in al-Mughni by Muwaffaq al-Din ibn Qudamah al-Hanbali 5/354-356, and Bidayat al-Mujtahid 2/263, and al-Kifayah Sharh al-Hidayah in Hanafi fiqh 7/468-469.
The second hadith is narrated by Abu Dawud from Safwan ibn Umayyah that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) borrowed armor from him on the day of Khaybar and said: 'Is it a seizure, O Muhammad?' He said: 'No, rather a guaranteed loan.' And Ahmad, al-Nasa'i, and al-Hakim narrated it from Ibn 'Abbas with the wording: 'Rather a loan to be returned.' And Ahmad and al-Nasa'i added: 'Some of it was lost, and he offered to compensate him for it, but he said: Today, O Messenger of Allah, I am more desirous of Islam.' (al-Talkhis al-Habir 3/52-53).
545
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement extracted from textual evidence in the Sunnah. It opens by summarizing two fundamental categories of contractual conditions derived from Sharia texts. First, there are conditions where the parties' will is given full authority within the limits of their rights and obligations, as long as the contract type remains intact and the condition does not contradict its essence. Second, there are conditions that are absolutely prohibited because they violate core Sharia objectives (maqasid al-shariah) and the general legal order. The author then provides an illustrative example from the law of loan ('ariyah) to demonstrate how differing textual evidence leads to different ijtihad positions within the Hanafi school and between madhahib. The issue is whether a borrowed item is inherently a trust (amanah) or can be made guaranteed (madhmunah) by a condition. The author presents a Hanafi opinion that combines the proofs: by default it is a trust, but if the lender stipulates the borrower's liability, it becomes guaranteed. This is presented as the most balanced ijtihad, though not the most famous. The Maliki school and the preferred Hanafi view hold it as an absolute trust.

Key Terms
سلطان الإرادة العقدية (sultan al-irada al-'aqdiyya): The authority or power of the contracting parties' will to create legal effects within the limits set by Sharia.
شروط عقدية (shurut 'aqdiyya): Conditions stipulated by the parties in a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for prohibiting certain conditions?
A) They contradict explicit Quranic verses.
B) They touch upon essential rulings that are part of Sharia objectives and general system.
C) They are not mentioned in the Sunnah.
D) They are deemed unfair by scholars.

Key Takeaway
Contractual conditions are valid unless they violate a fundamental Sharia objective.

The 'Ariyah (Loan) Dispute: Trust vs. Guaranteed by Condition
Expands on the limits of contractual will through the example of 'ariyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the Hanafi and Maliki positions on whether a loaned item ('ariyah) can be made guaranteed by a condition and identify the preponderant view in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
The 'ariyah is normally a trust, but a condition can make it guaranteed according to one Hanafi ijtihad, though the majority holds it as absolute trust.

This lesson examines a specific contractual condition dispute that exemplifies how differing proof texts lead to variant opinions. The case of 'ariyah illustrates the limits of contractual will and the method of reconciling contradictory evidences.

Guided Path
1
State the default ruling for 'ariyah
All schools agree that by default, the borrower is a trustee (amin) and not liable for loss unless there is transgression.
2
Identify the conflicting proof
The hadith of Safwan ibn Umayyah where the Prophet borrowed armor and called it 'a guaranteed loan' suggests that a guarantee can be imposed by condition.
This hadith is from Abu Dawud and others.
3
Understand the Hanafi reconciliation
One Hanafi ijtihad combines the two proofs: 'ariyah is a trust by origin, but if the lender stipulates guarantee, it becomes guaranteed. This honors both the default trust and the prophetic action.
This is described as 'the best and most appropriate ijtihad' but not the most famous.
4
Note the preponderant view
The majority of Hanafi scholars and the Maliki school hold that 'ariyah is an absolute trust regardless of stipulation, based on the general rule that it is a trust.
The preponderant Hanafi view and Maliki view align.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under the combined view, if the borrower is conditioned to guarantee, he is liable for loss; under the absolute trust view, he remains a trustee even with the condition.
Practical consequence for fatwa.

Ijtihad on Contractual Will: The Source of Contractual Effects
Explains agreement and disagreement on contractual freedom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that the effects of contracts are determined by Sharia, not the parties, and identify the complementary role of ijtihad through qiyas, istihsan, istislah, and sadd al-dhara'i'.

Core Idea
Contracts are legal causes whose effects are fixed by the Lawgiver, not the parties.

This passage from al-Zarqa clarifies that while Islamic ijtihad unanimously upholds the freedom and binding force of contracts, there is near-consensus that the legal effects—such as ownership transfer or liability—are determined by Sharia, not by the contracting parties. This principle is foundational for muftis evaluating contractual stipulations and for understanding the limits of party autonomy in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four aspects of contractual freedom
Recall from the previous chapter that the discussion revolves around four aspects: (1) freedom to contract, (2) its consensual nature, (3) arrangement of effects, and (4) regulatory texts. The passage addresses the first three.
2
Note consensus on the first two aspects
All ijtihad agree on freedom of contract, its consensual basis, and the binding force of a legitimate contract.
This is undisputed among schools.
3
Grasp the general principle on the third aspect
The majority agree that the effects and rulings of contracts are the work of the Lawgiver (Sharia), not the parties. The parties cannot arbitrarily assign consequences.
This is the core legal maxim: the effects are from Sharia.
4
Understand the rationale: contract as sabab shar'i
A contract is a legal cause (sabab shar'i) that Sharia has designed to produce specific results. Since Sharia creates the cause, it also determines its effects.
This links to usul al-fiqh: the hukm follows the sabab.
5
Recognize the complementary role of ijtihad
Although the basic effects are fixed, ijtihad elaborates on them for new cases via qiyas, istihsan, istislah, and sadd al-dhara'i'—methods accepted by different schools.
This explains how fiqh expands without compromising the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني والأربعون
الاجتهادات الإسلامية في سلطان الإرادة العقدية
١/٤٢ - أمام مجموعة النصوص الواردة في النواحي الأربع لحرية التعاقد المبينة في مطلع الفصل السابق يجد الباحث عن موقف الاجتهادات الإسلامية أنها متفقة في بعض هذه النواحي ومختلفة في بعض
أ - ففي الناحيتين الأولى والثانية اتفقت الاجتهادات على مبدأ حرية التعاقد ورضائيته، وعلى القوة الإلزامية للعقد المشروع.
ب - وفي الناحية الثالثة وهي ترتيب آثار العقود، تكاد تكون جمهرة الاجتهادات متفقة على مبدأ عام وهو أن ترتيب آثار العقود وأحكامها إنما الأصل من عمل الشارع لا من عمل المتعاقدين، فالشارع هو الذي ينظم هذه العقود ونتائجها في حقوق الناس، ويجعل كل عقد طريقاً إلى نتائج معينة يرتبها عليه .
وللاجتهاد في هذا السبيل عمل متمم في التفريع على ما أثبتته نصوص الشريعة من أحكام العقود بالطرق التي فتحها الشرع نفسه أمام الاجتهاد في غير المنصوص عليه أي بطريق القياس أو الاستحسان أو الاستصلاح أو سد الذرائع، على اختلاف الاجتهادات في بعض هذه الطرق
كما تقدم إيضاحه في بحث الاستصلاح أول الكتاب.
ومبنى النظر الفقهي في كون ترتيب آثار العقود من عمل الشارع هو أن العقد سبب شرعي، فهو طريق يرسمه الشرع ليصل بسالكيه إلى نتائج حقوقية يقرها بينهم فإلى الشارع يعود تحديد هذه النتائج ؛ وإنما تستمد
٥٤٧
Chapter Forty-Two
Islamic Ijtihad on Authority of Contractual Will
1/42 - Faced with the set of texts on the four aspects of freedom of contract mentioned at the beginning of the previous chapter, the researcher finds that the position of Islamic ijtihad is that they agree on some of these aspects and differ on others.
a) In the first and second aspects, ijtihad agreed on the principle of freedom of contract and its consensual nature, and on the binding force of a legitimate contract.
b) In the third aspect, which is the arrangement of the effects of contracts, the majority of ijtihad are almost in agreement on a general principle: that the arrangement of the effects and rulings of contracts is essentially the work of the Lawgiver, not the work of the contracting parties. The Lawgiver is the one who regulates these contracts and their results in the rights of people, and makes each contract a path to specific results that he attaches to it.
And ijtihad in this regard has a complementary role in branching out upon what the texts of Sharia have established regarding the rulings of contracts through the methods that the Lawgiver himself opened for ijtihad in matters not explicitly stated, that is, by way of qiyas, istihsan, istislah, or sadd al-dhara'i', despite differences in ijtihad regarding some of these methods.
As previously explained in the discussion of istislah at the beginning of the book.
The basis of the fiqhi view that the arrangement of effects of contracts is the work of the Lawgiver is that the contract is a legal cause (sabab shar'i); it is a path drawn by the Sharia for those who follow it to reach legal results that it establishes among them. So it is the Lawgiver who determines these results; they are derived only.
547
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage is an analytical discussion of the positions of Islamic ijtihad on the authority of the will in contracts. It identifies three of four aspects mentioned earlier: freedom of contract, its consensual nature, and the arrangement of legal effects. On the first two, all ijtihad are unanimous: contracts are free and consensual, and once valid, binding. On the third—the determination of contractual effects—the vast majority agree that it is the Lawgiver (Sharia) that defines the rights and obligations arising from each contract type, not the parties. The parties cannot arbitrarily assign effects. However, ijtihad has a complementary role in deriving rulings for new situations through accepted legal reasoning methods: qiyas (analogy), istihsan (juristic preference), istislah (public interest), and sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking pretexts). The author notes that different schools differ in use of these methods. The rationale is that contracts are legal causes (asbab shar'iyya) designed by the Lawgiver to produce specific legal results; hence the Lawgiver dictates those results. This explains why in Hanafi fiqh, the effects of a sale, lease, etc., are fixed by Sharia and not subject to party discretion.

Key Terms
حرية التعاقد (hurriyat al-ta'aqud): The principle that parties are free to enter into contracts and determine their terms within Sharia limits.
آثار العقود (athar al-'uqud): The legal effects and outcomes produced by a contract, such as transfer of ownership, obligation to pay, etc.
سبب شرعي (sabab shar'i): A legal cause or means that Sharia has designated to produce specific rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the principle that the arrangement of effects of contracts is the work of the Lawgiver?
A) The majority of scholars said so.
B) The contract is a sabab shar'i, so the Lawgiver determines its results.
C) The texts explicitly state all effects.
D) Parties cannot be trusted to define their own effects.

Key Takeaway
The effects of contracts are determined by Sharia, not by the contracting parties, and ijtihad plays a complementary role in deriving subsidiary rulings through recognized methods.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Classification of Ijtihadat on Party Autonomy in Contractual Conditions
Classifying juristic opinions on the scope of contractual conditions in Islamic law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify ijtihadat on contractual conditions into the two main groups, explain their theoretical basis, and apply the classification to analyze a contractual condition from a Hanafi mufti's perspective.

Core Idea
The Lawgiver delegates limited authority to modify contract effects; ijtihadat differ on how much freedom the parties have.

In Islamic law, the binding force of a contract comes from the Lawgiver, not the parties. However, there is a spectrum of opinions on how far parties can modify the default rulings of a contract through conditions. This lesson introduces you to the two major juristic orientations—restriction (muqtada al-'aqd) and freedom—and their implications for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups of ijtihadat
Read the passage carefully and note the two groups: (1) those that consider the original rule to be restriction, adhering to muqtada al-'aqd; (2) those that consider the original rule to be freedom (itlaq).
The first group sees fixed essential effects for each contract; the second allows more party discretion.
2
Understand the theory of muqtada al-'aqd
Analyze how the first group derives this theory: each contract has basic rulings prescribed by the Lawgiver or derived by ijtihad to preserve balance; parties may not violate these essential effects.
This is the stricter view, often associated with the Zahiri school and some Hanbali positions.
3
Grasp the opposing view: freedom (itlaq)
The second group holds that the original rule is freedom unless there is a clear prohibition. They allow parties to negotiate conditions broadly, as long as they do not contradict explicit texts.
Hanafi school generally leans toward this view, but with limits via istihsan and custom.
4
Locate the common ground
Note the author's emphasis that in all ijtihadat, the binding force of contracts comes from the Lawgiver, not the parties. The dispute is only about the extent of delegated authority.
This reconciles the two views as a matter of degree, not source of obligation.
5
Apply to a fatwa scenario
Consider a case where a buyer stipulates that the seller will repurchase the item at a specified future price. Determine whether such a condition is valid under each group's approach.
Under muqtada al-'aqd, this might violate the essential effect of immediate ownership transfer; under freedom, it may be valid if mutually agreed.

الباب السادس: نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
نظاماً
إرادة العاقدين سلطانها منه بالمقدار والحدود التي يحددها لها. فإنَّ للشرع يحميه، فهو الكفيل بإقامة الحدود بين الحقوق الخاصة وإرادات أصحابها من جهة وبين المصالح العامة وإرادة الشارع من جهة أخرى، كما أنَّ الشارع أيضاً هو الكفيل بتنظيم تلك الحقوق الخاصة بين الناس بناء على مباشرتهم الأسبابها حفظاً للتوازن ومنعاً للتغابن، وضبطاً لنظام التعامل
ج - أما الناحية الرابعة، وهي مدى حرية الشروط العقدية، أو مدى سلطان الإرادة في تحديد آثار العقد بين عاقديه، فإن الاجتهادات الإسلامية المشهورة على اختلاف أساسي واسع الشقة فيها .
الفصل (٤٢) الاجتهادات في سلطان الإرادة العقدية
ويمكن تصنيف الاجتهادات - ذات الشأن في هذا الموضوع - إلى زمرتين :
١ - الاجتهادات التي تعتبر أن الأصل الشرعي في حرية الشروط العقدية هو التقييد ؛ وهي الاجتهادات التي تمسكت بنظرية مقتضى العقد

(أولاً) الاجتهادات التي تمسكت بنظرية مقتضى العقد
هذه الاجتهادات ترى أن لكل عقد في الشرع أحكاماً أساسية تسمى: مقتضى العقد، نصَّ عليها الشرع مباشرة، أو استنبطها الاجتهاد وأثبتها حفظاً للتوازن بين العاقدين في الحقوق. فليس للعاقدين أن يشترطا من الشروط ما يخالف هذا المقتضى. فإن اشترطا شرطاً مخالفاً له = اعتبار أن إرادة العاقد لا إرادة الشارع، بل هي في ظل كل تشريع يجب تعتبر من عمل الشارع لا من عمل العاقد، لأن ترتب حكم العقد وأثره على العقد وإلزام العاقد به كشريعة له إنما هو اعتبار تشريعي صرف وكل إلزام للعاقد بنتيجة عقده لا يكون لولا إيجاب الشارع وإرادته الملزمة. وهذا نظر فقهائنا كما سلف بيانه، ولا يصح خلافه في الشرائع القانونية الوضعية. فالعقد يعتبر شريعة خاصة بين العاقدين في الفقه الإسلامي أيضاً ما دام فيه أن العقد قوة ملزمة شرعاً لعاقديه. وليس الفارق بين التشريعين في هذا الموضوع إلا مدى ما فوّضه الشارع إلى العاقدين من سلطان على تعديل الأحكام التي قرّرها التشريع مبدئياً في كل عقد. وهذا يعني مدى حرية الشروط العقدية المعدلة لتلك الأحكام، وليس معناه أن العاقدين هما اللذان يشرعان آثار العقد ويوجبانها بينهما . فقد يخلو العقد من كل شرط، وعندئذ لا شك في أن الأحكام التي تثبت إنما هي ما قرره التشريع من آثار لهذا العقد؛ فهل يعقل أن يقال: إن العقد تارة يوجب العاقدان آثاره بينهما، وتارة يوجبها التشريع ؟! أن الاجتهادات المختلفة في الفقه الإسلامي بعضها يضيق حرية الشروط العقدية، وبعضها يوسعها توسيعاً على غرار ما استقرت عليه المبادىء القانونية الحديثة وسنرى في ذلك .
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh
Systematically: The will of the contracting parties has its authority from Him [the Lawgiver] to the extent and limits that He determines for it. For the Lawgiver protects it, and He is the guarantor of establishing the boundaries between private rights and the wills of their owners on one hand, and between public interests and the Will of the Lawgiver on the other. Also, the Lawgiver is the guarantor of organizing those private rights among people based on their undertaking the causes thereof, to preserve balance, prevent exploitation, and regulate the system of dealings.
C. As for the fourth aspect: the extent of freedom of contractual conditions, or the extent of the authority of the will in determining the effects of the contract between its parties, the well-known Islamic ijtihadat differ fundamentally with a wide gap.
Chapter (42): The Ijtihadat on the Authority of Contractual Will
And the ijtihadat concerning this subject can be classified into two groups:
1. The ijtihadat that consider the original rule in the freedom of contractual conditions to be restriction; these are the ijtihadat that adhered to the theory of the inherent requirement of the contract (muqtada al-'aqd).
2. The ijtihadat that consider the original rule in those conditions to be freedom (itlaq).
(First): The ijtihadat that adhered to the theory of the inherent requirement of the contract
These ijtihadat see that each contract has basic rulings in the Law called: the inherent requirement of the contract (muqtada al-'aqd), which the Lawgiver directly prescribed, or ijtihad derived and established to preserve balance between the contracting parties in rights. So the parties may not stipulate conditions that contradict this inherent requirement. If they stipulate a condition contrary to it, [it is invalid because] consideration is that the will of the party is not the will of the Lawgiver; rather, under every legislation, it must be considered an act of the Lawgiver, not an act of the party, because the attachment of the legal ruling and its effect to the contract and binding the party to it as a law for him is purely a legislative consideration, and any binding of the party to the result of his contract would not occur without the imposition of the Lawgiver and His binding will. This is the view of our jurists, as previously explained, and it cannot be otherwise in positive legal systems. So the contract is considered a special law between the contracting parties in Islamic jurisprudence as well, as long as the contract has a legally binding force for its parties. And the difference between the two legislations in this subject is only the extent to which the Lawgiver has delegated to the parties authority to modify the rulings that the legislation has initially established for each contract. This means the extent of freedom of contractual conditions that modify those rulings, and it does not mean that the parties themselves legislate the effects of the contract and make them binding between them. For the contract may be free of any condition, and then there is no doubt that the rulings that are established are only those which the legislation has determined as effects for this contract; so is it reasonable to say that sometimes the parties impose the effects of their contract, and sometimes the legislation imposes them?! Rather, the different ijtihadat in Islamic jurisprudence: some narrow the freedom of contractual conditions, and some broaden it extensively, similar to what has become settled in modern legal principles. And we will see what is said in that regard.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's work analyzes the juristic opinions on the scope of party autonomy in contractual conditions. The author first reiterates that the effects of contracts are determined by the Lawgiver, not the parties, establishing that the will of the contracting parties operates within the limits set by the Lawgiver. He then introduces the fourth aspect: the freedom of contractual conditions (shurut 'aqdiyya). He classifies the ijtihadat into two main groups. The first group holds that the original rule (al-asl al-shar'i) regarding freedom of conditions is restriction (al-taqyid); these ijtihadat adhere to the theory of muqtada al-'aqd (the inherent requirements of the contract). According to this view, every contract has essential rulings prescribed directly by the Lawgiver or derived by ijtihad to maintain balance between the parties; parties cannot stipulate conditions that violate these essential rulings. The second group holds that the original rule is freedom (al-itlaq), allowing parties more latitude. The author then explains that the fundamental difference is not about who creates the contract's effects—the Lawgiver always does—but about the extent to which the Lawgiver has delegated authority to the parties to modify the default rulings. He emphasizes that even in Islamic jurisprudence, the contract is a 'special law' (shari'a khassa) between the parties, but its binding force comes from the Lawgiver, not the parties themselves. The passage concludes by noting that some ijtihadat narrow this freedom, while others broaden it, similar to modern legal principles. This passage is foundational for understanding the Hanafi position on contractual conditions, which generally leans toward the second group (freedom) based on istihsan and custom, though the Hanafi school also imposes limits via the concept of muqtada al-'aqd in specific cases.

Key Terms
مقتضى العقد (muqtada al-'aqd): The essential rulings inherent in a contract, as prescribed by the Lawgiver, which parties cannot alter without violating the contract's purpose.
سلطان الإرادة (sultan al-irada): The authority of the contracting parties' will to determine the effects of their contract, subject to the limits set by the Lawgiver.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Freedom of conditions is the original rule.
B) Restriction (al-taqyid) is the original rule.
C) All conditions are prohibited.
D) There is no original rule.

Key Takeaway
The classification of ijtihadat on contractual conditions into restriction (muqtada al-'aqd) and freedom (itlaq) reveals that the core issue is the extent of the Lawgiver's delegation, not the source of obligation.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Hanafi Doctrine on Contractual Conditions: Balancing Muqtada, Custom, and Balance
Comparative analysis of madhahib on contractual conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on conditions in financial commutative contracts and identify which conditions are valid under the three exceptions (textual permission, harmony with contract, valid custom).

Core Idea
A condition that gives extra benefit without countervalue corrupts the contract — unless text, harmony, or custom allows it.

The Hanafi school holds a nuanced position on contractual conditions. While generally prohibiting conditions that contradict the inherent implications (muqtada) of a contract in financial exchanges, it carves out three important exceptions. This lesson unpacks these exceptions and shows how custom (urf) can validate conditions that would otherwise be invalid. Understanding this framework is essential for a mufti to issue correct fatwas on modern contracts.

Guided Path
1
Understand muqtada al-aqd
Muqtada al-aqd refers to the default legal consequences that shariah attaches to a contract, such as transfer of ownership in sale. These are binding and cannot be altered without a valid reason.
Think of it as the 'default package' of rights and duties.
2
Identify a contradictory condition (shart munafi)
A condition is contradictory if it provides an extra benefit to one party beyond what the contract normally gives, without a countervalue (iwad). For example, stipulating that the seller bears transport cost in a sale.
Such conditions are considered mufsida (corrupting) in financial commutative contracts.
3
Apply the general rule
In financial contracts (sale, ijarah, etc.), a contradictory condition invalidates the entire contract. In non-financial contracts (marriage), the condition is void but the contract remains valid.
This is a key distinction: the effect differs by contract type.
4
Check the three exceptions
The Hanafis permit conditions that are: (a) explicitly allowed by shariah (e.g., khiyar al-shart, deferring price), (b) harmonious with the contract (e.g., demanding a guarantor for a deferred price, as it secures the right), or (c) established by valid custom (urf).
Custom must be consistent and not contrary to a clear text.
5
Determine the role of custom in restoring balance
Custom validates conditions because it implies the parties have factored the extra benefit into the countervalue. For example, if it is customary for the seller to repair defects after sale, the condition is valid because the price already accounts for that service.
This restores the equilibrium (tawazun) that the condition originally disrupted.

Source Text

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
يفسد العقد، كما لو اشترط الأجير العام من صباغ أو خياط مثلاً أن لا يضمن ما يتلف بصنعه (ر: ف ٦/٤) .
وهذا مبنى الاجتهاد الحنفي في الشروط العقدية. ويقاربه في ذلك نظر جمهور فقهاء المالكية، ثم الاجتهاد الشافعي .
فهذه الاجتهادات قد أخذت مبدئياً بنظرية مقتضى العقد، وتمسك أصحابها بفكرته إجمالاً، ثم اختلفوا في التفصيل وتشعبت أنظارهم. والاجتهاد الشافعي أكثرها تشدّداً وتضييقاً لحرية الشروط.
فمقتضيات العقود كلها في نظرهم إنما تثبت بدليل شرعي. وليس للعاقد أن يخالفها أو يضيف إليها شيئاً أو يقيدها بقيد إلا إذا قام دليل شرعي يجيز التزامه ويوجب الوفاء به كاشتراط تأجيل الثمن في البيع، وتأجيل المهر في النكاح.
وقد انفرد الإمام مالك برأي في الشروط التي تفسد العقود، وهو أن الشرط الذي ينافي مقتضى العقد ويفسده إذا تنازل عنه شارطه فلم يتمسك به صح العقد ولغا الشرط؛ وهو يبني ذلك على نظرية المانع (ر: ف ۲۸/ فيعتبر الشرط المنافي مانعاً من صحة العقد، فإذا زال المانع عاد الممنوع (ر) بداية المجتهد (۱۳۵۲) و نظرية العقد للأستاذ أبي زهرة ف/ ١٤٩).
٣ - موقف الاجتهاد الحنفي في هذا المقام :
والاجتهاد الحنفي يعتبر كل شرط متضمن لمنفعة زائدة على أصل مقتضى العقد مخالفاً ومفسداً لعقد المعاوضة المالية؛ كما لو اشترط في البيع حمل المبيع على حساب البائع أو إقراض أحد المتبايعين للآخر قرضاً، ونحو ذلك.
أما إذا كان ذلك الشرط في عقود غير المعاوضات المالية كالزواج فيلغو الشرط بنفسه ولا يؤثر في العقد.
ولكن الاجتهاد الحنفي يستثني من المنع ثلاثة أنواع من الشروط فيعتبرها صحيحة لازمة :
۱ - الشرط الذي ورد الشرع بجوازه کاشتراط تأجيل ثمن المبيع واشتراط الخيار لأحد المتبايعين وهو المسمى : خيار الشرط»، وما يقاس على ذلك كخيار النقد وخيار التعيين
۲ - الشرط الذي يلائم العقد كاشتراط البائع على المشتري تقديم كفيل أو رهن بالثمن المؤجّل، لأنه توثيق له ر ف ٥/٣٧).
٣ - الشرط الذي يجري به العرف المعتبر شرعاً، فإن العرف يصحح الشروط التي تعتبر في الأصل مفسدة للعقد.
فلو تعارف الناس في بعض الأمكنة مثلاً بيع البضاعة بشرط حملها على حساب البائع أو بشرط قيام البائع بتصليح الآلة المبيعة إذا اختلت، صح ذلك .
والاجتهاد الحنفي يبني موقفه من حرية الشروط العقدية على شيء من المبالغة والتوسع في التمسك بمبدأ التوازن بين حقوق العاقدين الناشئة بالعقد؛ فيرى أنَّ اشتراط منفعة لجانب أحد العاقدين يخل بالتوازن الواجب ويؤدي إلى النزاع، لأنها منفعة زائدة على أصل مقتضى العقد بلا عوض يقابلها فتشبه ربا الفضل الممنوع شرعاً. فإذا جرى العرف على اشتراطها العاقد على بصيرة من أمرها فيحسب لها حساب في العوض المقابل فيعود التوازن مضموناً، ويكون التعارف نافياً للنزاع. (ر: رد المحتار ١٢٣/٤).
أما خيار التعيين فهو أن يقع البيع وما في حكمه من عقود المعاوضات، كالصلح والإجارة على واحد غير معيّن من عدة أشياء، بشرط أن يكون لأحد العاقدين حق تعيين ما يختار منها :(ر: المجلة (٣١٦ .
Chapter Six: The Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. It corrupts the contract, such as when a general worker like a dyer or tailor stipulates that he will not guarantee what is destroyed by his work (see: F 6/4). This is the basis of the Hanafi ijtihad regarding contractual conditions. The view of the majority of Maliki jurists approximates it, then the Shafi'i ijtihad. These ijtihads initially adopted the theory of the contractual implication (muqtada al-aqd) and adhered to its idea in general, then they differed in detail and their views branched out. The Shafi'i ijtihad is the most stringent and restrictive of the freedom of conditions. In their view, all contractual implications are established by a legal proof. A contracting party may not contradict them, add to them, or restrict them with a condition unless a legal proof permits that obligation and necessitates fulfilling it, such as stipulating deferment of the price in a sale or deferment of the dowry in marriage. Imam Malik held a unique opinion regarding conditions that corrupt contracts: a condition that contradicts the contractual implication and corrupts it, if the stipulator abandons it and does not insist on it, the contract becomes valid and the condition is void. He builds that on the theory of the preventer (see: F 28/; he considers the contradictory condition a preventer (mani') of the contract's validity; when the preventer is removed, the prevented thing returns. (See: Bidayat al-Mujtahid 1352 and Theory of Contract by Prof. Abu Zahra, p. 149). 3. The Position of Hanafi Ijtihad in This Matter: The Hanafi ijtihad considers every condition that includes an extra benefit beyond the original contractual implication as contradictory and corrupting for financial commutative contracts; such as stipulating in a sale that the seller bears the transport cost of the sold item, or that one of the two parties lends a loan to the other, and the like. However, if that condition is in non-financial commutative contracts like marriage, the condition itself becomes void and does not affect the contract. But the Hanafi ijtihad exempts three types of conditions from prohibition and considers them valid and binding: 1. A condition whose permissibility has been stated by the Law, such as stipulating deferment of the sale price and stipulating an option for one of the two parties, which is called 'khiyar al-shart', and what is analogous to it like khiyar al-naqd and khiyar al-ta'yin. 2. A condition that harmonizes with the contract, such as the seller stipulating that the buyer provide a guarantor or a pledge for the deferred price, because it is a security for it (see: F 5/37). 3. A condition that is established by valid custom (urf); for custom validates conditions that are originally considered corrupting for the contract. For example, if people in some places customarily sell goods with the condition that the seller bears transport, or with the condition that the seller repairs the machine if it breaks down, that is valid. The Hanafi ijtihad builds its position on the freedom of contractual conditions on a measure of emphasis and expansion in adhering to the principle of balance between the rights of the contracting parties arising from the contract; it holds that stipulating a benefit for one side of the two parties disturbs the required balance and leads to dispute, because it is an extra benefit beyond the original contractual implication with no countervalue (iwad) corresponding to it, thus resembling the prohibited riba al-fadl. If custom prevails on stipulating it, the contracting party is aware of it, takes it into account in the countervalue, so balance becomes guaranteed, and customary practice eliminates dispute. (See: Radd al-Muhtar 4/123). As for khiyar al-ta'yin, it is that the sale and analogous commutative contracts like sulh and ijarah occur on one unspecified item from several items, with the condition that one of the two parties has the right to specify which he chooses (see: Majalla article 316).
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This passage is a comparative analysis of the positions of the major Sunni schools on the validity of conditions in contracts, focusing on the Hanafi madhhab. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that all schools start from the concept of 'muqtada al-aqd' (contractual implication) — the legal effects that naturally flow from a contract by operation of Shariah. However, they differ on the extent to which contracting parties can modify these implications by stipulating conditions. The Hanafi position is elaborated: generally, any condition that provides an extra benefit to one party beyond the basic exchange is considered corrupting (mufsid) in financial commutative contracts (like sale, hire) because it disrupts the required balance and resembles riba al-fadl (the prohibited surplus in barter transactions of certain goods). Such a condition invalidates the entire contract. However, in non-financial contracts such as marriage, the condition is void but the contract remains valid. The Hanafis then list three exceptions where such conditions are valid and binding: (1) conditions explicitly permitted by divine law (e.g., stipulating a delayed price or an option); (2) conditions that harmonize with the nature of the contract (e.g., demanding a guarantor for a deferred debt, as it secures the right); (3) conditions that are established by valid custom (urf), because custom is recognized as a source of law in the Hanafi school and can legitimize conditions that would otherwise disrupt balance. The underlying rationale is to maintain equilibrium (tawazun) between the parties' rights; if a condition becomes customary, the parties factor it into the countervalue, restoring balance. The Maliki view is noted: they treat a contradictory condition as a 'preventer' — if the stipulator waives it, the contract becomes valid. The Shafi'i view is stricter, allowing modification only where a specific text permits. This passage illustrates the Hanafi method of balancing textual evidence, custom, and the principle of preventing dispute.

Key Terms
مقتضى العقد (muqtada al-aqd): The inherent legal effects and obligations that arise from a contract by virtue of Shariah, which the parties cannot alter without a specific legal permission.
شرط مناف (shart munafi): A condition that contradicts the muqtada of the contract, typically providing an extra benefit to one party without a countervalue.
عرف معتبر (urf mu'tabar): A custom that is not contrary to Shariah and is consistently practiced, which can validate conditions that otherwise would be considered corrupting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, which of the following is NOT one of the three types of conditions that are considered valid despite contradicting the muqtada of the contract?
A) Conditions permitted by explicit shariah texts.
B) Conditions that harmonize with the contract (like requiring a guarantor for deferred price).
C) Conditions that are customary and consistently practiced.
D) Conditions that provide a gratuitous benefit to one party without any countervalue.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, conditions in financial contracts are generally invalid if they provide an extra benefit without countervalue, but three exceptions (textual permission, harmony, custom) permit them, with custom playing a pivotal role in modern commerce.

The Principle of Freedom of Contractual Conditions
Presenting the ijtihad that contractual freedom is the original principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the evidence for the Hanbali ijtihad on freedom of contractual conditions and explain how the exception from the hadith of Barira applies.

Core Idea
Default: all conditions valid unless explicitly prohibited.

This passage introduces a major ijtihad in Islamic contract law that argues for maximal contractual freedom. It is a direct challenge to the notion that only certain traditional conditions are permissible. The Hanbali school, along with early Kufan jurists, bases this view on the general command to fulfill contracts and the prophetic endorsement of conditions, treating explicit prohibitions as narrow exceptions. Understanding this position is crucial for appreciating the spectrum of Islamic legal thought on contracts and for modern fatwa reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimed default principle
Read the first sentence: the original legal principle is freedom of contracts in types and conditions. Note that this is presented as the default (al-asl).
This principle is also called 'sultan al-iradah' (dominion of will).
2
Locate the primary textual evidences
Find the two proofs: the Qur'anic verse 'Awfu bi al-'uqud' (5:1) and the hadith 'al-Muslimun 'ala shurutihim'. Understand that these are cited to establish the general obligation and permissibility.
The argument is based on 'umum' (generality) and 'itlaq' (absoluteness).
3
Identify the restrictive exception
Look at the exclusion: conditions prohibited by explicit texts or principles (e.g., riba, halal-haram reversal). The hadith of Barira is cited as the source of the restriction.
The exception is treated as narrow, not the rule.
4
Note the schools and jurists adopting this view
List the proponents: Hanbali school, Shuraih, Ibn Shubruma, and some Malikis. Recognise this as a minority view among the major schools.
The Hanafi school holds a more restrictive position, as mentioned in the commentary.
5
Contrast with the Hanafi approach (as context)
Understand that the Hanafi school balances freedom with custom (urf) and the requirement that conditions do not contradict the contract's essence. This leads to a different default presumption.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is that Hanafi muftis scrutinise conditions more strictly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ثانياً)
الاجتهادات التي ترى أن الأصل في
حرية الشروط العقدية هو الإطلاق
هذه الاجتهادات ترى أنَّ الأصل الشرعي، بمقتضى دلائل نصوص الشريعة والسنة العملية، هو حرية العقود أنواعاً وشروطاً، ووجوب الوفاء بكل ما يلتزمه العاقدان ويشترطانه ما لم يكن في نصوص الشريعة أو قواعدها ما يمنع من عقد أو شرط معيَّن؛ فعندئذ يمتنع بخصوصه، على خلاف القاعدة، ويعتبر الاتفاق عليه باطلاً كالتعاقد على الربا أو الشروط التي تحل حراماً أو تحرم حلالاً.
وهذا هو مبنى الاجتهاد الحنبلي، بحسب نصوص فقهائه المختلفة، وهو أوسع الاجتهادات الفقهية الإسلامية وأرحبها صدراً بمبدأ سلطان الإرادة، ويتفق جوهر نظريته فيه مع النظريات القانونية الحديثة في الفقه الأجنبي كما سنرى.
ومثله مذهب شريح القاضي ( ر ) ف (18/42)، ومذهب عبدالله بن شُبْرُمة الكوفي (1) .. وعلى هذا الرأي بعض فقهاء المذهب المالكي. فكون هذه الحرية هي المبدأ الأصلي العام في العقود والشروط بنظر هذه الاجتهادات هو المستفاد من قول القرآن العظيم : ﴿ يَاأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا أَوْفُوا بِالْعُقُودِ ﴾ بمقتضى عموم لفظه وإطلاقه ؛ ومن قول الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام : المسلمون على شروطهم .
والقيد الاستثنائي المانع هو المستفاد من قول النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام في حديث بريرة المتقدم: كل شرط ليس في كتاب الله فهو باطل.
(Second) The ijtihads that hold that the original principle regarding freedom of contractual conditions is absolute freedom. These ijtihads hold that the original legal principle, by virtue of the evidences of the texts of Sharia and the practical Sunnah, is freedom of contracts in types and conditions, and the obligation to fulfill whatever the two contracting parties commit to and stipulate, unless there is in the texts of Sharia or its principles something that prohibits a specific contract or condition; then it is prohibited specifically, contrary to the rule, and the agreement upon it is considered void, such as contracting on riba or conditions that permit what is forbidden or forbid what is permissible. This is the basis of the Hanbali ijtihad, according to the texts of its various jurists, and it is the broadest and most capacious of Islamic fiqh ijtihads regarding the principle of the dominion of the will, and the essence of its theory agrees with modern legal theories in foreign jurisprudence as we will see. Similar is the madhhab of Shuraih al-Qadi and the madhhab of Abdullah ibn Shubruma al-Kufi. And some jurists of the Maliki school are of this opinion. That this freedom is the general original principle in contracts and conditions according to these ijtihads is derived from the saying of the Great Quran: 'O you who have believed, fulfill [all] contracts' by virtue of the generality and absoluteness of its wording; and from the saying of the Messenger, peace be upon him: 'Muslims are bound by their conditions.' And the exceptional restrictive condition is derived from the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him, in the previously mentioned hadith of Barira: 'Every condition not in the Book of Allah is void.'
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage presents the second major ijtihad position on the scope of contractual freedom in Islamic law. It argues that the original default rule (al-asl) is complete freedom of contract in both form and content, based on the general command in the Qur'an (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:1) to fulfill contracts and the prophetic hadith that Muslims are bound by their conditions. This freedom is only restricted when a specific contract or condition directly contradicts an explicit scriptural text or a clear principle of Sharia, such as agreements involving usury (riba) or conditions that legalize the prohibited or prohibit the lawful. The Hanbali school is identified as the primary representative of this view, along with the early Kufan jurists Shuraih and Ibn Shubruma, and some Malikis. The author notes that this approach is the most accommodating of human will and aligns with modern legal theories. The restrictive exception is derived from the hadith of Barira, which voids any condition not in accordance with Allah's law, but this is treated as a narrow exception rather than the rule. In the Hanafi context, this passage highlights a key difference: while the Hanafi school also recognizes custom and conditions, it generally subjects contractual stipulations to stricter scrutiny, particularly in areas like sale and marriage, often distinguishing between valid (sahih), defective (fasid), and void (batil) conditions. The Hanbali approach, by contrast, starts from a presumption of validity for all conditions unless explicitly prohibited.

Key Terms
سلطان الإرادة (sultān al-irādah): The principle that the contracting parties' will (intention) is the primary source of obligations and effects in contracts, limited only by explicit legal prohibitions.
شرط فاسد (shart fāsid): A condition that is invalid because it contradicts a clear textual prohibition or a fundamental principle of Sharia.
أوفوا بالعقود (awfū bi al-ʻuqūd): Qur'anic verse (5:1) used as evidence for the obligation to fulfill contracts, interpreted here as establishing the general principle of contractual obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which Qur'anic verse is cited by the Hanbali and other scholars as primary evidence for the default principle of freedom of contractual conditions?

B) يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا أَوْفُوا بِالْعُقُودِ
C) وَأَحَلَّ اللَّهُ الْبَيْعَ وَحَرَّمَ الرِّبَا
D) فَإِن تَابُوا وَأَقَامُوا الصَّلَاةَ وَآتَوُا الزَّكَاةَ فَخَلُّوا سَبِيلَهُمْ

Key Takeaway
The Hanbali school and its allies hold that freedom of contractual conditions is the default, rooted in the Qur'anic command to fulfill contracts and the hadith of conditions, with only explicit textual prohibitions as exceptions.

The Qawida of Party Autonomy in Contracts
Explaining the principle of party autonomy and its evidentiary basis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the primary textual evidence for the qawida of party autonomy, classify the Quranic verse as 'amm, and apply the Hanafi usul rule that 'amm is definitive until specified, deriving the mufta bihi position that conditions are valid unless they contradict a clear Sharia text.

Core Idea
Freedom of contract is the default; only clear textual prohibitions override party will.

This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's legal philosophy text explores the Islamic principle of contractual freedom, focusing on the Hanbali school's broad interpretation. For a Hanafi mufti, understanding this evidence is essential because the same proofs are used across schools but with different interpretive weights. The Quranic verse 'Fulfill contracts' and the hadith 'Muslims are bound by their conditions' form the basis of a general qawida, but their scope is contested. This lesson dissects the evidentiary structure and shows how Hanafi usul principles would apply.

Guided Path
1
State the qawida
Identify the central legal maxim: contracts and conditions are permissible and binding unless a clear Sharia text prohibits them.
2
Identify the primary dalil
Locate the two main proofs: Quran 5:1 ('O you who have believed, fulfill contracts') and the hadith 'Muslims are bound by their conditions.'
These are cited as evidence for the general rule in the passage.
3
Classify the text in usul
Classify the Quranic verse as 'amm (general) and mutlaq (absolute). According to Hanafi usul, an 'amm command indicates obligation (wujub) and applies to all instances unless specified.
The hadith 'al-muslimun' is also general but often treated as a supporting dalil.
4
Identify the specifying text
Identify the hadith of Barirah ('Every condition not in the Book of Allah is void') and Umar's statement on conciliation as the takhsis (specification) that restricts the general rule.
These texts limit the general permission to conditions that do not contradict Sharia.
5
Apply Hanafi usul
Apply the Hanafi rule that an 'amm text is qat'i (definitive) until specified by a reliable source. The Barirah hadith is authentic and provides a valid exception. Thus, the mufta bihi position is that conditions are presumed valid unless they directly contradict a clear text or violate an established principle.
Note the difference with the Hanbali view that accepts more conditions; the Hanafi school also considers the contract's essential purpose (muqtaḍā al-ʿaqd).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٤٢) الاجتهادات في سلطان الإرادة العقدية
نصوص الشريعة والسنة العملية، هو حرية العقود أنواعاً وشروطاً، ووجوب الوفاء بكل ما يلتزمه العاقدان ويشترطانه ما لم يكن في نصوص الشريعة أو قواعدها ما يمنع من عقد أو شرط معيَّن؛ فعندئذ يمتنع بخصوصه، على خلاف القاعدة، ويعتبر الاتفاق عليه باطلاً كالتعاقد على الربا أو الشروط التي تحل حراماً أو تحرم حلالاً.
وهذا هو مبنى الاجتهاد الحنبلي، بحسب نصوص فقهائه المختلفة، وهو أوسع الاجتهادات الفقهية الإسلامية وأرحبها صدراً بمبدأ سلطان الإرادة، ويتفق جوهر نظريته فيه مع النظريات القانونية الحديثة في الفقه الأجنبي كما سنرى.
ومثله مذهب شريح القاضي ( ر ) ف (١٨/٤٢)، ومذهب عبدالله بن شُبْرُمة الكوفي (1) .. وعلى هذا الرأي بعض فقهاء المذهب المالكي. فكون هذه الحرية هي المبدأ الأصلي العام في العقود والشروط بنظر هذه الاجتهادات هو المستفاد من قول القرآن العظيم : ﴿ يَاأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا أَوْفُوا بِالْعُقُودِ ﴾ بمقتضى عموم لفظه وإطلاقه ؛ ومن قول الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام : المسلمون على شروطهم .
والقيد الاستثنائي المانع هو المستفاد من قول النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام في حديث بريرة المتقدم: كل شرط ليس في كتاب الله فهو باطل بالمعنى الذي سلف بيانه في تفسير هذا الحديث (ر : ف ٧/٤١ الحاشية)؛ وكذا من قول عمر بن الخطاب في وصيته القضائية إلى أبي الأشعري : الصلح جائز بين المسلمين إلا صلحاً أحل حراماً أو حرم حلالاً هذا حلالاً» ر ف ٦/٣ الحاشية وكلام عمر هو التفسير الصحيح للشرط الممنوع الذي وصفه الرسول الله بأنه ليس في كتاب الله». ويطعن هؤلاء في ثبوت ما يروى من أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: «نهى عن بيع و شرطه (۱) بأنه ضعيف السند في نظر علماء الحديث ؛ وعلى فرض ثبوته يحمل على الشرط المنافي لكتاب الله وشريعته كما في حديث بريرة فإنه هو الثابت. ٥/٤٢ - ففي هذه الاجتهادات لا يعتبر للعقود مقتضيات ضيقة بحدود ثابتة تتحكم في شروط المتعاقدين بل يرون أن الشارع في الشريعة الإسلامية قد فوَّض إلى إرادة العاقدين تحديد هذه المقتضيات ضمن نطاق حقوقهما في كل ما لا يصادم نصَّاً من نصوص الشريعة ولا ينقض أصلاً من أصولها الثابتة.
ويقول ابن تيمية رحمه الله في تعليل ذلك : إن العقود إنما وَجَبَ الوفاء بها لإيجاب الشارع الوفاء بها مطلقاً إلا ما خصّه الدليل على أن الوفاء بها من الواجبات التي اتفقت عليها الملل والعقلاء جميعهم وأدخلها في الواجبات العقلية من يقول بالوجوب (۲). والأصل في العقود رضا المتعاقدين، ونتيجتها هو ما أوجباه على أنفسهما بالتعاقد».
Chapter (42): The Juristic Efforts Concerning the Authority of Will in Contracts. The texts of Sharia and the practical Sunnah indicate that there is freedom of contracts in types and conditions, and the obligation to fulfill everything that the two contracting parties undertake and stipulate, unless there is in the texts of Sharia or its rules something that prevents a specific contract or condition; then it is prohibited specifically, contrary to the general rule, and the agreement is considered void, such as contracting on riba or conditions that make lawful what is unlawful or make unlawful what is lawful. This is the basis of the Hanbali juristic effort, according to the various texts of its jurists, and it is the broadest and most spacious of Islamic juristic efforts in embracing the principle of the authority of will, and the essence of its theory in this regard agrees with modern legal theories in foreign jurisprudence as we will see. Similar is the school of Shuraih al-Qadi (may Allah have mercy on him) in (18/42), and the school of Abdullah ibn Shubruma al-Kufi (1)... And some jurists of the Maliki school hold this opinion. That this freedom is the general original principle in contracts and conditions according to these juristic efforts is derived from the saying of the Great Quran: {O you who have believed, fulfill [all] contracts} by virtue of the generality and absoluteness of its wording; and from the saying of the Messenger, peace be upon him: 'Muslims are bound by their conditions.' And the exceptional restrictive limitation is derived from the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him, in the previously mentioned hadith of Barirah: 'Every condition not in the Book of Allah is void' in the sense that has been explained previously in the interpretation of this hadith (see: p. 41/7 footnote); and likewise from the saying of Umar ibn al-Khattab in his judicial will to Abu al-Ash'ari: 'Conciliation is permissible among Muslims except for a conciliation that makes lawful what is unlawful or makes unlawful what is lawful' (see p. 3/6 footnote and the commentary). And the saying of Umar is the correct interpretation of the prohibited condition that the Messenger of Allah described as 'not in the Book of Allah.' And these [scholars] challenge the authenticity of what is narrated that the Prophet, peace be upon him, 'forbade a sale and a condition' (1) by saying that it is weak in chain according to hadith scholars; and if it were established, it is interpreted as referring to a condition contrary to the Book of Allah and His Sharia, as in the hadith of Barirah, which is the established one. 5/42 - So in these juristic efforts, contracts are not considered to have narrow implications with fixed limits that control the conditions of the contracting parties. Rather, they hold that the Lawgiver in Islamic Sharia has delegated to the will of the contracting parties the determination of these implications within the scope of their rights in everything that does not conflict with a text of Sharia nor violate an established principle. And Ibn Taymiyyah, may Allah have mercy on him, says in explanation: 'Contracts are only obligatory because the Lawgiver made fulfillment obligatory absolutely, except what the evidence specifies, and that fulfillment is among the obligations agreed upon by all religions and rational people, and those who believe in rational obligation include it in rational obligations (2). The basis of contracts is the consent of the contracting parties, and their result is what they have obligated upon themselves by contracting.'
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanbali school's expansive view of party autonomy (سلطان الإرادة) in contracts. The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, introduces the topic by stating that Islamic texts affirm freedom of contract in type and conditions, unless a specific prohibition exists from a clear text or a definitive rule. The default is binding fulfillment of any agreement, with exceptions like riba or conditions that reverse legal rulings. He notes that the Hanbali school represents the broadest acceptance of this principle, alongside the views of Shuraih, Ibn Shubruma, and some Malikis. The evidence for the general principle is derived from the Quranic verse 'Fulfill contracts' and the hadith 'Muslims are bound by their conditions.' The exceptional limit comes from the hadith of Barirah: 'Every condition not in the Book of Allah is void' – but this is interpreted narrowly to mean only conditions that directly contradict a clear textual ruling, not mere additional stipulations. The author also cites Umar ibn al-Khattab's statement on conciliation. The passage argues against the hadith 'forbade a sale and a condition' by questioning its authenticity and, if accepted, restricting it to conditions that violate Sharia. Ultimately, the Hanbali view delegates extensive power to the contracting parties to define the effects of their contracts, as long as no explicit text or established principle is violated. Ibn Taymiyyah's explanation reinforces that fulfillment is obligatory by divine command, but the content of the obligation stems from the parties' own consent and stipulation.

Key Terms
سلطان الإرادة العقدية (sulṭān al-irādah al-ʿaqdiyyah): The authority or autonomy of the contracting parties' will to determine the terms and effects of their contract.
حرية العقود (ḥurriyyat al-ʿuqūd): Freedom of contract: the principle that parties may create any type of contract and include any conditions they wish, as long as not prohibited by Sharia.
المسلمون على شروطهم (al-muslimūna ʿalā shurūṭihim): A prophetic hadith indicating that Muslims are bound by the conditions they agree upon, used as evidence for enforceability of contractual stipulations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The hadith 'Every condition not in the Book of Allah is void'
B) The Quranic verse 'Fulfill contracts' and the hadith 'Muslims are bound by their conditions'
C) The statement of Umar ibn al-Khattab about conciliation
D) The Hanbali school's unique methodology

Key Takeaway
The principle of party autonomy in contracts is derived from general Quranic and hadith texts, but its scope varies between schools; the Hanbali view is the most permissive, while the Hanafi view imposes additional restrictions based on the contract's implied purpose.

The Principle of Contractual Conditions and Ibn Taymiyya's Argument
Analyzing a hadith on prohibited conditions and presenting a broader contractual freedom principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Ibn Taymiyya's principle that contracts are binding by Sharia command, and distinguish valid from invalid conditions based on the Sharia.

Core Idea
Contracts are binding because the Lawgiver commanded fulfillment, not because of rational obligation.

This passage addresses a key question in contractual theory: to what extent can contracting parties stipulate conditions? Some rely on a hadith that seems to prohibit conditions altogether. The author shows that this hadith is either weak or applies only to conditions contradicting Sharia. He then cites Ibn Taymiyya's broader principle that contractual obligations arise from the Sharia's command, giving parties wide freedom as long as they do not violate a text or principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested hadith
Find the hadith that some use to invalidate all conditions in sales. Note the two counter-arguments: weak chain, and if authentic, limited to conditions contrary to Sharia.
2
Understand the overarching principle
Recognize that contracts are not bound by narrow fixed requirements; parties can agree on conditions within the broad limits of Sharia.
3
Analyze Ibn Taymiyya's talil
Ibn Taymiyya explains that contracts must be fulfilled because the Lawgiver obligated fulfillment absolutely, except where evidence specifies. The origin is mutual consent.
This rejects the Mu'tazila view of rational obligation.
4
Apply the principle to a case study
Consider a condition that seems to restrict a right. Determine if it contradicts a clear text or principle of Sharia. If not, it is valid.
This is the mufti's approach.

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
موسى.
باطل بالمعنى الذي سلف بيانه في تفسير هذا الحديث (ر : ف ٧/٤١ الحاشية)؛ وكذا من قول عمر بن الخطاب في وصيته القضائية إلى أبي الأشعري : الصلح جائز بين المسلمين إلا صلحاً أحل حراماً أو حرم حلالاً» ر ف ٦/٣ الحاشية وكلام عمر هو التفسير الصحيح. للشرط الممنوع الذي وصفه الرسول الله بأنه ليس في كتاب الله». ويطعن هؤلاء في ثبوت ما يروى من أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: «نهى عن بيع و شرطه (1) بأنه ضعيف السند في نظر علماء الحديث ؛ وعلى فرض ثبوته يحمل على الشرط المنافي لكتاب الله وشريعته كما في حديث بريرة فإنه هو الثابت. 5/42 - ففي هذه الاجتهادات لا يعتبر للعقود مقتضيات ضيقة بحدود ثابتة تتحكم في شروط المتعاقدين بل يرون أن الشارع في الشريعة الإسلامية قد فوَّض إلى إرادة العاقدين تحديد هذه المقتضيات ضمن نطاق حقوقهما في كل ما لا يصادم نصَّاً من نصوص الشريعة ولا ينقض أصلاً من أصولها الثابتة.
ويقول ابن تيمية رحمه الله في تعليل ذلك : إن العقود إنما وَجَبَ الوفاء بها لإيجاب الشارع الوفاء بها مطلقاً إلا ما خصّه الدليل على أن الوفاء بها من الواجبات التي اتفقت عليها الملل والعقلاء جميعهم وأدخلها في الواجبات العقلية من يقول بالوجوب (2). والأصل في العقود رضا المتعاقدين، ونتيجتها هو ما أوجباه على أنفسهما بالتعاقد».

(1) حديث: نهي عن بيع وشرط . . . أخرجه الطبراني في الأوسط، ونقل الزيلعي عن ابن القطان أنه ضعفه نصب الراية (18/4 وانظر : بدائع الصنائع 175/5 ، وفتح القدير 76/6).

(2) الذين يقولون بالوجوب العقلي هم فريق المعتزلة ورأيهم في ذلك أن الأمور التي تتصف بالحسن الذاتي كالإيمان بالله تعالى وكالصدق والعدل يعتبر الناس مكلفين بها بحكم العقل، ومحاسبين عليها عند الله تعالى دون توقف على ورود الشرائع. الإلهية بها وتبليغها بواسطة الرسل. وهو خلاف ما عليه جمهور فقهاء المسلمين الذين يتمسكون بظاهر قوله تعالى: ﴿وما كنا معذبين حتى نبعث رسولا﴾ [الإسراء 15/17]. والمسألة معروفة في علم الكلام وعلم أصول الفقه بمسألة «الحسن والقبح العقليين».
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. Musa. Invalid in the sense previously explained in the interpretation of this hadith (refer to footnote 7/41); likewise from the statement of Umar ibn al-Khattab in his judicial will to Abu al-Ash'ari: 'Reconciliation is permissible among Muslims except a reconciliation that makes lawful what is unlawful or makes unlawful what is lawful' (refer to footnote 6/3). And Umar's statement is the correct interpretation of the prohibited condition that the Messenger of Allah described as 'not in the Book of Allah'. And these people challenge the authenticity of what is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited a sale and its condition (1) — that it is weak in chain according to hadith scholars; and assuming its authenticity, it is interpreted as the condition contradictory to the Book of Allah and His Sharia, as in the hadith of Barira, which is authentic. 5/42 — So in these ijtihads, contracts are not considered to have narrow requirements with fixed boundaries controlling the conditions of the contracting parties; rather, they hold that the Lawgiver in Islamic Sharia has delegated to the will of the contracting parties the determination of these requirements within the scope of their rights in everything that does not contradict a text of Sharia nor overturn a fixed principle of its principles. And Ibn Taymiyya (may Allah have mercy on him) said in explanation of that: 'Contracts must be fulfilled because the Lawgiver obligated fulfillment absolutely, except what is specified by evidence, and fulfillment is among the obligations agreed upon by all religions and rational people, and those who advocate rational obligation included it among rational obligations (2). The origin in contracts is the consent of the contracting parties, and their result is what they have obligated upon themselves by contracting.' (1) Hadith: 'He prohibited a sale and a condition...' narrated by al-Tabarani in al-Awsat; al-Zayla'i narrated from Ibn al-Qattan that he weakened it. Nasb al-Raya (4/18); see also Bada'i' al-Sana'i' (5/175), Fath al-Qadir (6/76). (2) Those who advocate rational obligation are the Mu'tazila; their view is that matters characterized by intrinsic goodness, such as belief in Allah, truthfulness, and justice, people are obligated by reason, and accountable for them before Allah without depending on the revelation of divine laws and their delivery by messengers. This is contrary to the majority of Muslim jurists who adhere to the apparent meaning of His statement: 'And We would not punish until We send a messenger' [al-Isra 17:15]. The issue is known in theology and usul al-fiqh as the issue of 'rational good and evil'.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage introduces Chapter Six on contract theory. The author first addresses a hadith that seems to prohibit conditions in sales, i.e., 'نهي عن بيع وشرط'. He argues that this hadith is weak in chain according to hadith scholars, and if taken as authentic, it must be interpreted as applying only to conditions that directly contradict the Book of Allah and the Sharia. He supports this with the statement of Umar on reconciliation. The author then cites Ibn Taymiyya's explanation that contracts are binding because the Lawgiver commanded fulfillment, not because of rational obligation alone. Ibn Taymiyya states that the basis of contracts is mutual consent. The footnote clarifies that the Mu'tazila school holds a rationalist view of obligation, which is rejected by the majority of jurists including the Hanafis. This passage underscores the Hanafi principle that contractual freedom is broad, limited only by clear texts of Sharia.

Key Terms
الشرط الممنوع (al-shart al-mamnūʿ): The condition that is prohibited because it contradicts the Book of Allah or the Sharia
الوجوب العقلي (al-wujūb al-ʿaqlī): The concept that certain moral obligations are known by reason alone, without revelation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the basis for the binding nature of contracts?
A) Rational obligation
B) The command of Sharia
C) Mutual consent alone
D) Social customs

Key Takeaway
Contracts are valid and binding due to the Sharia command, and parties may stipulate conditions as long as they do not contradict clear texts or principles.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Hanbali Interpretation of Muqtada al-Aqd: Broadening the Contract's Scope
Analysis of Hanbali interpretation of muqtada al-'aqd with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanbali qawida 'Whatever is for the benefit of the contracting party is part of the benefit of the contract' to distinguish valid from invalid conditions in contracts, and compare this view with the narrower Hanafi approach.

Core Idea
The Hanbali school interprets muqtada al-'aqd broadly to include legitimate party benefits, unlike other schools that restrict it.

In this passage, the author presents the Hanbali school's nuanced approach to muqtada al-'aqd (the requisite of the contract). Unlike some other madhahib that narrowly define the contract's inherent effects, Hanbali jurists consider the legitimate interests of the contracting parties as part of the contract's own benefit. This leads to significant practical differences in which conditions are deemed valid. We will examine the key Hanbali qawida that governs this principle, see how it is applied to marriage and pledge contracts, and contrast it with the Hanafi position to understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence of the difference).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanbali definition of muqtada al-'aqd
Read the passage to understand how the Hanbalis define muqtada al-'aqd. They do not limit it to the immediate legal effects but include any benefit that is legitimate (mashru'ah) for the contracting party.
The key phrase: 'They consider the benefit of the contracting party as part of the benefit of the contract itself, as long as it is legitimate.'
2
Apply the qawida to the marriage condition examples
Examine the two marriage examples: stipulation of no marital enjoyment (invalidates contract) vs. stipulation that husband not move her from her home (valid with option to annul). Note why the first negates the contract's purpose while the second is a legitimate benefit.
The qawida 'Whatever is for the benefit of the contracting party is part of the benefit of the contract' is explicitly stated in the passage.
3
Distinguish between invalid and valid conditions
Based on the passage, identify the criterion: a condition is invalid if it contradicts the fundamental legal purpose of the contract (e.g., stipulating no marital enjoyment in marriage, or stipulating that a pledge not be sold). It is valid if it serves a legitimate interest without negating the contract's essence.
The passage also gives examples from pledge (rahn) contracts where conditions that prevent recovery of debt are invalid.
4
Contrast with the narrow view of other schools
The passage mentions that other schools (including Hanafis) take a narrower view, considering any non-inherent benefit as contradictory to muqtada al-'aqd. Identify the thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafis reject many conditions that Hanbalis accept.
The Hanafi position is not detailed here, but we know from usul: Hanafis hold that conditions adding benefits not normally part of the contract are tantamount to altering the contract's subject matter, unless explicitly allowed.
5
Verify the exceptions to the qawida
Note that the qawida has istithna'at: conditions that are contrary to Sharia (e.g., facilitating haram) or that nullify the contract's purpose are not valid. These are mentioned in the istithna'at of the qawida panel.
Examples include stipulating that a woman travel alone (haram) or stipulating no marital enjoyment (negates purpose).

٤٢ / ٦ - موقف الاجتهاد الحنبلي من نظرية مقتضى العقد :
هذا، وإن الفقه الحنبلي لم يهمل نظرية مقتضى العقد التي تمسك بها من ضيق على نفسه من رجال المذاهب الأخرى. فكثيراً ما يمنع فقهاء الحنابلة بعض الشروط في بعض العقود ويعللون بمخالفة ذلك الشرط لمقتضى العقد.
ولكنهم أوسع نظراً في تفسير مقتضى العقد وحدوده. فهم لا يعتبرون ان كغيرهم كل مصلحة يشترطها أحد العاقدين لنفسه مما لا يوجبها العقد بذاته تكون منافية لمقتضاه ؛ بل يعتبرون مصلحة العاقد من مصلحة العقد نفسه ما دامت ،مشروعة، أي غير محرَّمة شرعاً. فلا يعتبر الشرط منافياً لمقتضى العقد في نظر المحققين من الحنابلة إلا في النواحي الأساسية التي إذا شرط خلافها تعطلت الغاية الشرعية من العقد فعندئذ قد يبطل العقد بالشرط، وقد يبطل الشرط ويصح العقد، وذلك على حسب العقد، وذلك على حسب حال الشرط ودرجة مناقضته لغاية العقد.

(ر: كشاف القناع شرح الإقناع ٥٢/٣ طبعة المطبعة الشرقية).
كان من مصلحة

Chapter (42): Ijtihadat in the Authority of Contractual Will. Then he (may Allah have mercy on him) cited the texts of Sharia indicating this principle, which we have mentioned at the beginning of the research (cf. Fatawa Ibn Taymiyyah 3/239). 42/6 - The Hanbali Ijtihad Position on the Theory of Muqtada al-Aqd: This, and Hanbali fiqh did not neglect the theory of muqtada al-aqd to which those who narrowed themselves from among the men of other schools adhered. The Hanbali jurists often prevent some conditions in some contracts and justify this by the violation of that condition to the muqtada al-aqd. But they are broader in interpretation of muqtada al-aqd and its limits. They do not consider, like others, that every benefit stipulated by one of the contracting parties for himself, which the contract itself does not require, is contradictory to its muqtada; rather, they consider the benefit of the contracting party as part of the benefit of the contract itself, as long as it is legitimate, i.e., not prohibited by Sharia. So, in the view of the verifiers among the Hanbalis, a condition is not considered contrary to muqtada al-aqd except in fundamental aspects where stipulating the opposite would render the legal purpose of the contract ineffective. Then, in that case, the contract may be void due to the condition, or the condition may be void and the contract valid, depending on the contract and the state of the condition and its degree of contradiction to the purpose of the contract. - On this basis, they said: If it is stipulated in a marriage contract that the marital enjoyment is not permissible, the contract becomes void due to this condition, because then there is no meaning for marriage; rather, it becomes like a fictitious contract. As for if it is stipulated that marital enjoyment is not to be practiced, then the marriage is valid and the condition is nullified because it contradicts muqtada al-aqd, since it is inconceivable that enjoyment be permissible by the contract and prohibited by the condition. The contract is not void here because its legal purpose, which is the permissibility of enjoyment, is achieved and agreed upon by both parties, and the mentioned condition is an additional impermissible matter, so it is nullified without the contract. • Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh - On this basis, they also said: If a woman stipulates in the marriage contract that the husband should not travel with her when she travels, this condition is invalid because Sharia does not permit her to travel alone. But if she stipulates that her husband should not travel with her if he wants to travel, or that he should not move her from her home, then her condition is valid because it is a legitimate benefit that does not prevent the purpose of marriage. So her stipulation is valid, and the woman is given the option of annulment if the husband does not fulfill it. 42/7 - And they responded to those who hold that such a condition is not from the benefit of the contract with a response in which they established an important fiqhi rule that was the dividing line between their generous theory and the narrow theory of others regarding the limits of muqtada al-aqd. They said: The statement that this condition is not from the benefit of the contract is a rejected statement, because this condition is for the benefit of the woman, and whatever is for the benefit of the contracting party is from the benefit of the contract (like stipulating a pledge in a sale). (Reference: Kashshaf al-Qina' Sharh al-Iqna' 3/52, al-Matba'a al-Sharqiyya edition). They stated regarding the pledge (rahn) that it is not valid to stipulate that the pledge should not be sold when the debt falls due, or that the debt should not be recovered from its price. Likewise, it is not valid to stipulate an option for the pledgor, nor to set a time limit for the pledge. They justified this by saying that these are all invalid conditions because they contradict muqtada al-aqd, since the purpose of the pledge contract is lost when these conditions are fulfilled. They differed on whether the pledge becomes void with these conditions, or only the condition is nullified. (Reference: al-Mughni 4/429). And thus in most contracts, they distinguish between valid and invalid conditions, and justify the invalidity by stating that they contradict muqtada al-aqd in the sense we have explained. (1) Here a notable restriction: i.e., the legitimate benefit of the contracting party, which is not prohibited by Sharia.
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanbali school's approach to the concept of muqtada al-'aqd (the requisite of the contract). The author explains that Hanbali jurists, unlike some other schools, adopt a broader interpretation. They do not consider every additional condition that benefits one party as automatically contradictory to the contract's requisite. Instead, they evaluate whether the condition serves a legitimate (shar'i) interest. If the condition does not undermine the fundamental legal purpose of the contract, it is permissible. For example, stipulating in a marriage contract that the husband will not take his wife away from her home is valid because it protects her legitimate interest without negating the essence of marriage. However, a condition that would render the contract pointless, such as stipulating no marital enjoyment, invalidates the contract itself. The passage also discusses analogous rules for pledge (rahn) contracts, showing that conditions contradicting the contract's purpose (e.g., preventing sale of the pledge at maturity) are invalid. The author highlights a key fiqh principle: 'Whatever benefits the contracting party is part of the contract's benefit,' which encapsulates the Hanbali view.

Key Terms
مقتضى العقد (muqtada al-'aqd): The essential requisites and inherent effects of a contract; what the contract necessarily implies by its nature.
شرط فاسد (shart fasid): A condition that contradicts muqtada al-'aqd or Sharia, rendering the contract or the condition invalid depending on the case.
خيار الفسخ (khiyar al-faskh): Option to annul the contract available to the woman if the husband fails to fulfill a valid condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanbali view presented, what is the ruling if a woman stipulates in the marriage contract that her husband shall not travel with her when he wishes to travel?
A) The condition is valid, and she has the option to annul the marriage if the husband does not fulfill it.
B) The condition is invalid because it contradicts the muqtada al-'aqd of marriage.
C) The condition is valid only if the husband agrees after the contract.
D) The condition is invalid, and the marriage itself is also invalid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanbali school interprets muqtada al-'aqd broadly, allowing conditions that serve the legitimate interests of the parties, unless they negate the contract's fundamental purpose.
