The Principle: What Benefits the Contracting Party Benefits the Contract
Ruling on woman's condition in marriage and the principle that what benefits the contracting party benefits the contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida 'what benefits the contracting party benefits the contract' to evaluate the validity of conditions in contracts and distinguish between legitimate and prohibited interests.

Core Idea
A condition that serves a legitimate interest of a party is considered to serve the contract itself.

Contracts are not rigid; they allow conditions that serve the legitimate interests of the parties. This lesson examines a key Hanbali qawida that broadens the scope of valid conditions: whatever benefits a contracting party in a Sharia-recognized way also benefits the contract. Understanding this principle helps us navigate nuanced cases, such as conditions in marriage agreements that restrict a husband's travel or relocation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of conditions in the marriage contract
The passage distinguishes between a condition that violates Sharia (the woman traveling alone) and one that is a legitimate interest (preventing removal from home). Identify which is valid and why.
The first condition is invalid because it demands something prohibited; the second is valid because it does not contradict the purpose of marriage.
2
Extract the general qawida from the rebuttal
The author refutes the narrow view that such a condition is not in the contract's interest. The response contains the maxim: 'Whatever is in the interest of the contracting party is in the interest of the contract.' State the maxim in Arabic and English.
Arabic: ما كان من مصلحة العاقد كان من مصلحة العقد. English: 'What benefits the contracting party benefits the contract.'
3
Apply the qawida to the analogy of the pledge in a sale
The maxim is illustrated by the valid condition of a pledge (rahn) in a sale. Explain how this analogy supports the principle and confirms that the condition in marriage is valid.
In a sale, a pledge benefits the seller (security for payment), yet it is valid because it serves the contract's purpose (ensuring performance). Similarly, the condition in marriage benefits the woman without undermining marriage's purpose.
4
Note the restriction: legitimate interest only
The passage includes a footnote restricting 'interest' to that which is legitimate (not prohibited by Sharia). Why is this restriction critical?
The maxim does not validate conditions that require haram acts. Only Sharia-recognized benefits count.
5
Contrast with the opposing view
The text mentions 'others' who consider such conditions outside the contract's interest. The dispute map shows this is about the scope of muqtada al-'aqd. Summarize the opposing argument and why the author rejects it.
Opponents argue conditions beyond primary purpose are invalid; the author counters using the qawida, expanding legitimate conditions.

الباب السادس: نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه - وعلى هذا الأساس أيضاً قالوا : إذا شرطت المرأة في عقد النكاح أن لا يسافر الزوج معها إذا سافرت كان هذا الشرط باطلاً، لأن الشريعة لا تجيز لها السفر وحدها . أما إذا شرطت أن لا يسافر زوجها بها إذا أراد السفر، أو أن لا ينقلها من دارها، فإنَّ شرطها هذا ،صحيح لأنه مصلحة مشروعة لا تمنع المقصود من النكاح فيصح اشتراطها ويثبت للمرأة خيار الفسخ إذا لم يف لها الزوج به. ٧/٤٢ - وقد ردوا على من يرى أن مثل هذا الشرط ليس من مصلحة العقد رداً قرروا به ضابطاً فقهياً هاماً كان فيه حد الفصل بين نظريتهم السمحة ونظرية غيرهم الضيّقة في حدود مقتضى العقد، فقالوا : والقول بأن هذا الشرط ليس من مصلحة العقد قول مردود، لأن هذا الشرط من مصلحة ،المرأة، وما كان من مصلحة العاقد (۱) العقد كاشتراط الرهن في البيع». (ر: كشاف القناع شرح الإقناع ٥٢/٣ طبعة المطبعة الشرقية). كان من مصلحة (۱) هنا قيد ملحوظ أي من مصلحة العاقد المشروعة، وهي التي لم يحرمها الشرع ..
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh - And on this basis, they also said: If a woman stipulates in the marriage contract that the husband should not travel with her when she travels, this condition is invalid, because the Sharia does not permit her to travel alone. But if she stipulates that her husband should not travel with her if he wants to travel, or that he should not move her from her home, then this condition of hers is valid because it is a legitimate interest that does not prevent the purpose of marriage, so her stipulation is valid and the woman is given the option to annul if the husband does not fulfill it. 7/42 - And they refuted those who think that such a condition is not in the interest of the contract, establishing an important jurisprudential principle that was the dividing line between their broad theory and the narrow theory of others regarding the limits of what the contract requires. They said: The claim that this condition is not in the interest of the contract is rejected, because this condition is in the interest of the woman, and whatever is in the interest of the contracting party is in the interest of the contract, like stipulating a pledge in a sale. (Reference: Kashshaf al-Qina'...) (1) Here there is a notable restriction: the legitimate interest of the contracting party, which the Sharia has not prohibited.
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanbali school (referred to as 'they') on a specific condition in a marriage contract. The author first distinguishes between two types of conditions: one that is invalid (traveling alone with the woman) because it contradicts a Sharia prohibition, and one that is valid (preventing the husband from taking her away) because it serves a legitimate interest without undermining the purpose of marriage. The passage then articulates a key jurisprudential principle: a condition that benefits the contracting party is considered to benefit the contract itself. This principle is illustrated with the analogy of stipulating a pledge in a sale, which is valid. The footnote clarifies that the 'interest' must be legitimate in the eyes of the Sharia. This principle is central to distinguishing the Hanbali approach from other schools that may be more restrictive.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A condition or stipulation made within a contract that modifies or adds to the rights and obligations of the parties.
مصلحة (maslahah): Benefit or interest of a contracting party, provided it is legitimate (not prohibited by Sharia).
مقتضى العقد (muqtada al-'aqd): The inherent requirements and implications of a contract by its nature.
ما كان من مصلحة العاقد كان من مصلحة العقد (ma kana min maslahat al-'aaqid kana min maslahat al-'aqd): Jurisprudential maxim: whatever benefits the contracting party benefits the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the condition that the husband not travel with her (if she travels alone) invalid?
A) Because it is not in the woman's interest.
B) Because it involves a prohibited act (traveling alone for a woman).
C) Because it contradicts the purpose of marriage.
D) Because the husband's consent is not obtained.

Key Takeaway
A condition that serves a legitimate interest of a party is considered to benefit the contract itself, expanding the scope of valid conditions beyond the contract's inherent requirements.

Hanbali Ijtihad on Reversing Contractual Liability
Discussion of Hanbali views on reversing contractual liability by condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two Hanbali positions on qalb muqtada al-aqd and apply the Hanafi position to a scenario where a condition reverses liability.

Core Idea
The Hanbali school is divided on whether a condition can reverse a contract’s liability nature; the apparent madhhab says no, but a narration from Ahmad says yes.

This passage examines a key legal-philosophical dispute within the Hanbali school: can a contractual condition reverse the inherent liability nature of a contract—transforming a trust (amanah) into a guarantee (daman), or vice versa? Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to evaluate how different schools treat the scope of contractual will, and it directly impacts fatwa practice when parties attempt to alter default liabilities by agreement. The Hanafi position, which generally rejects such reversal, will be contrasted as a benchmark.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Recognize that the dispute concerns whether a stipulation can override the default liability classification (amanah or daman) that the contract inherently imposes. For example, a borrowed item is normally a trust; can the borrower stipulate that he will be liable regardless of negligence?
This is not about adding benefits but about changing the fundamental legal nature.
2
State the two Hanbali positions
Ibn Qudamah (first view) holds that reversal is invalid: a trust cannot become guaranteed by stipulation because no cause for liability exists, and a guaranteed contract cannot become a trust because the cause remains. The second view, attributed to Ahmad, accepts reversal based on the hadith 'al-muslimun 'ala shurutihim'.
The second view relies on the general principle of honoring conditions.
3
Examine the proofs
Ibn Qudamah uses analogical reasoning (qiyas): stipulating guarantee for a trust is like stipulating guarantee for a deposit or property in the owner’s hands—no basis. The second view uses a textual proof (dalil naqli) from the hadith.
The argument type is dalil naqli for the second view, but the first view also employs qiyas.
4
Identify the tarjih (preferred position)
The passage states that Ibn Qudamah's view is 'zahir al-madhhab' (the apparent position of the Hanbali school). This means the operative fatwa within Hanbali tradition follows the rejection of reversal.
Tarjih is based on the weight of the riwayah and the strength of the analogy.
5
Compare with the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school generally aligns with the first Hanbali view: the contract’s muqtada cannot be altered by stipulation because implied terms are binding. However, Hanafis may allow exceptions via custom or specific contracts (e.g., istisna).
This comparison helps you see the broader madhhab landscape when issuing fatwas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٤٢) الاجتهادات في سلطان الإرادة العقدية وقد يتوسعون أحياناً في المنع فيحكمون بفساد شروط لا لأنها تنافي مقتضى العقد، بل لأنَّ العقد لا يقتضيها كاشتراط منفعة الراهن للمرتهن (ر) المغني أيضاً). والظاهر أن منعهم هذا الشرط في الرهن لما فيه من شبهة الربا عن الحق المرهون فيه وإلا فإنّ قواعدهم ونصوصهم في البيع وغيره تقتضي صحة اشتراط المنافع الزائدة على أصل مقتضى العقد. ٨/٤٢ ـ وقد اختلفت الروايات عن أحمد في صحة اشتراط قلب مقتضى العقد من حكم الضمان إلى حكم الأمانة، وبالعكس. فقد قال شمس الدين بن قدامة المقدسي في الشرح الكبير : وإن اشترط المستعير نفي ضمان العارية لم يسقط الضمان . وكل ما كان أمانة لا يصير مضموناً باشتراط ضمانه، لأن مقتضى العقد كونه أمانة فإذا شرط ضمانه فقد التزم ضمان ما لم يوجد سبب لضمانه فلا يلزمه كما لو اشترط ضمان الوديعة أو ضمان مال في يد مالكه . وما كان مضموناً لا ينتفي ضمانه بشرطه لأن مقتضى العقد الضمان، فإذا شرط نفي ضمانه لا ينتفي مع وجود سببه كما لو اشترط نفي ضمان ما يتعدى فيه وعن أحمد أنه ذكر له ذلك فقال : المؤمنون على شروطهم؛ وهذا يدل على نفي الضمان بشرطه ولكن الأول هو ظاهر المذهب». (ر: الشرح الكبير، كتاب العارية ٣٦٦/٤ - ٣٧٧) . فأنت ترى من هذا أن مسؤوليات العاقدين الأساسية الناشئة عن طبيعة العقد يتقبل الاجتهاد الحنبلي انقلابها بالشرط. ١/٤٢ - مزايا المذهب الحنبلي في سلطان الإرادة إجمالاً: وهذا الاجتهاد الحنبلي، وما على أساسه وغراره في فهم نصوص ٥٥٧
Section (42): Ijtihadat on the Authority of Contractual Will. And they may sometimes be expansive in prohibition, judging conditions invalid not because they contradict the requirements of the contract, but because the contract does not necessitate them, such as stipulating a benefit for the creditor from the pledge (reference: al-Mughni). And apparently, their prohibition of this condition in the pledge is due to a suspicion of riba regarding the pledged right, otherwise their rules and texts in sale and elsewhere dictate the validity of stipulating additional benefits beyond the original requirements of the contract. 8/42 - And the narrations from Ahmad differed on the validity of stipulating the reversal of the contract's requirement from a guarantee (daman) to a trust (amanah), and vice versa. Shams al-Din ibn Qudamah al-Maqdisi said in al-Sharh al-Kabir: If the borrower stipulates the negation of liability for a borrowed item (ariyah), the liability is not waived. And everything that is a trust does not become guaranteed by stipulating its guarantee, because the contractual requirement is that it is a trust; if he stipulates its guarantee, he has committed to guarantee something for which no cause exists, so it is not binding on him, just as if he stipulated guarantee for a deposit (wadi'ah) or for property in the possession of its owner. And what is guaranteed does not have its liability negated by stipulation, because the contractual requirement is liability; if he stipulates the negation of its liability, it is not negated while its cause exists, just as if one stipulated negation of liability for something in which transgression occurs. And it is reported from Ahmad that he was asked about this and said: 'The believers are bound by their conditions'; this indicates negation of liability by stipulation, but the first is the apparent position of the madhhab. (Reference: al-Sharh al-Kabir, Book of Ariyah, 4/366-377). So you see from this that the basic responsibilities of the contracting parties arising from the nature of the contract, according to Hanbali ijtihad, can be reversed by stipulation. 1/42 - The merits of the Hanbali school regarding contractual will in summary: And this Hanbali ijtihad, and on its basis and pattern in understanding the texts...
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This section deals with the Hanbali school's approach to contractual conditions, specifically regarding the power of the parties' will to alter the inherent nature of contracts. The author first notes that Hanbali scholars sometimes prohibit conditions not because they contradict the contract's muqtada, but because they introduce elements not required by it, like stipulating a benefit for the creditor in a pledge (fearing riba). However, they generally allow stipulating additional benefits beyond the original purpose. The core of the passage addresses a key dispute among Hanbali scholars: can a condition reverse the nature of a contract from guarantee to trust or vice versa? For example, a borrowed item is normally a trust (not liable unless the borrower transgresses); can the parties stipulate that it becomes guaranteed (liable regardless)? Ibn Qudamah holds that such a stipulation is ineffective because it contradicts the contract's nature. He analogizes to depositing (wadi'ah) or property in the owner's hands. Similarly, a contract that is inherently guaranteeing (like a loan or guarantee) cannot be changed to a trust by stipulating no liability. However, there is a narration from Imam Ahmad that suggests the opposite, citing the hadith 'The believers are bound by their conditions,' which would support the validity of reversing liability by condition. The author states that the first view (Ibn Qudamah) is the apparent position of the madhhab (zahir al-madhhab). The passage ends by noting that the Hanbali ijtihad generally accepts that basic responsibilities can be reversed by stipulation, indicating a flexible approach to contractual will.

Key Terms
قلب مقتضى العقد (qalb muqtada al-'aqd): Reversing the inherent requirement of a contract, such as changing the nature from a trust (amanah) to a guarantee (daman) or vice versa.
ضمان (daman): Liability for loss or damage of an item in one's possession; a contract that imposes liability regardless of negligence (e.g., loan, lease).
أمانة (amanah): Trust; an item held without liability except in cases of transgression (e.g., borrowed item, deposit).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the apparent position of the Hanbali school (zahir al-madhhab), what happens if a borrower stipulates that he will not be liable for a borrowed item?
A) The stipulation is valid and releases the borrower from liability.
B) The stipulation is invalid and the liability is not waived.
C) The stipulation is valid only if it is in writing.
D) The stipulation is valid only if the lender agrees to compensation.

Key Takeaway
The apparent Hanbali position, like the Hanafi, rejects conditions that reverse the inherent liability nature of contracts, despite a dissenting narration from Ahmad based on 'al-muslimun 'ala shurutihim'.

Hanbali Approach to Contractual Conditions: Permissiveness, Internal Dispute, and Merits
Comparative fiqh discussion on Hanbali approach to contractual conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanbali school's permissive stance on contractual conditions, identify the intra-Hanbali dispute on reversing default contract effects (muqtada), and evaluate the school's evidence from the hadith 'al-mu'minun 'ala shurutihim' and traditions.

Core Idea
Hanbali fiqh permits conditions that reverse default contract implications, grounded in the hadith and extensive prophetic traditions.

This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm examines the Hanbali school's distinctive approach to party autonomy. It first presents the general principle allowing conditions that add benefits beyond the contract's default requirements, then delves into an internal dispute about whether conditions can reverse the default nature of a contract (e.g., from liability to trust). Finally, it praises the Hanbali reasoning as groundbreaking and rooted in deep knowledge of prophetic and companion traditions. For a Hanafi student, this comparison highlights the flexibility within Islamic law when textual evidence supports broader party autonomy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Hanbali Principle
Find the statement about conditions that are not required by the contract but add extra benefits. The author notes that Hanbali rules in sale and other contracts permit such stipulations, except when there is a suspicion of riba.
The example given is stipulating a benefit for the pawner from the pawnbroker – prohibited due to riba suspicion, not due to contradicting muqtada.
2
Examine the Intra-Hanbali Dispute
Read the section 8/42 about differing reports from Ahmad on whether a condition can reverse the default liability/trust nature. Identify the two positions: (1) a trust cannot become liable by stipulation, and vice versa (Ibn Qudamah's apparent position); (2) the hadith 'al-mu'minun...' suggests that such conditions are effective.
Imam Ahmad is reported to have cited the hadith, but the school's apparent position is the first.
3
Analyze the Evidence and Basis
Understand that the Hanbali school's permissiveness is based on the hadith and a wealth of narrations from the Prophet and Companions about valid contracts and conditions. Ibn Taymiyyah and Abu Zahra are cited to support this.
The author emphasizes that Hanbali flexibility in transactions contrasts with their strictness in worship.
4
Contrast with Hanafi School
Note that the Hanafi school also recognizes party autonomy but limits it to conditions that do not contradict the express requirements (muqtada) of the contract. The Hanbali school allows conditions that reverse muqtada as long as there is a textual basis.
This distinction has practical fatwa implications when choosing between schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٤٢) الاجتهادات في سلطان الإرادة العقدية
وقد يتوسعون أحياناً في المنع فيحكمون بفساد شروط لا لأنها تنافي مقتضى العقد، بل لأنَّ العقد لا يقتضيها كاشتراط منفعة الراهن للمرتهن (ر) المغني أيضاً).
والظاهر أن منعهم هذا الشرط في الرهن لما فيه من شبهة الربا عن الحق المرهون فيه وإلا فإنّ قواعدهم ونصوصهم في البيع وغيره تقتضي صحة اشتراط المنافع الزائدة على أصل مقتضى العقد.
٨/٤٢ ـ وقد اختلفت الروايات عن أحمد في صحة اشتراط قلب مقتضى العقد من حكم الضمان إلى حكم الأمانة، وبالعكس. فقد قال شمس الدين بن قدامة المقدسي في الشرح الكبير : وإن اشترط المستعير نفي ضمان العارية لم يسقط الضمان . وكل ما كان أمانة لا يصير مضموناً باشتراط ضمانه، لأن مقتضى العقد كونه أمانة فإذا شرط ضمانه فقد التزم ضمان ما لم يوجد سبب لضمانه فلا يلزمه كما لو اشترط ضمان الوديعة أو ضمان مال في يد مالكه . وما كان مضموناً لا ينتفي ضمانه بشرطه لأن مقتضى العقد الضمان، فإذا شرط نفي ضمانه لا ينتفي مع وجود سببه كما لو اشترط نفي ضمان ما يتعدى فيه وعن أحمد أنه ذكر له ذلك فقال : المؤمنون على شروطهم؛ وهذا يدل على نفي الضمان بشرطه ولكن الأول هو ظاهر المذهب». (ر: الشرح الكبير، كتاب العارية ٣٦٦/٤ - ٣٧٧) .
فأنت ترى من هذا أن مسؤوليات العاقدين الأساسية الناشئة عن طبيعة العقد يتقبل الاجتهاد الحنبلي انقلابها بالشرط.
١/٤٢ - مزايا المذهب الحنبلي في سلطان الإرادة إجمالاً:
وهذا الاجتهاد الحنبلي، وما على أساسه وغراره في فهم نصوص الشريعة حول مبدأ سلطان الإرادة العقدية لا ينقضي منه إعجاب المتأمل ؛ وهو الاجتهاد الجدير بالخلود. فهو في باب العقود والشروط كالأفق الفسيح واسع الحدود، ولكن حدوده هي الطبيعة نفسها !! ولا سيما إذا عرفنا أن مبدأ سلطان الإرادة الذي قرره الاجتهاد الحنبلي منذ اثني عشر قرناً، استنباطاً من نصوص الشريعة الإسلامية الخصيبة وأصولها المحكمة الواضحة، لم تكن لتعرفه أو تفهمه الشرائع العالمية والفقه الروماني، ولم تنتبه إليه الأفكار التشريعية والاجتماعية في أوربا إلا منذ قرنين كما سنرى، مع أن الإمام أحمد بن حنبل صاحب المذهب رحمه الله تعالى، معدود من فقهاء مدرسة الحديث لا من مدرسة الرأي (ر: ف ١/٥٣ الحاشية)(١)
على أنَّ ابن تيمية رحمه الله ان یری عمق اطلاع أحمد وتمكنه في الآثار الثابتة من الحديث والسنة هو السبب في سماحة مذهبه التعاملي، إذ يقول: «ليس في الفقهاء الأربعة أكثر تصحيحاً للشروط من أحمد. وإن عامة ما يصححه من العقود والشروط له دليل شرعي خاص من أثر أو قياس فلا يعارض ذلك بأنه مخالف لمقتضى العقد. وقد بلغه في العقود والشروط من الآثار عن النبي و عن الصحابة ما لم يجده عند غيره من الأئمة.
وهكذا أيضاً یری الأستاذ الجليل الشيخ محمد أبو زهرة في كتابه «ابن حنبل : أنَّ علم أحمد بالآثار كان يسعفه في فتح أبواب للشروط ظن غيره أنها مغلقة فدراسة أحمد للآثار جعلته يفهم أن منطق الفقه الأثري يوجب الإطلاق والإباحة حتى يقوم دليل التقييد والمنع (ر) کتاب ابن حنبل ف/ ۲۲۸ - ۲۲۹) .
(1) يلحظ أن الاجتهاد الحنبلي في أمور العبادات وتطهير النجاسات والتورع هو غالباً أضيق من سواه، مما جعله في نظر الناس عنواناً على التشدد في الدين، عكس ما هو السعة والسماحة في أحكام التعامل بالعقود والشروط مما لم يتسع له غيره من المذاهب .
Chapter (42): Juristic reasoning regarding the authority of will in contracts. And sometimes they widen the prohibition, ruling on the invalidity of conditions not because they contradict the requirement of the contract, but because the contract does not necessitate them, such as stipulating a benefit for the pawnbroker from the pawner (see al-Mughni). And apparently their prohibition of this condition in pawn is due to a suspicion of usury relating to the right pawned. Otherwise, their rules and texts in sale and other contracts necessitate the validity of stipulating additional benefits beyond the original requirement of the contract. 8/42 - And there has been a difference in the reports from Ahmad regarding the validity of stipulating to reverse the requirement of the contract from liability to trustworthiness, and vice versa. Shams al-Din ibn Qudamah al-Maqdisi said in al-Sharh al-Kabir: 'If the borrower stipulates the negation of liability for the loan, it does not drop liability. And everything that is a trust does not become liable by stipulating its liability, because the requirement of the contract is that it is a trust. So if he stipulates its liability, he has undertaken liability for something for which no cause of liability exists, and thus it is not binding, just as if he stipulated liability for a deposit or for property in the hands of its owner. And what is liable does not have its liability negated by stipulation, because the requirement of the contract is liability. So if he stipulates negation of its liability, it is not negated while its cause exists, just as if one stipulates negation of liability for what is subject to transgression.' And it is reported from Ahmad that he was mentioned this and said: 'The believers are bound by their conditions.' This indicates the negation of liability by stipulation, but the first is the apparent position of the school. (see al-Sharh al-Kabir, Book of Loan, 4/366-377). So you see from this that the basic responsibilities of the contracting parties arising from the nature of the contract are accepted by Hanbali reasoning to be reversed by condition. 1/42 - Merits of the Hanbali school regarding the authority of will in summary: This Hanbali reasoning, and what is based on it and in its manner in understanding the texts of sacred law regarding the principle of the authority of will in contracts, does not cease to amaze the contemplator; it is reasoning worthy of immortality. In the area of contracts and conditions, it is like a vast horizon with broad limits, but its limits are nature itself! Especially when we know that the principle of the authority of will established by Hanbali reasoning twelve centuries ago, deduced from the fertile texts of Islamic law and its well-established and clear principles, was not known or understood by the universal legal systems and Roman law, and legislative and social thought in Europe did not become aware of it until two centuries ago, as we will see, even though Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, the founder of the school, may Allah have mercy on him, is counted among the jurists of the hadith school and not the school of opinion (see section 1/53 footnote). Moreover, Ibn Taymiyyah, may Allah have mercy on him, saw that the depth of Ahmad's knowledge and his mastery of the established narrations from hadith and sunnah is the reason for the leniency of his transactional school, as he says: 'There is no one among the four jurists who validates more conditions than Ahmad. Indeed, most of what he validates from contracts and conditions have a specific legal evidence from a narration or analogy, and that is not opposed by saying it contradicts the requirement of the contract. And he has received in the matter of contracts and conditions such narrations from the Prophet and the Companions as not found by any other imam.' Similarly, the esteemed scholar Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra sees in his book 'Ibn Hanbal' that Ahmad's knowledge of narrations enabled him to open doors for conditions that others thought were closed. Ahmad's study of narrations made him understand that the logic of tradition-based jurisprudence requires absoluteness and permissibility until a proof of restriction and prohibition arises (see his book Ibn Hanbal, pp. 228-229). (1) Note that the Hanbali reasoning in matters of worship, purification of impurities, and scrupulousness is often narrower than others, which made it in people's eyes a sign of strictness in religion, contrary to the breadth and tolerance in the rulings of transactions through contracts and conditions, which no other school has matched.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm discusses the Hanbali school's distinctive approach to the principle of party autonomy (sultan al-irada) in contracts. The author begins by noting that although some jurists prohibit conditions that do not conflict with the contract's default implications (muqtada al-'aqd) but are merely not necessitated by it, the Hanbali school is more permissive. An example given is stipulating a benefit for the pawner from the pawnbroker; the prohibition in pawn is due to a suspicion of riba, but otherwise Hanbali rules permit such stipulations. The core of the passage then examines a disagreement within the Hanbali school itself regarding whether conditions can reverse the default nature of a contract—e.g., from liability (daman) to trust (amanah) or vice versa. Ibn Qudamah presents the 'apparent' position of the school that such a reversal is ineffective: a trust does not become liable by stipulation, nor does liability become a trust. However, Imam Ahmad is reported to have cited the hadith 'al-mu'minun 'ala shurutihim' (believers are bound by their conditions), which suggests that the condition could negate liability. The author then concludes that the Hanbali school indeed allows the inversion of basic contractual responsibilities by condition. The passage praises Hanbali reasoning as groundbreaking, pre-modern, and based on a deep knowledge of prophetic and companion traditions. It cites Ibn Taymiyyah and Abu Zahra to support the claim that Ahmad ibn Hanbal validated more conditions than other imams because of his extensive familiarity with the textual sources. A footnote clarifies that Hanbali strictness in worship contrasts with its leniency in transactions. For the Hanafi student, this comparative analysis highlights the flexibility that can exist within the shari'ah when contractual freedom is prioritized, though the Hanafi school takes a somewhat more restrictive view on conditions that contravene the explicit requirements of the contract.

Key Terms
سلطان الإرادة العقدية (sultan al-irada al-'aqdiyya): The authority of the will in contracts; party autonomy to stipulate conditions that may alter default legal effects.
مقتضى العقد (muqtada al-'aqd): The default legal implication or requirement of a contract type (e.g., sale implies ownership transfer, loan implies trust).
شرط (shart): A stipulation or condition imposed by the contracting parties; can be valid or invalid depending on its relation to the contract's requirements.
ضمان (daman): Liability; the legal responsibility for loss or damage, typically imposed on a person who has possession of another's property under certain contracts (e.g., loan, lease with liability).
أمانة (amanah): Trust; a contract where the possessor is not liable for loss unless guilty of negligence (e.g., deposit, loan).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the Hanbali school's broad acceptance of contractual conditions that reverse default legal effects?
A) Analogy to sale contracts
B) The hadith 'al-mu'minun 'ala shurutihim' and numerous narrations from the Prophet and Companions
C) The principle of necessity
D) The Hanbali school follows the opinion of the fuqaha' of Madinah

Key Takeaway
The Hanbali school's validation of conditions that reverse default contract effects is a landmark in Islamic legal theory, rooted in tradition-based flexibility, and stands in contrast to the Hanafi principle that conditions must not contradict muqtada.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Hanbali Principle of Permissibility in Contracts and Conditions
Explains the Hanbali principle of permissibility in contracts and its application to implied and customary conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanbali legal maxim 'الأصل في العقود والشروط الإباحة' and apply it to at least two furu' cases, distinguishing valid conditions from those prohibited by explicit texts.

Core Idea
Hanbali school presumes all contracts and conditions permissible until explicit Sharia restriction.

This lesson explores the foundational Hanbali maxim governing contracts, which grants broad freedom to contracting parties. The maxim is derived from the school's hadith-based methodology and is applied consistently across transactional law, including marriage. Understanding this principle is crucial for comparative fiqh and mufti training.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maxim and Its Scope
Locate the Hanbali maxim 'الأصل في العقود والشروط الإباحة حتى يقوم دليل التقييد والمنع' in the passage and understand it governs all contracts and conditions.
The maxim is not explicitly stated in one sentence but is summarized by the commentator.
2
Understand the Derivation from Hanbali Methodology
Note how Imam Ahmad's deep knowledge of hadith and Sunnah leads to this broad permissibility, as quoted from Ibn Taymiyyah and Abu Zahra.
This methodology is termed 'figh athari' by the author.
3
Apply to Implied Conditions (al-shart al-malhuz)
The Hanbali school treats conditions agreed before the contract as binding even if not expressed later, based on the command to fulfill conditions.
Contrast with schools that require explicit stipulation.
4
Apply to Customary Conditions (al-ma'ruf 'urfan)
Customarily known terms are treated as if they were stipulated, as stated: 'المعروف عرفاً كالمشروط شرطاً'.
This sub-maxim (dabt) parallels the Hanafi maxim 'العادة محكمة'.
5
Identify the Istithna (Prohibited Conditions)
Conditions that contradict explicit Sharia texts or reduce obligatory rights are not permissible. This is the exception to the general permissibility.
The passage mentions 'ما يمنعه الشرع بنص خاص' or 'ما ينقص الحقوق والواجبات'.

Chapter Six: The theory of contracts and related matters in Islamic jurisprudence concerning the principle of the authority of the contractual will. The admiration of the contemplator is never exhausted; it is an ijtihad worthy of eternity. In the chapter of contracts and conditions, it is like a vast horizon with wide boundaries, but its boundaries are nature itself. Especially when we know that the principle of the authority of the will, established by Hanbali ijtihad twelve centuries ago, derived from the fertile texts of Islamic Sharia and its firm clear principles, was not known or understood by universal laws and Roman jurisprudence, and European legislative and social ideas did not pay attention to it until two centuries ago, as we will see, even though Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, the founder of the school, may Allah have mercy on him, is counted among the jurists of the school of hadith, not the school of opinion (see: F 1/53 footnote). Moreover, Ibn Taymiyyah, may Allah have mercy on him, sees that the depth of Ahmad's knowledge and his mastery of the established narrations from hadith and Sunnah is the reason for the leniency of his transactional school. He says: 'None of the four jurists corrects more conditions than Ahmad. Most of what he validates from contracts and conditions has a specific Sharia evidence from narration or analogy, so it is not objected that it contradicts the requirement of the contract. He received narrations about contracts from the Prophet and the Companions that other imams did not find.' Likewise, the venerable professor Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra says in his book 'Ibn Hanbal': 'Ahmad's knowledge of narrations enabled him to open doors of conditions that others thought were closed, because his study of narrations made him understand that the logic of athari jurisprudence necessitates permissibility and allowance until evidence of restriction and prohibition arises.' (See: Ibn Hanbal book, pp. 228-229). (1) It is noted that Hanbali ijtihad in matters of worship, purification of impurities, and scrupulousness is often narrower than others, which made it, in people's eyes, a sign of strictness in religion, contrary to the breadth and leniency in rulings on transactions of contracts and conditions, which no other school has expanded upon. Chapter (42): Ijtihadat on the Authority of the Contractual Will. 10/42 - The Hanbali ijtihad equated in consideration between conditions stipulated in the body of the contract and conditions agreed upon before the contract, even if not expressed at the time of contracting, as long as the contracting party relied on them; because the command to fulfill conditions, contracts, and covenants covers both situations. This is the apparent view of Ahmad, and he affirmed it in 'Al-Muntaha', and said in 'Al-Insaf': it is the correct view about which there is no doubt. (See: Kashshaf al-Qina' 3/52). Thus, Ahmad considered an implied condition like an expressed condition, and also considered what is known by custom as a condition as if it were stipulated. The aspects in which the advantages of the Hanbali school in the authority of the will are manifested. 11/42 - Among the aspects that draw attention from Hanbali ijtihad on the freedom of conditions are six important aspects of the principle of the authority of the will, whose value is known in the balance of modern legal jurisprudence. They are: 12/42 - The first aspect: The Hanbali ijtihad did not differentiate between the marriage contract and others in the freedom to stipulate conditions and their binding force within its general limits previously mentioned. It allowed spouses to stipulate whatever they wish in the marriage contract of rights, interests, and conditions that do not contradict the subject of marriage and its Sharia system in Islam. Imam Ahmad, based on this principle, permitted a woman to stipulate that she not travel with her husband, or that he not move her from her home, or that he not marry another woman, or that one of the spouses stipulate that the other be wealthy, etc. Every valid condition stipulated by one of the spouses, if not fulfilled, permits the other spouse to annul the marriage. What is prevented from conditions in marriage is only what is prevented by Sharia with a specific text, or what reduces the rights and duties that are part of the Sharia system in marriage, as
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanbali school's principle of freedom of contract (سلطان الإرادة) and its application to marriage conditions. The author begins by praising the Hanbali ijtihad for establishing this principle twelve centuries ago, deriving it from revelatory texts (nusus), unlike Western legal systems that discovered it only recently. He notes that Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal belonged to the school of hadith, not ra'y, yet his methodology led to broad permissibility in contracts and conditions. Ibn Taymiyyah is quoted to support this: Ahmad validated more conditions than other imams because he found specific textual evidence for them. The passage then summarizes the Hanbali position: the default in contracts and conditions is permissibility (ibaha) until evidence of restriction appears. A key sub-maxim is that conditions agreed before the contract are treated like those expressed in the contract, and customarily known terms are like stipulated terms. The first of six aspects is presented: the Hanbali school applies the same freedom of conditions to marriage as to other contracts. Spouses may stipulate a wide range of conditions, such as a wife stipulating that her husband not travel or not marry another wife. If such a valid condition is breached, the other spouse may annul the marriage. The only prohibited conditions are those that contradict explicit Sharia texts or that reduce the essential rights and duties of marriage. The passage underscores that marital conditions are valid and binding as long as they do not conflict with the core Sharia framework.

Key Terms
سلطان الإرادة (sultan al-irada): The authority of the will in contracts; the principle that contracting parties are free to set terms within Sharia limits.
الشرط الملحوظ (al-shart al-malhuz): An implied condition that the parties contemplated but did not explicitly express in the contract.
المعروف عرفاً (al-ma'ruf 'urfan): What is customarily recognized; custom has the force of a stipulated condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanbali school, what is the ruling on a condition that was agreed upon before the contract but not mentioned during the contract?
A) It is invalid because it was not part of the contract.
B) It is valid and binding if the parties relied on it.
C) It is valid only if the other party accepts it after the contract.
D) It is invalid because it creates uncertainty.

Key Takeaway
The Hanbali school's default position in contracts and conditions is permissibility, with exceptions only for conditions that explicitly contradict Sharia or reduce obligatory rights.

Hanbali Application to Marriage Conditions
Explains the Hanbali school's permissive approach to conditions in the marriage contract.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanbali view on stipulating conditions in the marriage contract and identify which conditions are prohibited, and contrast this with the Hanafi approach based on the text.

Core Idea
Hanbali school applies the same freedom of conditions to marriage as to other contracts, allowing spouses to stipulate a wide range of terms.

This lesson examines the first of six aspects of the Hanbali principle: the equal treatment of marriage conditions. The school permits spouses to stipulate many conditions, such as not traveling or not marrying another wife, and allows annulment if breached. Understanding this helps muftis appreciate the flexibility in Hanbali law and the boundaries set by Sharia.

Guided Path
1
Note the Non-Differentiation Principle
The passage states that Hanbali ijtihad did not differentiate between marriage and other contracts in terms of freedom to stipulate conditions.
This is explicitly stated: 'لم يفرق بين عقد النكاح وغيره'.
2
List Permissible Marriage Conditions from the Passage
Identify examples: wife may stipulate not traveling with husband, not being moved from her home, or that husband not marry another wife; also, any condition that does not conflict with the Sharia system of marriage.
These are listed in the passage after 'أجاز الإمام أحمد'.
3
Identify the Consequence of Breach
If a valid condition is not fulfilled, the other spouse has the right to annul the marriage (فسخ النكاح).
This is explicitly stated: 'إذا لم يتحقق يسوغ للزوج الآخر فسخ النكاح'.
4
Identify Prohibited Conditions
Two categories: (1) conditions prevented by a specific text, (2) conditions that reduce the rights and duties that are part of the Sharia system in marriage.
Examples of the latter would be stipulating no maintenance or no conjugal rights.
5
Contrast with General Approach of Other Schools
The passage implies that other schools differentiate marriage from other contracts and are more restrictive. Note that the Hanafi school, for instance, generally invalidates conditions that conflict with the purpose of marriage.
This inference is drawn from the phrase 'لم يفرق...' which implies others differentiate.

Chapter Six: The theory of contracts and related matters in Islamic jurisprudence concerning the principle of the authority of the contractual will. The admiration of the contemplator is never exhausted; it is an ijtihad worthy of eternity. In the chapter of contracts and conditions, it is like a vast horizon with wide boundaries, but its boundaries are nature itself. Moreover, Ibn Taymiyyah, may Allah have mercy on him, sees that the depth of Ahmad's knowledge and his mastery of the established narrations from hadith and Sunnah is the reason for the leniency of his transactional school. He says: 'None of the four jurists corrects more conditions than Ahmad. Most of what he validates from contracts and conditions has a specific Sharia evidence from narration or analogy, so it is not objected that it contradicts the requirement of the contract. He received narrations about contracts from the Prophet and the Companions that other imams did not find.' Likewise, the venerable professor Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra says in his book 'Ibn Hanbal': 'Ahmad's knowledge of narrations enabled him to open doors of conditions that others thought were closed, because his study of narrations made him understand that the logic of athari jurisprudence necessitates permissibility and allowance until evidence of restriction and prohibition arises.' (See: Ibn Hanbal book, pp. 228-229). (1) It is noted that Hanbali ijtihad in matters of worship, purification of impurities, and scrupulousness is often narrower than others, which made it, in people's eyes, a sign of strictness in religion, contrary to the breadth and leniency in rulings on transactions of contracts and conditions, which no other school has expanded upon. Chapter (42): Ijtihadat on the Authority of the Contractual Will. 10/42 - The Hanbali ijtihad equated in consideration between conditions stipulated in the body of the contract and conditions agreed upon before the contract, even if not expressed at the time of contracting, as long as the contracting party relied on them; because the command to fulfill conditions, contracts, and covenants covers both situations. This is the apparent view of Ahmad, and he affirmed it in 'Al-Muntaha', and said in 'Al-Insaf': it is the correct view about which there is no doubt. (See: Kashshaf al-Qina' 3/52). Thus, Ahmad considered an implied condition like an expressed condition, and also considered what is known by custom as a condition as if it were stipulated. The aspects in which the advantages of the Hanbali school in the authority of the will are manifested. 11/42 - Among the aspects that draw attention from Hanbali ijtihad on the freedom of conditions are six important aspects of the principle of the authority of the will, whose value is known in the balance of modern legal jurisprudence. They are: 12/42 - The first aspect: The Hanbali ijtihad did not differentiate between the marriage contract and others in the freedom to stipulate conditions and their binding force within its general limits previously mentioned. It allowed spouses to stipulate whatever they wish in the marriage contract of rights, interests, and conditions that do not contradict the subject of marriage and its Sharia system in Islam. Imam Ahmad, based on this principle, permitted a woman to stipulate that she not travel with her husband, or that he not move her from her home, or that he not marry another woman, or that one of the spouses stipulate that the other be wealthy, etc. Every valid condition stipulated by one of the spouses, if not fulfilled, permits the other spouse to annul the marriage. What is prevented from conditions in marriage is only what is prevented by Sharia with a specific text, or what reduces the rights and duties that are part of the Sharia system in marriage, as
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanbali school's principle of freedom of contract (سلطان الإرادة) and its application to marriage conditions. The author begins by praising the Hanbali ijtihad for establishing this principle twelve centuries ago, deriving it from revelatory texts (nusus), unlike Western legal systems that discovered it only recently. He notes that Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal belonged to the school of hadith, not ra'y, yet his methodology led to broad permissibility in contracts and conditions. Ibn Taymiyyah is quoted to support this: Ahmad validated more conditions than other imams because he found specific textual evidence for them. The passage then summarizes the Hanbali position: the default in contracts and conditions is permissibility (ibaha) until evidence of restriction appears. A key sub-maxim is that conditions agreed before the contract are treated like those expressed in the contract, and customarily known terms are like stipulated terms. The first of six aspects is presented: the Hanbali school applies the same freedom of conditions to marriage as to other contracts. Spouses may stipulate a wide range of conditions, such as a wife stipulating that her husband not travel or not marry another wife. If such a valid condition is breached, the other spouse may annul the marriage. The only prohibited conditions are those that contradict explicit Sharia texts or that reduce the essential rights and duties of marriage. The passage underscores that marital conditions are valid and binding as long as they do not conflict with the core Sharia framework.

Key Terms
سلطان الإرادة (sultan al-irada): The authority of the will in contracts; the principle that contracting parties are free to set terms within Sharia limits.
الشرط الملحوظ (al-shart al-malhuz): An implied condition that the parties contemplated but did not explicitly express in the contract.
المعروف عرفاً (al-ma'ruf 'urfan): What is customarily recognized; custom has the force of a stipulated condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanbali school allows broad stipulations in marriage contracts, with annulment as a remedy for breach, except for conditions that contradict explicit texts or reduce essential Sharia rights.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Hanbali Ijtihad on Binding Conditions in Contracts and Marriage
Explaining the Hanbali school's expansive view on conditions in contracts, especially marriage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanbali principle that equates pre-agreed conditions with explicit ones and evaluate its application in marriage contracts, comparing it with Hanafi views.

Core Idea
In Hanbali fiqh, a condition kept in mind is treated as if uttered, making prenuptial agreements binding even if not written.

The Hanafi school has detailed rules on conditions in contracts, generally requiring explicit stipulation at the time of contract. The Hanbali school offers a more expansive approach, treating pre-agreed conditions and customary practices as binding. This lesson explores the Hanbali ijtihad, its evidence, and its application, particularly in marriage, to help you understand a different legal philosophy within Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key Hanbali principles
The passage states two principles: (1) A condition kept in mind (al-shart al-malhuz) is like a condition uttered (al-shart al-malfuz). (2) What is known by custom (al-ma'ruf 'urfan) is like a stipulated condition. Identify these in the Arabic text.
These principles expand the scope of binding conditions beyond explicit statements.
2
Examine the evidence from hadith and precedent
The Hanbali ijtihad relies on the Prophet's hadith: 'The conditions most worthy of fulfillment are those by which you make private parts lawful' (Bukhari). Also, Umar ibn al-Khattab's ruling: 'Rights are limited by conditions.' Understand how these support the Hanbali position.
The hadith is general, but Hanbalis apply it broadly to all marriage conditions.
3
Analyze the scope: conditions in marriage contracts
The passage explains that the Hanbali school does not differentiate between marriage and other contracts regarding freedom of conditions. Spouses may stipulate any rights or circumstances that do not contradict the essence of marriage, such as no travel, no polygyny, or financial status.
Contrast this with Hanafi views: Hanafis generally require conditions to be in line with the nature of marriage and often limit them.
4
Distinguish valid from prohibited conditions
Valid conditions are those not prohibited by specific text and not reducing essential marital rights (e.g., mahr, nafaqa, istimta'). Prohibited examples: temporary marriage (nikah mut'ah), no dowry, no maintenance. Identify the line drawn by the Hanbali school.
The prohibition of certain conditions is based on explicit Sharia texts, not mere policy.
5
Note the internal Hanbali debate on option clauses
The passage mentions that some Hanbali jurists allow stipulating an option period (khiyar) in marriage, as in commercial contracts. If the period lapses without annulment, the marriage is confirmed. This was debated within the school.
This shows that even within the expansive Hanbali approach, there was ikhtilaf on specific matters.

Chapter (42) Ijtihads on the Authority of Contractual Will. 10/42 - The Hanbali ijtihad equalized in consideration between conditions stipulated in the body of the contract and conditions agreed upon before the contract, even if not explicitly stated at the time of contracting, as long as the contracting party relied on them, because the command to fulfill conditions, contracts, and covenants encompasses both cases. This is the apparent position of Ahmad, and it is affirmed in 'Al-Muntaha', and he said in 'Al-Insaf': it is the correct position about which there is no doubt. (See: Kashshaf al-Qina' 3/52). Thus, Ahmad considered a condition that is kept in mind as a condition that is uttered, and he also considered what is customary as a condition stipulated. The aspects in which the advantages of the Hanbali school in the authority of will manifest. 11/42 - Among the matters worthy of consideration from the Hanbali ijtihad on freedom of conditions are six important aspects of the principle of the authority of will, whose value is known in the balance of modern legal jurisprudence, namely: 12/42 - The first aspect: The Hanbali ijtihad did not differentiate between the marriage contract and others in the freedom to stipulate conditions and their binding strength within its general limits previously mentioned. It permitted spouses to stipulate whatever they wish in the marriage contract of rights, interests, and circumstances that do not conflict with the subject of marriage and its legal system in Islam. Imam Ahmad, based on this principle, allowed a woman to stipulate not to travel with her husband, or not to be moved from her house, or that he not marry over her, or for one spouse to stipulate that the other be wealthy, and so on. And every valid condition stipulated by one of the spouses, if it is not fulfilled, permits the other spouse to annul the marriage. The only conditions prohibited in marriage are those prohibited by a specific text of Sharia, or those that reduce the rights and obligations that are part of the legal system of marriage, such as stipulating a timed marriage, or no dowry, or no spousal maintenance, or no marital intimacy, and the like. Details of that are explained in the books of the school (see: Al-Mughni, Al-Sharh al-Kabir, Kashshaf al-Qina', and Kitab al-Furu' and its correction, chapter on conditions in marriage). 13/42 - The Hanbali ijtihad relies on the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'The conditions most worthy of fulfillment are those by which you make private parts lawful.' Thus, he did not differentiate between financial dowry conditions and other lawful interests of the spouses that are related to their will; rather, he considered conditions in marriage more deserving of adherence and stronger than others. The Hanbalis also adhere in this regard to what Imam al-Bukhari narrated in his Sahih regarding the judgment of Umar ibn al-Khattab, may God be pleased with him: A man married a woman and stipulated for her the right to reside in her house, then he wanted to move her, and they brought the case to Umar, who said: 'She has her condition.' The man said, 'Then they will divorce us!' So Umar answered him with that eternal constitutional phrase: 'Rights are limited by conditions, and you have what you stipulated.' (See: I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in 3/338, and Kashshaf al-Qina' 3/53). The Hanbali ijtihad also permitted, despite disagreement among the school's jurists, stipulating an option (khiyar) in the marriage contract for a specified period, as in exchange contracts. If the period passes without the husband who has the option annulling, the marriage becomes binding. (See: Kitab al-Furu' and its correction 3/60).
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
The passage presents the Hanbali school's approach to contractual conditions, specifically within the broader discussion of the authority of the will (سلطان الإرادة). It opens by establishing that the Hanbali ijtihad equates conditions explicitly stated in the contract with those agreed upon before the contract, even if unwritten, as long as the parties relied on them. This is based on the general command to fulfill conditions and covenants. The text notes that Imam Ahmad's position is affirmed in key Hanbali works, and it introduces two principles: (1) the condition kept in mind is like the condition uttered (الشرط الملحوظ كالشرط الملفوظ), and (2) what is customary is like a stipulated condition (المعروف عرفاً كالمشروط شرطاً). The passage then focuses on one of six aspects: the Hanbali school does not differentiate between marriage contracts and other contracts regarding freedom of conditions. It permits spouses to stipulate any rights, interests, or circumstances that do not oppose the essence of marriage, such as no travel or no polygyny. If such a valid condition is not fulfilled, the other spouse has the right to annul the marriage. The only prohibited conditions are those explicitly forbidden by Sharia or those that undermine essential marital rights (e.g., temporary marriage, no dowry, no maintenance). The evidence includes the hadith 'The conditions most worthy of fulfillment are those by which you make private parts lawful' and the precedent of Umar ibn al-Khattab ruling that rights are bound by conditions. The passage also mentions a debated issue within the Hanbali school: stipulating an option period in marriage contracts, akin to commercial contracts—if the period lapses without annulment, the marriage is confirmed.

Key Terms
سلطان الإرادة (sultan al-irada): The principle that individuals have the authority to bind themselves contractually according to their will, subject only to Sharia limits.
الشرط الملحوظ كالشرط الملفوظ (al-shart al-malhuz ka al-shart al-malfuz): A condition that is kept in mind (agreed upon but not recited in the contract) is treated as if it were explicitly stated.
المعروف عرفاً كالمشروط شرطاً (al-ma'ruf 'urfan ka al-mashrut shartan): What is known by custom is like a stipulated condition.

Key Takeaway
The Hanbali school's expansive approach to conditions in marriage is based on the hadith and the principle that implicit agreements are binding, allowing annulment for breach of valid conditions.

Hanbali Ijtihad on Restrictive Conditions in Sale
Explains Hanbali permissive stance on restrictive conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanbali justification for restrictive conditions in sale contracts and evaluate the Hanafi objection based on the nature of ownership.

Core Idea
Hanbali fiqh permits conditions that restrict ownership when they serve a legitimate interest; other schools prohibit them.

In this lesson we explore a fundamental difference in contractual philosophy between the Hanbali school and the other three major schools. The Hanbali approach grants parties greater freedom to tailor the effects of a sale through conditions that limit the buyer's absolute ownership. This contrasts sharply with the Hanafi view that ownership rights are fixed by Shari'ah and cannot be altered by agreement. Understanding this divergence is key to appreciating the scope of contractual autonomy in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanbali position
List the specific examples of restrictive conditions that the Hanbali school permits: non-resale, right of first refusal, retention of usufruct, requirement to endow or manumit.
These conditions restrict the buyer's freedom but serve a legitimate interest.
2
Understand the opposing view
State the reasoning of the other schools (Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i): that ownership rights are determined by Shari'ah and cannot be altered by the parties. The condition is void because it contradicts the nature of sale.
The core principle: 'ثمرات الملك وحقوقه لا يرتبها العاقد؛ وإنما يرتبها الشرع'.
3
Analyze the Hanbali justification
Explain that Shari'ah grants parties the authority to determine contractual effects (سلطان الإرادة العقدية). Conditions serving valid interests are acceptable, making ownership qualified rather than absolute.
This delegation is the basis for party autonomy.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
In Hanafi fiqh, such conditions are void but do not void the sale; in Hanbali, they are valid and binding. The consequence affects the enforceability of the condition.
The practical difference: a mufti's ruling on the same contract differs by school.

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه

١٥/٤ - الناحية الثالثة :
إنَّ الاجتهاد الحنبلي سوّغ تقييد الملك المطلق عند مباشرة عقده بشروط تحفظية تمنع بعض تصرفات المالك، أو تقيد طرق الانتفاع، أو تستثني بعض الحقوق، أو توجب على المالك بعض الواجبات .
- فجوز لبائع الأمة الرقيقة أن يشترط على المشتري عدم بيعها، بل يختصها بالتسري والاستيلاد، لأنها قد تكون عزيزة على البائع فيريد أن يحفظ لها الصيانة والكرامة .
- وكذلك جوز اشتراط البائع على المشتري أنه إذا أراد بيع المبيع فالبائع أحق باسترداده بثمنه فليس للمشتري عندئذ أن يبيعه على غير بائعه.
- وجوّز أيضاً بيع الشيء مع شرط احتفاظ البائع بمنفعته مدة معينة.
- وجوز أيضاً بيع العقار على شرط أن يقفه المشتري، وبيع العبد على شرط أن يعتقه، ونحو ذلك.
وهذه الشروط وأمثالها لا تقبلها الاجتهادات الأخرى غير المذهب الحنبلي، لأنها عندهم تعارض ما يقتضيه عقد البيع، وما يستلزمه الملك المطلق من حرية تصرف المشتري فيما اشتراه فإنَّ ثمرات الملك وحقوقه لا يرتبها العاقد ؛ وإنما يرتبها الشرع ترتيباً يمنع تجاوز الإنسان فيها على حقوق غيره.
ولكن نظر الاجتهاد الحنبلي في ذلك أن هذه الشروط الإرادية لها تأثير في تحديد آثار العقد بسلطة منحها الشرع العاقدين، وفوضهما بمقتضاها هذا التحديد بحسب مصالحهما. فلا يكون الملك المنتقل بعقد البيع مع هذه الشروط ملكاً مطلقاً بل مقيداً.

١٦/٤٢ - الناحية الرابعة :
الفصل (٤٢) الاجتهادات في سلطان الإرادة العقدية
إنَّ الاجتهاد الحنبلي صحح طريقة البيع بما ينقطع عليه السعر، أي بما يستقر عليه سعر السوق في يوم البيع، دون تحديد للثمن عند العقد. وهذا لم يقبله أحد من الأئمة الثلاثة غير أحمد، لما فيه من جهالة الثمن عند العقد (۱)
أساساً
ولكن الاجتهاد الحنبلي اعتبر في هذا الاتفاق والشرط ما يضع صالحاً لتحديد الثمن ونفي الجهالة وحسم النزاع. وقد دافع عنه ابن القيم وأيد نظريته من الوجهة الشرعية والمصلحة الواقعية بما فيه الكفاية (ر ) إعلام الموقعين طبعة المطبعة المنيرية بمصر،

وهذا غاية ما وصل إليه المنطق القانوني الحديث في شرائط التعاقد وما جاءتنا به المادة / ٩٦/ من القانون المدني الجديد لدينا من أنه : إذا اتفق العاقدان على النواحي الأساسية في العقد وأرجىء البحث في التفاصيل
(۱) إن متأخري الحنفية جوزوا من هذا القبيل النوع الذي أسموه: بيع الاستجرار، للحاجة إليه، واختلفوا في طريقة تخريجه (ر ) رد المحتار ١٢/٤). وبيع الاستجرار: هو أن يأخذ الإنسان حاجاته اليومية من عند اللحام أو البدال أو غيرهما دون اتفاق على ثمن كل منها عند الأخذ، ثم يحاسبه كل مدة. وهو المسمى اليوم بطريقة الحساب الجاري.
(۲) ويقول شيخ الإسلام ابن تيمية رحمه الله في كتابه «قاعدة العقود : وقد نص أحمد على أنه يجوز أن يأخذ بالسعر، فيجوز الشراء بالعوض المعروف والاستئجار بالعوض المعروف وكذلك التزوج بالعوض المعروف بل عوض المثل في البيع والإجارة أولى بالقبول، فإنه يوجد مثل المبيع والمأجور كثيراً، ويعرف عوضه بكثرة العرف؛ بخلاف المرأة فإن ما يشابهها في صفاتها المقصودة من كل وجه متعذره .
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh

15/4 - Third Aspect:
Indeed, Hanbali ijtihad permitted restricting absolute ownership at the time of entering the contract with protective conditions that prevent some of the owner's dispositions, or restrict methods of benefit, or exempt certain rights, or impose certain obligations on the owner.
- So it allowed the seller of a young slave-girl to stipulate on the buyer that he not sell her, but rather confine her to concubinage and bearing children, because she might be dear to the seller and he wants to preserve her modesty and dignity.
- Likewise, it allowed the seller to stipulate on the buyer that if he wants to sell the object of sale, the seller is more entitled to take it back at its price; then the buyer cannot sell it to someone other than his seller.
- It also allowed selling an item with the condition that the seller retain its usufruct for a certain period.
- It also allowed selling real estate on condition that the buyer endow it, and selling a slave on condition that he free him, and the like.
These conditions and their like are not accepted by other ijtihads besides the Hanbali school, because according to them they contradict what the contract of sale requires and what absolute ownership entails of the buyer's freedom to dispose of what he bought, for the fruits of ownership and its rights are not created by the contracting party; rather, the Law creates them in a manner that prevents a person from trespassing on the rights of others.
But the Hanbali ijtihad considered in this that these voluntary conditions have an effect in determining the effects of the contract by a power granted by the Law to the contracting parties, and it delegated to them accordingly this determination according to their interests. Thus the ownership transferred by the contract of sale with these conditions is not absolute but restricted.

16/42 - Fourth Aspect:
Chapter (42): Ijtihads on the Authority of Contractual Will
Indeed, Hanbali ijtihad validated the method of selling at what the price settles upon, i.e., at what the market price on the day of sale stabilizes, without specifying the price at the time of the contract. This was not accepted by any of the three imams besides Ahmad, due to the ignorance of the price at the time of contract (1) basically.
But Hanbali ijtihad considered in this agreement and condition what places a valid means for determining the price, negating ignorance, and settling disputes. Ibn Qayyim defended it and supported his theory from both the legal perspective and practical interest sufficiently (see I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in, Al-Muniriyya Press, Egypt, 3/402).
This is the furthest reached by modern legal logic in terms of contract conditions, and what Article 96 of our new Civil Code has brought us: if the parties agree on the essential aspects of the contract and defer the details...
(1) The later Hanafis permitted of this type what they called: bay' al-istijrar, due to need, and they differed on the method of its derivation (see Radd al-Muhtar 4/12). Bay' al-istijrar is: a person takes his daily needs from the butcher, grocer, or others without agreement on the price of each at the time of taking, then settles accounts every period. It is what today is called the current account method.
(2) Shaykh al-Islam Ibn Taymiyya, may Allah have mercy on him, says in his book Qa'idat al-'Uqud: Ahmad stipulated that it is permissible to buy at the price, so purchase at the known consideration and hire at the known consideration are permissible, likewise marriage at the known consideration; rather, the fair price in sale and hire is more worthy of acceptance, for the like of the sold item and hired item exist frequently, and its consideration is known by the prevalence of custom; unlike the woman, for what resembles her in all intended qualities is rare.
Source pages p. 532

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct areas where Hanbali ijtihad diverges from the other three major schools (Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, is comparing legal philosophies. The first aspect (الناحية الثالثة) deals with restrictive conditions in contracts of sale. The Hanbali school permits the seller to impose conditions that limit the buyer's absolute ownership, such as prohibiting resale, requiring the seller's right of preemption, retaining usufruct, or compelling an act of piety like endowment or manumission. The other schools reject such conditions because ownership rights are determined by Shari'ah, not by the parties; the buyer must have free disposition. The Hanbali justification is that Shari'ah grants parties the authority to shape contractual effects according to their interests, making ownership not absolute but qualified. The second aspect (الناحية الرابعة) concerns selling at the prevailing market price without specifying a fixed price at the time of contract. This is invalid in the other schools due to uncertainty (gharar) in the price. Hanbalis, however, consider the market price a sufficient determinant, avoiding dispute. The author notes that later Hanafis allowed a similar practice called bay' al-istijrar (running account) due to need, and that modern civil law has adopted a comparable principle by allowing agreements on essentials while deferring details. Ibn Taymiyya and Ibn Qayyim defended this view. The passage illustrates the Hanbali emphasis on party autonomy (سلطان الإرادة العقدية) and practical convenience, contrasting with the stricter formalism of other schools.

Key Terms
سلطان الإرادة العقدية (sultan al-irada al-'aqdiyya): The power of the contracting parties' will to shape the effects of a contract, including imposing conditions that restrict ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do the other three schools (excluding Hanbali) reject restrictive conditions like the seller stipulating that the buyer cannot resell the item?
A) Because such conditions are not explicitly mentioned in the Quran or Sunnah.
B) Because ownership rights are determined by Shari'ah and cannot be altered by the contracting parties.
C) Because they consider such conditions a form of riba.
D) Because they prioritize the buyer's absolute freedom of disposition.

Key Takeaway
The Hanbali school permits restrictive conditions in sale contracts based on party autonomy, while the Hanafi school voids them because ownership rights are fixed by Shari'ah.

Hanbali Validation of Open-Price Sales and Bay' al-Istijrar
Explains Hanbali validation of open-price sales and later Hanafi accommodation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanbali validation of selling at market price without specifying a price, and identify how later Hanafis allowed bay' al-istijrar by necessity.

Core Idea
Hanbali fiqh permits sale at market price without specifying the price at contract time; later Hanafis allowed similar practice via bay' al-istijrar.

This lesson explores another area where Hanbali ijtihad diverges from the other schools: the validity of a sale where the price is not fixed at the time of contract but is determined by the market price on the day of sale. The other schools reject this due to gharar (uncertainty), but Hanbalis allow it as a practical necessity. We also see how later Hanafis adapted by permitting bay' al-istijrar (running account sales) to meet commercial needs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanbali position
State that Hanbali ijtihad validates a sale where the price is based on the prevailing market price on the day of sale, without fixing a price at the contract time.
This is known as 'البيع بما ينقطع عليه السعر'.
2
Understand the objection from other schools
Explain that the other three imams (Abu Hanifa, Malik, Shafi'i) reject this due to ignorance of the price (jahalah) at the time of contract, which leads to gharar.
The price is an essential element; uncertainty invalidates the sale.
3
Analyze the Hanbali justification
The Hanbali school considers that the market price provides a sufficient determination, negating ignorance and settling disputes. Ibn Qayyim defended this view.
The condition of price is fulfilled by reference to the market rate.
4
Recognize the later Hanafi accommodation
Later Hanafis allowed a similar practice called bay' al-istijrar (recurrent sale without immediate price fixing) due to widespread need, analogizing to current account practices.
This shows fiqh's ability to adapt to necessity.
5
Connect to modern law
Note that modern civil codes (e.g., Article 96 of the new Civil Code) allow agreements on essential aspects while deferring details, reflecting the Hanbali approach.
The passage highlights the convergence of classical fiqh and modern legislation.

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه

١٥/٤ - الناحية الثالثة :
إنَّ الاجتهاد الحنبلي سوّغ تقييد الملك المطلق عند مباشرة عقده بشروط تحفظية تمنع بعض تصرفات المالك، أو تقيد طرق الانتفاع، أو تستثني بعض الحقوق، أو توجب على المالك بعض الواجبات .
- فجوز لبائع الأمة الرقيقة أن يشترط على المشتري عدم بيعها، بل يختصها بالتسري والاستيلاد، لأنها قد تكون عزيزة على البائع فيريد أن يحفظ لها الصيانة والكرامة .
- وكذلك جوز اشتراط البائع على المشتري أنه إذا أراد بيع المبيع فالبائع أحق باسترداده بثمنه فليس للمشتري عندئذ أن يبيعه على غير بائعه.
- وجوّز أيضاً بيع الشيء مع شرط احتفاظ البائع بمنفعته مدة معينة.
- وجوز أيضاً بيع العقار على شرط أن يقفه المشتري، وبيع العبد على شرط أن يعتقه، ونحو ذلك.
وهذه الشروط وأمثالها لا تقبلها الاجتهادات الأخرى غير المذهب الحنبلي، لأنها عندهم تعارض ما يقتضيه عقد البيع، وما يستلزمه الملك المطلق من حرية تصرف المشتري فيما اشتراه فإنَّ ثمرات الملك وحقوقه لا يرتبها العاقد ؛ وإنما يرتبها الشرع ترتيباً يمنع تجاوز الإنسان فيها على حقوق غيره.
ولكن نظر الاجتهاد الحنبلي في ذلك أن هذه الشروط الإرادية لها تأثير في تحديد آثار العقد بسلطة منحها الشرع العاقدين، وفوضهما بمقتضاها هذا التحديد بحسب مصالحهما. فلا يكون الملك المنتقل بعقد البيع مع هذه الشروط ملكاً مطلقاً بل مقيداً.

١٦/٤٢ - الناحية الرابعة :

إنَّ الاجتهاد الحنبلي صحح طريقة البيع بما ينقطع عليه السعر، أي بما يستقر عليه سعر السوق في يوم البيع، دون تحديد للثمن عند العقد. وهذا لم يقبله أحد من الأئمة الثلاثة غير أحمد، لما فيه من جهالة الثمن عند العقد (۱)
أساساً
ولكن الاجتهاد الحنبلي اعتبر في هذا الاتفاق والشرط ما يضع صالحاً لتحديد الثمن ونفي الجهالة وحسم النزاع. وقد دافع عنه ابن القيم وأيد نظريته من الوجهة الشرعية والمصلحة الواقعية بما فيه الكفاية (ر ) إعلام الموقعين طبعة المطبعة المنيرية بمصر،

Suspending Contracts on Conditions in Hanbali Fiqh
Explains the Hanbali permissibility of suspending contracts on conditions and the basis in hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanbali position on ta'liq al-'aqd and compare it with the Hanafi position, applying the relevant maxim in fatwa construction.

Core Idea
Hanbalis permit suspension in all contracts; Hanafis restrict it to suitable obligations.

This passage explores a significant madhhab divergence: the permissibility of suspending a contract on a future condition (ta'liq). While the Hanbali school, citing the general hadith 'Muslims are bound by their conditions', allows suspension in almost all contracts including marriage, the majority of jurists and the Hanafis take a more restrictive view. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to apply legal maxims and condition-based reasoning in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling per Hanbali school
Read the passage to determine what type of act is being discussed (ta'liq of contracts) and what the Hanbali ruling is (permissible in all contracts).
2
Contrast with Hanafi and majority positions
Identify the Hanafi position (permitted only in certain obligations with suitable conditions) and the majority position (generally void except in annulments).
Note the school's logic: transfer contracts require immediate effect.
3
Analyze the Hanbali dalil
Examine the hadith 'Muslims are bound by their conditions' – its chain, generality, and how it supports the Hanbali view.
The hadith is general, but Hanafis restrict it based on the nature of contracts.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
See how the Hanafis counter the hadith by distinguishing contract types and requiring suitability of conditions.
They argue that conditions contradicting the essence of a contract are invalid.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Apply the difference to a concrete case, e.g., conditional marriage, and state the ruling in each school.
This shows the real-world impact of the ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٧/٤٢ - الناحية الخامسة :
إن الاجتهاد الحنبلي أجاز تعليق التصرف بشرط معلق في جميع أنواع العقود والفسوخ من بيع وإجارة وكفالة وإقالة وإبراء وغيرها حتى عقد النكاح أيضاً ؛ وذلك على اختلاف في الآراء المذهبية في هذا التعميم. وتعليق العقد هو إنشاؤه بصيغة تفيد ارتباط وجوده بأمر آخر معلق عليه وهو المسمى بالشرط الجعلي كما تقدم (ر: ف ٦/٢٨). وذلك كقولك : إن جاءت بضاعتي الفلانية اليوم فقد بعتكها بكذا، وكقول المرأة: زوجتك نفسي على كذا من المهر إن رضي أبي أو أخي مثلاً.
وجمهور الفقهاء قد أغلقوا باب التعليق في جميع العقود ولا سيما التمليكات ،والنكاح فاعتبروها بالتعليق باطلة وإنما سوّغوا تعليق الإسقاطات المحضة كالطلاق والإعتاق وسوّغ الحنفية تعليق عقود الالتزامات والإطلاقات كالكفالة والوكالة بالشرط الملائم دون غير الملائم كما سنرى (ر : ف (١٤/٤٣ ) .
ومستند الإمام أحمد في تجويز تعليق العقود بوجه عام هو إطلاق قول النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام المسلمون على شروطهم (۲) فإنه لم
(۲) أخرجه أبو داود (٣٥٩٤) ، والدارقطني (۳۰۰)، والحاكم (٤٩/٢)، والبيهقي (٧٩/٦) من حديث أبي هريرة وروي من حديث عائشة وأنس بن مالك، وعمرو بن عوف، ورافع بن خديج وعبدالله بن عمر والحديث بمجموع طرقه يرتقي إلى الصحيح لغيره.
17/42 - The fifth aspect: The Hanbali ijtihad permitted suspending an act on a suspended condition in all types of contracts and dissolutions, such as sale, lease, guarantee, rescission, release, and others, including the marriage contract; with differences in madhhab opinions on this generalization. Suspending a contract is its creation in a formula that indicates linking its existence to another matter on which it is suspended, called a contractual condition as previously (see F 6/28). For example, your saying: 'If my such-and-such goods arrive today, I have sold them to you for such-and-such,' and a woman saying: 'I have married you to myself for such-and-such dower if my father or brother agrees.' The majority of jurists have closed the door of suspension in all contracts, especially transfers of ownership and marriage, considering them void by suspension; they only permitted suspension of pure annulments like divorce and manumission. The Hanafis permitted suspension of contracts of obligations and releases like guarantee and agency with a suitable condition, not an unsuitable one, as we will see (see F 14/43). The basis of Imam Ahmad in permitting suspension of contracts in general is the generality of the Prophet's saying: 'Muslims are bound by their conditions,' for it is not...
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage examines the Hanbali position on suspending contracts (ta'liq) on future conditions - a topic of significant disagreement among madhhabs. The Hanbali school, based on the general hadith 'Muslims are bound by their conditions,' permits suspension in virtually all contracts including sales, leases, guarantees, and even marriage. The majority of jurists (including Shafi'is, Malikis, and originally Hanafis) generally reject suspending transfer-of-ownership contracts, considering them void if conditioned on a future event; they only allow suspension of pure annulments like divorce and freeing slaves. The Hanafis take a middle position: they allow suspension in contracts of obligation (like guarantee and agency) but only if the condition is suitable and relevant. The author contrasts the Hanbali liberal approach with the more restrictive views, highlighting the basis in the prophetic hadith that is general in its wording.

Key Terms
تعليق العقد (ta'liq al-'aqd): Creating a contract in a form that links its existence to a future condition (shart ja'li)
الشرط الجعلي (al-shart al-ja'li): A condition explicitly stipulated by the parties that suspends the contract's effect

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Hanafi
B) Maliki
C) Hanbali
D) Majority of jurists

Key Takeaway
The Hanbali school permits ta'liq in all contracts based on the hadith's generality; the Hanafis restrict it to obligations with suitable conditions due to the nature of transfer contracts.
