The Hanbali Position on Suspending Contracts and the Hanafi Response
Comparison of Hanbali and Hanafi views on suspending contracts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanbali ruling on suspending contracts, identify the dalil, and explain why the Hanafi school restricts it to specific contracts.

Core Idea
Imam Ahmad allows suspending all contracts using the hadith 'Muslims are bound by their conditions'; Hanafis restrict it to commitments.

This lesson explores a key difference between the Hanbali and Hanafi schools regarding contracts that are attached to a future condition (ta'liq). While the majority of jurists invalidate such contracts in property transfers and marriage, the Hanbali school, based on a broad application of the hadith, permits them universally. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to evaluate dalil and apply tarjih in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanbali position
State what Imam Ahmad and Ibn al-Qayyim allow regarding suspending contracts.
2
Identify the majority position
Note which schools restrict ta'liq and to which contracts (Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i).
Hanafis allow ta'liq only in kafala and wakala with a suitable condition.
3
Examine the Hanbali dalil
Analyze the hadith 'المسلمون على شروطهم' and its interpretation by Hanbalis.
They apply it generally to all conditions not violating Shariah.
4
Examine the Hanafi dalil
Understand why Hanafis restrict ta'liq: qiyas on marriage, tamlikat require immediacy.
They consider the hadith applicable only to non-suspensive conditions.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical outcome in fatwa for a hypothetical suspended contract.
In Hanafi, the contract is void; in Hanbali, it is valid.

١٧/٤٢ - الناحية الخامسة : إن الاجتهاد الحنبلي أجاز تعليق التصرف بشرط معلق في جميع أنواع العقود والفسوخ من بيع وإجارة وكفالة وإقالة وإبراء وغيرها حتى عقد النكاح أيضاً ؛ وذلك على اختلاف في الآراء المذهبية في هذا التعميم. وتعليق العقد هو إنشاؤه بصيغة تفيد ارتباط وجوده بأمر آخر معلق عليه وهو المسمى بالشرط الجعلي كما تقدم (ر: ف ٦/٢٨). وذلك كقولك : إن جاءت بضاعتي الفلانية اليوم فقد بعتكها بكذا، وكقول المرأة: زوجتك نفسي على كذا من المهر إن رضي أبي أو أخي مثلاً.

وجمهور الفقهاء قد أغلقوا باب التعليق في جميع العقود ولا سيما التمليكات ،والنكاح فاعتبروها بالتعليق باطلة وإنما سوّغوا تعليق الإسقاطات المحضة كالطلاق والإعتاق وسوّغ الحنفية تعليق عقود الالتزامات والإطلاقات كالكفالة والوكالة بالشرط الملائم دون غير الملائم كما سنرى (ر : ف (١٤/٤٣ ) .

ومستند الإمام أحمد في تجويز تعليق العقود بوجه عام هو إطلاق قول النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام المسلمون على شروطهم (۲) فإنه لم يستثن منه إلا الشرط الذي ينافي كتاب الله تعالى، وهو الشرط الذي يحل حراماً أو يحرم حلالاً؛ فيستوي فيه الشروط التعليقية والشروط التقييدية(۱)

ويقول ابن القيم في تعليل ذلك :
«إن تعليق العقود والفسوخ والتبرعات والالتزامات وغيرها بالشروط أمر قد تدعو إليه الضرورة أو الحاجة أو المصلحة، فلا يستغني عنه المكلف. وقد نص الإمام أحمد على جواز تعليق النكاح بالشرط كما يتعلق الطلاق وعلى جواز تعليق البيع والإبراء إعلام الموقعين، طبعة المنيرية ۲۳۷/۳ - ۲۳۸ / باختصار) .

وصحة تعليق العقود بالشروط هو ما يقتضيه اليوم مبدأ سلطان الإرادة في القوانين الحديثة ومقتضى المادتين / ٢٦٥ - ٢٦٦/ من قانوننا المدني.
17/42 - The fifth aspect: The Hanbali ijtihad allowed suspending an action by a conditional condition in all types of contracts and cancellations, such as sale, lease, guarantee, rescission, release, and others, even the marriage contract; however, there is disagreement among the schools regarding this generalization. Suspending a contract is its creation in a form that indicates the linking of its existence to another matter upon which it is conditional, which is called the stipulative condition as previously mentioned (see: F 6/28). For example, saying: 'If my specific goods arrive today, I have sold them to you for such amount,' or a woman saying: 'I have married myself to you for such mahr if my father or brother consents,' etc. The majority of jurists have closed the door of suspension in all contracts, especially property transfers and marriage, considering them void if suspended; they only allowed suspension of pure relinquishments like divorce and manumission. The Hanafis allowed suspension of contracts of commitment and agency, like guarantee and agency, with a suitable condition but not an unsuitable one, as we will see (see: F 14/43). The basis of Imam Ahmad in generally permitting the suspension of contracts is the general application of the Prophet's statement, peace be upon him: 'Muslims are bound by their conditions' (2), from which nothing is excluded except a condition that contradicts the Book of Allah, i.e., a condition that makes lawful what is unlawful or makes unlawful what is lawful; thus, suspensive and restrictive conditions are equal in this regard. Ibn al-Qayyim says in its explanation: 'Suspending contracts, cancellations, donations, obligations, and others by conditions is something that necessity, need, or interest may require, and the accountable person cannot do without it. Imam Ahmad explicitly allowed suspending marriage by a condition just as divorce is suspended, and allowed suspending sale and release.' The validity of suspending contracts by conditions is what is required today by the principle of party autonomy in modern laws and by Articles 265-266 of our civil code.
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanbali position on suspending contracts (ta'liq al-'uqud) by attaching a condition precedent. The Hanbali school, represented by Imam Ahmad, allows suspending all types of contracts—including sales, leases, guarantees, rescissions, releases, and even marriage—based on the general application of the hadith 'Muslims are bound by their conditions.' The only exception is a condition that contradicts Islamic law. This is a broad interpretation compared to the majority of schools, including the Hanafi, Maliki, and Shafi'i, which generally restrict suspension to non-commutative acts like divorce and manumission. However, the Hanafi school does allow suspension in contracts of commitment (e.g., kafala and wakala) provided the condition is suitable (mulaim). The passage also quotes Ibn al-Qayyim's rational justification: necessity, need, and public interest. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, notes that this Hanbali view aligns with modern legal principles of party autonomy as reflected in the Syrian civil code.

Key Terms
تعليق العقد (ta'līq al-ʿaqd): Creating a contract using a formula that links its existence to a future uncertain event (condition precedent).
الشرط الجعلي (al-sharṭ al-jaʿlī): A stipulative condition that is inserted into a contract by mutual agreement, distinct from a legal condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Qiyas on divorce
C) Istihsan based on custom
D) Ijma of the Companions

Key Takeaway
The Hanbali school allows suspending all contracts based on the hadith 'المسلمون على شروطهم', while the Hanafi school restricts ta'liq to contracts of commitment, considering tamlikat void if suspended.

The Hanbali Position on 'Urbun: An Iftaa Analysis
Presents the Hanbali ijtihad permitting 'urbun (earnest money) in sales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanbali position on 'urbun, apply the legal maxim contrasting majority and minority views, and evaluate the strength of the Hanbali dalil for fatwa purposes.

Core Idea
The Hanbali school permits earnest money ('urbun) based on a companion practice, whereas the majority forbids it as unjust enrichment.

In this passage, al-Zarqa presents the Hanbali ijtihad permitting 'urbun as a sixth aspect of contractual freedom. The majority of schools reject it, but the Hanbali rely on a precedent from 'Umar's approval. This lesson analyzes the dispute and examines how a fatwa might balance authority and convenience.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanbali position on 'urbun
Identify the ruling: the Hanbali school permits 'urbun. Explain the mechanism: the buyer pays a deposit; if he completes the sale, it counts toward the price; if he withdraws, the seller keeps it.
This is a form of conditional sale with earnest money.
2
Identify the majority position and its rationale
The majority (Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i) consider 'urbun invalid because the seller receives money without providing any consideration in the event of the buyer's default. This is seen as akin to gambling (maysir) or unjust enrichment.
The majority relies on the principle that property cannot be acquired without valid consideration.
3
Examine the Hanbali dalil
The Hanbali proof is a companion precedent: Nafi', 'Umar's governor, purchased a prison house from Safwan ibn Umayyah with a condition that if 'Umar was not satisfied, Safwan would keep a specified sum. 'Umar approved this condition, establishing permissibility.
This is an example of qawl sahabi (companion's practice) as a source of law, which the Hanbali school accepts broadly.
4
Analyze the legal reasoning
The Hanbali school considers that 'Umar's approval indicates the validity of such conditions. The majority may counter that this was a specific transaction and does not establish a general rule, or that it conflicts with stronger principles.
The dispute centers on the weight given to a companion's practice when it appears to contradict general prohibitions.
5
Determine the fatwa relevance
In modern fatwa practice, a mufti must consider the strength of evidence. The author presents the ikhtilaf without tarjih, so the mufti must weigh the Hanbali dalil against the majority view, considering the needs of contemporary contracts.
Some later Hanbali authorities may adopt 'urbun, while Hanafi muftis still reject it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤٢ / ١٨ - الناحية السادسة :
إنَّ الاجتهاد الحنبلي أجاز طريقة العربون، وهي أن يبيع الإنسان شيئاً ويأخذ من المشتري مبلغاً يسمى «عربوناً» لتوثيق الارتباط المال من بينهما، على أساس أن المشتري إذا قام بتنفيذ عقده احتسب العربون من الثمن، وإن نكل كان العربون للبائع.

وجمهرة المذاهب على أن ذلك غير جائز، لأنَّ فيه شرط مال يستحقه البائع بلا عوض، وعليه الاجتهاد الحنفي والمالكي والشافعي. ولكن أحمد ومحمد بن سيرين ونافع بن الحارث أجازوه تمسكاً بما روى نافع وهو عامل لعمر بن الخطاب على مكة : أنه اشترى لعمر دار السجن بمكة من صفوان بن أمية وشَرَط له أنه إن لم يرضها عمر فلصفوان مبلغ معين من المال. وقد أقر عمر شرطه هذا (ر: الشرح الكبير، باب الشروط في البيع ٥٨/٤ - ٥٩ ، وبداية المجتهد ١٣٥/٢).
42/18 - The sixth aspect: The Hanbali ijtihad permitted the method of 'urbun (earnest money). It is that a person sells something and takes from the buyer an amount called 'urbun to strengthen the contractual bond between them, on the basis that if the buyer executes his contract, the 'urbun is counted as part of the price; if he defaults, the 'urbun goes to the seller. The majority of schools consider this impermissible, because it involves a condition for money that the seller receives without compensation, and upon this is the Hanafi, Maliki, and Shafi'i ijtihad. However, Ahmad, Muhammad ibn Sirin, and Nafi' ibn al-Harith permitted it, adhering to what Nafi' (who was 'Umar ibn al-Khattab's governor over Mecca) reported: that he bought for 'Umar the prison house in Mecca from Safwan ibn Umayyah, and stipulated that if 'Umar was not satisfied, Safwan would receive a specified amount of money. And 'Umar approved this condition (see: al-Sharh al-Kabir, chapter on conditions in sale, vol. 4 pp. 58-59, and Bidayat al-Mujtahid, vol. 2 p. 135).
Source pages p. 535

Commentary
This passage discusses the sixth aspect of party autonomy in contracts: the Hanbali position on 'urbun (earnest money or down payment). In an 'urbun arrangement, the seller takes a deposit from the buyer; if the buyer completes the purchase, the deposit counts toward the price; if the buyer withdraws, the seller keeps the deposit. The majority of schools—Hanafi, Maliki, and Shafi'i—rule this invalid because it involves the seller receiving money without giving any consideration in return if the buyer backs out. This is considered a form of gambling or unjust enrichment. However, the Hanbali school, following Imam Ahmad, along with Ibn Sirin and Nafi' ibn al-Harith, permit it based on a precedent from the practice of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab. Nafi', 'Umar's governor, purchased a house for 'Umar from Safwan ibn Umayyah with a condition that if 'Umar was not satisfied, Safwan would keep a specified sum. 'Umar approved this condition, establishing a precedent. The Hanbali school thus views 'urbun as a valid contract term that serves to secure the deal, as long as it does not involve riba or clear injustice.

Key Terms
العربون (al-ʿurbūn): An earnest payment given by the buyer to the seller as a down payment; if the buyer completes the sale, it is part of the price; if the buyer withdraws, it is forfeited to the seller.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It involves a condition that allows the seller to take money without giving anything in return if the buyer withdraws.
B) It was not practiced by the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم).
C) It leads to price manipulation.
D) It is a form of riba.

Key Takeaway
The Hanbali school permits 'urbun based on a companion precedent, while the majority forbids it; this ikhtilaf is preserved in fatwa practice.

Invalid Conditions: Fasid vs. Batil in Hanafi Fiqh
Categorization of two types of invalid contractual conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a contractual condition as fasid or batil according to Hanafi fiqh and determine whether the contract remains valid or becomes void.

Core Idea
A condition that contradicts the contract's purpose is void but ignored; a condition that negates its essence nullifies the entire contract.

In Islamic contract law, not all invalid conditions are equal. Some are fasid (irregular) and are simply dropped, leaving the contract intact. Others are batil (void) and nullify the contract itself. This lesson explores the Hanafi distinction, drawing on the broader classification found in comparative fiqh. Understanding this distinction is essential for a mufti advising on contractual disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Read the contract term and determine its nature. Ask: what is the party stipulating?
Focus on the content, not the wording.
2
Check against the contract's purpose (muqtada al-ʿaqd)
Compare the condition with the essential purpose of the contract type. For marriage, the purpose is halal intimacy. For sale, it is the transfer of ownership.
The essential purpose is derived from the contract's definition in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Evaluate the effect on the contract's goal (ghāya)
If the condition merely contradicts a non-essential aspect (e.g., stipulating no second wife), it is fasid (invalid but voidable). If it negates the contract's entire goal (e.g., stipulating no intercourse in marriage), it is batil and voids the contract.
The key is whether the condition makes the contract's fundamental outcome impossible.
4
Apply the Hanafi ruling
For a fasid condition: the contract is valid and the condition is dropped. For a batil condition: the contract is void (batil) and must be rescinded.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
5
Practice with examples
Work through scenarios: marriage with condition of no financial support (fasid), or sale with condition that buyer cannot resell (could be fasid or batil depending on context).
Always refer to the school's detailed rulings in al-Hidayah or al-Mabsut.

Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical discussion on the authority of will in contracts, specifically examining the Hanbali school's approach to stipulations. The author begins by categorizing conditions: (3) an invalid condition that is void because it contradicts the contract's purpose (e.g., the wife stipulating no intercourse), and (4) an invalid condition that nullifies the entire contract because it negates the contract's essential goal (e.g., stipulating that the wife never be permissible to the husband). He then notes that the Hanbali school has diverse ijtihad on these matters, with some rulings being well-known (mashhur) and others less so. For instance, the validity of stipulating an option in marriage is disputed among Hanbalis. The key point is that these diverse opinions are all rooted in the school's principles and are reliably transmitted from Imam Ahmad or his senior followers. The author then broadens the discussion to all schools, arguing that both rajih (preponderant) and marjuh (preponderated) opinions are valuable legislative assets. Over time, changing circumstances may elevate a marjuh opinion to rajih. He praises Ibn al-Qayyim, a prominent Hanbali jurist, for his insights. This passage teaches the mufti to respect the full spectrum of opinions within a madhhab and to recognize their potential future relevance, rather than dismissing them as weak.

Key Terms
شرط لا يصح ويلغو (shart la yasihh wa yalghu): An invalid condition that is void and does not affect the contract, but is simply ignored.
شرط لا يصح ويُلغي العقد (shart la yasihh wa yulghī al-ʿaqd): An invalid condition that nullifies the contract because it negates the contract's essential purpose.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage and Hanafi fiqh, what is the ruling on a condition in a marriage contract that the wife will never live with the husband?
A) The condition is fasid and the marriage is valid but the condition is dropped.
B) The condition is batil and the marriage is void.
C) The condition is valid and must be fulfilled.
D) The marriage is suspended pending arbitration.

Key Takeaway
Invalid conditions that contradict the contract's purpose are void but do not harm the contract; conditions that negate the contract's essence make the contract void.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Legislative Value of Marjuh Opinions: A Principle for Muftis
Reflection on the value of marjuh opinions with praise for Ibn al-Qayyim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the principle that marjuh (preponderated) opinions are valuable legislative resources and identify circumstances where a marjuh view may become mufta bihi in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A marjuh opinion is not worthless; it may become the mufta bihi when circumstances change and its proof becomes stronger.

In every madhhab, there are opinions that are well-known (mashhur) and others that are less known or considered weaker (marjuh). Students often dismiss marjuh views as irrelevant, but the passage reminds us that these opinions are part of the school's legal wealth. Changing times may elevate a marjuh view to prominence. This lesson explores this principle as it applies to the Hanafi school and the mufti's role.

Guided Path
1
Identify rajih and marjuh opinions
In the Hanafi school, the rajih opinion is the one that has stronger evidence (dalil) or is supported by the majority of later scholars. The marjuh is a non-dominant view that still has a basis in the school's usul.
Marjuh does not mean invalid; it means outweighed in the tarjih process.
2
Understand why marjuh may become rajih
Over time, social needs, economic conditions, or new evidence may tilt the balance. The passage states that what was considered weak may in fact be stronger in a different context.
The mufti must be alert to changing 'urf (custom) and public interest (maslahah).
3
Apply to a Hanafi example
For instance, the permissibility of using written evidence in court was initially marjuh in Hanafi fiqh (due to preference for oral testimony) but became rajih with changing commercial practices.
Many Hanafi fatwa collections contain such shifts.
4
Formulate a fatwa considering marjuh opinions
When a mufti faces a pressing issue with no clear rajih ruling, he may look to marjuh opinions that address the need, provided they have sound bases.
This is not arbitrary; it requires careful scholarly deliberation and grounding in usul.
5
Reflect on the passage's conclusion
The passage praises Ibn al-Qayyim for his ability to identify deeper insights that were ahead of his time. This teaches humility and openness in ijtihad.
Ibn al-Qayyim is not Hanafi, but his method is instructive.

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
إنَّ الآراء والنظريات الفقهية في المذاهب المعتبرة، سواء منها الراجح والمرجوح كلها ثروة تشريعية قيّمة يحتاج إليها. وقد يظهر تطور المصالح الزمنية وإعادة النظر أنَّ ما كان من الآراء الفقهية مرجوحاً هو الذي يجب أن يكون الراجح وما كان يظن ضعيف المبنى هو في الحقيقة أقوى وأسد ولكن مرمى نظر صاحبه كان أمام قافلته بمسافات لا تدركها أبصارهم ؛ فيبقى غير معتمد عليه حتى تصل العصور بالأجيال إلى مرمى ذلك النظر فإذا هو البصر الحديد، والفهم الرشيد!!
وفي كل مذهب أنظار فقهية شتى من هذا القبيل يمتاز فيها المذهب بما لم يدركه سواه من المذاهب.
٢١/٤٢ - والله در ابن القيم - وهو من أعلام فقهاء المذهب الحنبلي
Chapter (42): Ijtihadat on the Authority of Contractual Will. Or he may not marry upon her. And the fruit of its validity is the permissibility of annulment upon violation. 3 - A condition that is invalid and is void, and it is what contradicts the purpose of the contract, such as the wife stipulating that the husband not have intercourse with her. 4 - A condition that is invalid and nullifies - i.e., invalidates - the contract, the goal of the contract, such as her stipulation that she is not permissible for the husband through marriage. What is lacking in it. 20/42 - This, and we have only presented in these six aspects foundations and principles of the authority of will in the effects of contracts and contractual conditions present in Hanbali ijtihad and narrated from its Imam. And we draw attention to the fact that in some of these fundamental aspects there is disagreement among the jurists of the Hanbali school, and that what is agreed upon of foundations has in its secondary applications much disagreement concerning the limits and details of the suspended [condition]. For example, the running of the option of stipulation in the marriage contract, and the validity of its suspension upon a condition is contrary to the well-known [view] in the Hanbali school. Likewise, the suspension of financial commutative contracts and the method of sale upon what cuts off the price, as previously explained, the opinions of Hanbali jurists did not agree upon them: some ruled their permissibility and some ruled their invalidity. But the important thing is that they are juristic opinions that the principles and basic theories of the school have accommodated, and they are established in narration and transmission from the Imam of the school or its senior figures, even if some of them were not the well-known and preferred [view]. (1) 1 In what we have written in this research, reference is made to the following sources: From the old books in general fiqh and madhhab fiqh: (1) 1 - Bidayat al-Mujtahid by Qadi Ibn Rushd al-Hafid al-Maliki, Chapter on Sales of Conditions and Stipulations, vol. 2, p. 567. p. 137-132. = Sixth Section: Theory of Contracts and What Pertains to Them in Fiqh. Indeed, the juristic opinions and theories in the reliable schools, whether they are preponderant or preponderated, are all a valuable legislative wealth that is needed. And it may appear from the development of temporal interests and reconsideration that what was preponderated among the juristic opinions is what should be preponderant, and what was thought weak in structure is in reality stronger and more correct, but the aim of its author's view was ahead of his caravan by distances that their sights could not perceive; so it remains undependable until the ages with generations reach the aim of that view, and then it becomes the sharp sight and sound understanding!! And in every school there are various juristic perspectives of this kind, by which the school is distinguished by what no other school has grasped. 21/42 - And excellent is Ibn al-Qayyim - and he is among the eminent jurists of the Hanbali school.
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical discussion on the authority of will in contracts, specifically examining the Hanbali school's approach to stipulations. The author begins by categorizing conditions: (3) an invalid condition that is void because it contradicts the contract's purpose (e.g., the wife stipulating no intercourse), and (4) an invalid condition that nullifies the entire contract because it negates the contract's essential goal (e.g., stipulating that the wife never be permissible to the husband). He then notes that the Hanbali school has diverse ijtihad on these matters, with some rulings being well-known (mashhur) and others less so. For instance, the validity of stipulating an option in marriage is disputed among Hanbalis. The key point is that these diverse opinions are all rooted in the school's principles and are reliably transmitted from Imam Ahmad or his senior followers. The author then broadens the discussion to all schools, arguing that both rajih (preponderant) and marjuh (preponderated) opinions are valuable legislative assets. Over time, changing circumstances may elevate a marjuh opinion to rajih. He praises Ibn al-Qayyim, a prominent Hanbali jurist, for his insights. This passage teaches the mufti to respect the full spectrum of opinions within a madhhab and to recognize their potential future relevance, rather than dismissing them as weak.

Key Terms
الراجح والمرجوح (al-rajih wa al-marjuh): The preponderant (rajih) and the preponderated (marjuh) opinions within a madhhab; the former is the standard fatwa, the latter is considered weaker but still part of the school's heritage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what may happen to a marjuh (preponderated) opinion over time?
A) It becomes invalid and is discarded.
B) It remains marjuh forever.
C) It may become rajih (preponderant) due to the development of temporal interests.
D) It is always rejected in favor of the opposite school.

Key Takeaway
Marjuh opinions are not to be disregarded; they are a vital part of the Hanafi school's legal heritage and may become relied upon as circumstances change.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
10 min
Ibn Qayyim's Two Universal Maxims on Contractual Conditions
Two universal propositions governing conditions in contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Ibn Qayyim's two universal propositions on conditions and apply them to determine the validity of a contractual condition.

Core Idea
A condition that contradicts Sharia is void; a condition that does not is binding when stipulated.

In this passage, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa introduces Ibn Qayyim al-Jawziyyah's profound insight on conditions in contracts. Ibn Qayyim critiqued the inconsistency of some jurists who nullify conditions without scriptural basis and proposed two universal propositions that govern all conditions. These maxims are foundational for understanding the balance between contractual freedom and Sharia limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem Ibn Qayyim addresses
Note his critique that many jurists nullify conditions not nullified by the Lawgiver and corrupt contracts without valid reason. They lack a consistent rule.
This sets the context for the maxims.
2
State the first universal proposition
Any condition that contradicts Allah's ruling and His Book is absolutely void, regardless of its nature.
This is the negative rule: conditions conflicting with Sharia are invalid.
3
State the second universal proposition
Any condition that does not contradict Sharia and is permissible to grant or omit without stipulation becomes binding when the parties actually stipulate it.
This is the positive rule: non-conflicting conditions are enforceable.
4
Connect to the broader principle of contractual will
Ibn Taymiyyah's statement that the basis of contracts is consent complements these maxims. Conditions are validated by mutual agreement as long as they do not violate Sharia.
This integrates the two maxims into the theory of contracts.

الفصل (٤٢) الاجتهادات في سلطان الإرادة العقدية
- فإنَّ له في هذا المقام كلاماً نفيساً مخلداً يعد من مفاخر الأنظار الفقهية. فإنه، بعد أن استعرض في كتابه إعلام الموقعين بعض نصوص الشريعة وآثار السنة المروية في قاعدة الشروط والروايات الثابتة عن الإمام أحمد في تعليق العقود وتقييدها بالشروط، يقول ما نصه :
والمقصود أنَّ للشروط عند الشارع شأناً ليس عند كثير من الفقهاء. فإنَّ بعضهم يلغون شروطاً لم يلغها الشارع، ويفسدون بها العقد من غير مفسدة تقتضي فساده وهم متناقضون فيما يقبل التعليق بالشروط من العقود وما لا يقبله فليس لهم ضابط مطّرد منعكس يقوم عليه دليل. فالصواب الضابط الشرعي الذي دل عليه النص، وفيه قضيتان كليتان:
- إحداهما : ان كل شرط خالف حكم الله وناقض كتابه فهو باطل كائناً ما كان .
- والثانية : ان كل شرط لا يخالف حكمه ولا يناقض كتابه، وهو يجوز بذله وتركه بدون اشتراط، فهو لازم بالشرط.

الفقه
٥٦٩
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. Indeed, the juristic opinions and theories in the reliable schools, whether the preponderant or the preponderated, are all a valuable legislative wealth that is needed. The development of temporal interests and re-evaluation may reveal that what was considered preponderated among the juristic opinions is what should be preponderant, and what was thought to be weak in foundation is actually stronger and sounder, but the scope of its author's view had been ahead of his caravan by distances that their eyes could not perceive; so it remains unrelied upon until the ages with generations reach the scope of that view, then it becomes sharp sight and right understanding!! And in every school there are various juristic views of this kind by which the school excels over other schools. 21/42 - May Allah reward Ibn Qayyim - who is among the prominent jurists of the Hanbali school - ... [references omitted] ... Chapter (42): The Ijtihadat on the Authority of Contractual Will. Indeed, he has in this context a precious and immortal statement that is considered a source of pride for juristic views. For he, after reviewing in his book I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in some texts of Sharia and transmitted traditions concerning the rule of conditions and the established narrations from Imam Ahmad about suspending contracts and restricting them with conditions, says verbatim: And the point is that conditions with the Lawgiver have a status that they do not have with many jurists. For some of them nullify conditions that the Lawgiver has not nullified, and they corrupt the contract with them without any corruption necessitating its invalidity, and they are contradictory regarding which contracts accept suspension by conditions and which do not; they have no consistent and exhaustive rule supported by evidence. The correct thing is the legal rule indicated by the text, which contains two universal propositions: One: Every condition that contradicts Allah's ruling and opposes His Book is void, whatever it may be. Two: Every condition that does not contradict His ruling nor oppose His Book, and it is permissible to grant it and omit it without stipulation, it is binding by the stipulation. Nothing is excepted from these two propositions; the Book of Allah, the Sunnah of His Messenger, and the consensus of the Companions have indicated them, and we pay no attention to rebuttals with sectarian issues and opinionated statements, for they do not demolish a rule from the rules of Sharia. (I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in, al-Muniriyya edition 3/339-340 abridged). /22 - On the occasion of this statement of Ibn Qayyim, we recall that great constitutional statement of his teacher Shaykh al-Islam Ibn Taymiyyah, may Allah have mercy on him, which we previously cited in [542], where he said: And the basis in contracts is the consent of the contracting parties, and the result is what they have obligated upon themselves through contracting. This magnificent statement is what should rightly be considered the Islamic constitutional principle regarding the authority of contractual will.
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm presents two foundational maxims on conditions in contracts, quoted from Ibn Qayyim al-Jawziyyah, a leading Hanbali jurist. The author first sets the tone by emphasizing that all juristic opinions, even those considered weak (marjuh), are valuable legislative wealth, as changing circumstances may later bring them to the fore. He then introduces Ibn Qayyim's profound insight: conditions (shurut) have a higher status in Sharia than many jurists recognize. Some jurists nullify conditions that the Lawgiver never nullified, thereby corrupting contracts without valid reason. They lack a consistent and comprehensive rule (dabit muttarid mun'akis) for determining which contracts can be tied to conditions. The correct approach, Ibn Qayyim asserts, is the legal rule derived from the sources, consisting of two universal propositions (qadiyatan kulliyatan): (1) Any condition that contradicts Allah's ruling and His Book is absolutely void; (2) Any condition that does not contradict Sharia and is permissible to grant or omit without stipulation is binding when stipulated. These two maxims are exhaustive—nothing is excepted—based on the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus of the Companions. Sectarian disagreements (masa'il madhhabiyyah) do not overturn such foundational rules. Finally, the author ties this to Ibn Taymiyyah's constitutional principle that the basis of contracts is mutual consent (rida al-muta'aqidayn), affirming the doctrine of contractual will (sultan al-iradah al-'aqdiyyah) in Islamic jurisprudence.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A stipulation or condition attached to a contract by the parties.
سلطان الإرادة العقدية (sultan al-iradah al-'aqdiyyah): The principle that the parties' free will is the foundation and determinant of contractual obligations.
قضية كلية (qadiyah kulliyah): A universal proposition that applies to all relevant cases within its scope.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Qayyim's two universal propositions, what is the status of a condition that does not contradict the Sharia and that the parties may voluntarily grant or omit without stipulating it?
A) It is void because it was not originally part of the contract.
B) It is binding if it is stipulated, provided it does not contradict Sharia.
C) It is only binding if it is related to the price in a sale.
D) It is void because it introduces an extra obligation beyond the contract's essence.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Qayyim's two universal propositions provide a clear and consistent rule: conditions are void only if they contradict Sharia; otherwise, they are binding when stipulated.
